للحمل ہوگی اور یہ ایسے ہے جیسے یوں کہنا والله لا ضربن رجلا تو گویا نکرہ شرط مثبت میں ایجاب جزی کے لئے اور اس کی نقبض اور نفی کی جانب عموم اور سلب کلی کے لئے ہو جائیگا اور شرط منفی میں نکرہ عام اور مفید ہوگا سلب کلی کا اور اس کی جانب نقیض اور نفی میں خصوص اور ایجاب جزی کے لئے ہوگا تو خلاصہ یہ ہوا کہ پہلی صورت میں نکرہ تحت النفی آگیا لہذا عام ہو گا بخلاف تانی صورت کے کہ یہاں تحت النفی نہیں آتا لہذا عام نہیں ہوگا بلکہ خاص اور مثبت ایجاب جزی کا ہوگا۔
And the indefinite noun in the position of condition: when an indefinite occurs in the position of a condition, if the condition is positive (muthbat) – meaning the condition is affirmative, not negative – then it becomes general in the side of negation. That is, on the side of negation, this indefinite becomes general. For example, if someone says: 'If I strike a man, then my slave is free,' the word 'rajulan' (a man) is indefinite, occurring in a positive condition. So on the side of negation of the positive condition, it becomes general. Its meaning: 'I will not strike any man'; otherwise my slave is free. Because the oath here is for prevention (man'). Know that an oath is sometimes for prevention (i.e., I will not do this) and sometimes for incitement (haml) i.e., to encourage and desire. Since 'If I strike a man, my slave is free' is an oath of prevention, the meaning is 'I will not strike a man.' The condition of fulfilling the oath and avoiding sin requires that he does not strike any man. So it is for universal negation, hence it becomes general on the side of negation. The qualification that the condition be positive is because if the condition is negative, the indefinite in such a condition does not become general. For example, 'If I do not strike a man, my slave is free' – here the word 'rajulan' is not for generality, nor does its meaning become universal negation. Rather, it is for partial affirmation (ījāb juz'ī). So to fulfill the oath and avoid sin, striking just one man suffices. The summary is that this type of condition, like 'If I do X, my slave is free,' is in fact an oath to realize the opposite of the content of the condition. If the condition is positive, it is like saying 'By Allāh, I will not strike any man.' If the condition is negative, the oath is for incitement, and it is like saying 'By Allāh, I will certainly strike a man.' So the indefinite in a positive condition becomes for partial affirmation on its own side, and for universality and universal negation on its opposite side. In a negative condition, the indefinite becomes universal and conveys universal negation on its own side, and on its opposite side becomes particular and partial affirmation. The summary is that in the first case, the indefinite falls under negation, so it becomes general; unlike the second case where it does not fall under negation, so it does not become general but remains specific and for partial affirmation.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi usul rule regarding the scope of an indefinite noun (nakirah) when it appears in a conditional clause within an oath. The key point is that the rule depends on whether the condition itself is positive or negative. If the condition is positive, such as 'If I strike a man...,' the indefinite (a man) is understood to refer to 'any man' when considering the negation side – i.e., the oath prohibits striking any man. This is because the oath is for prevention (man'). Conversely, if the condition is negative, like 'If I do not strike a man...,' the indefinite does not become general; it refers to a specific man, and the oath becomes one of incitement (haml). The author provides examples with oath formulations to clarify the distinction. This rule is crucial in Hanafi fiqh for determining when a person has violated an oath (hanth) and what is required to avoid it. The passage also briefly notes that an oath can be for prevention or incitement, which affects the interpretation. The final summary reiterates the two possibilities: positive condition → indefinite becomes general under negation; negative condition → indefinite remains specific on its own side and becomes general only on the opposite side. This analysis is part of the broader usul topic of 'āmm and khāṣṣ.

Key Terms
نكرة (nakirah): An indefinite noun that does not specify a particular individual.
شرط مثبت (shart muthbat): A positive condition that affirms an action.
یمین للمنع (yamīn lil-man'): An oath that prohibits an action, i.e., 'I swear I will not do X.'
یمین للحمل (yamīn lil-ḥaml): An oath that incites or encourages an action, i.e., 'I swear I will do X.'
ایجاب جزئی (ījāb juz'ī): Partial affirmation, referring to a specific instance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears, 'By Allāh, if I strike a man, my slave is free,' what is the scope of the indefinite 'a man'?
A) It becomes general, meaning any man.
B) It remains specific, meaning a particular man.
C) It becomes negative.
D) It is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The scope of an indefinite noun in a conditional oath depends on the condition's polarity: positive → general under negation; negative → specific.

The Indefinite Noun with a General Attribute is General
Demonstrating that an indefinite noun qualified by a general attribute is general, with Quranic and rational evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an indefinite noun qualified by a general attribute becomes general, and identify the usul evidence (dalil and ta'lil) that supports this rule.

Core Idea
An indefinite noun qualified by a general attribute becomes general — the cause for a general ruling must itself be general.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi usul principle: that a nakirah mawṣūfah (indefinite qualified by a general attribute) is considered 'āmm (general). The author proves this using Quranic evidence and reasoning from ta'līl (causation) and the principle of nisbah ilā al-mushtaq. This principle affects how we interpret indefinite expressions in the revealed texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the Hanafi ruling: an indefinite noun qualified by a general attribute (e.g., 'abdun mu'minun) becomes general and includes all individuals that carry that attribute.
This is a rule of usul al-fiqh about the scope of indefinite expressions.
2
Analyze the linguistic example
Examine the example 'lā ajālisu ar-rajula 'āliman' (I do not sit except with a knowledgeable man). Here 'rajulan' is indefinite and qualified by 'āliman' (knowledgeable). According to the rule, it applies to every knowledgeable man, not just one.
The generality comes from the attribute, not from the indefinite itself.
3
Trace the Quranic dalil
Read the verse 'Wa-la-'abdun mu'minun khayrun min mushrik' (Q. 4:25). The indefinite 'abdun is qualified by 'mu'minun'. The Hanafi argue it is general because it is the cause (illah) for the general ruling in Q. 2:221 prohibiting marrying polytheists. A cause for a general ruling must be general.
This is the ta'līl argument: the ruling's illah must match the ruling's scope.
4
Evaluate the nisbah ilā al-mushtaq argument
Consider the principle that when a ruling is attributed to a derivative (e.g., 'ālim from 'ilm), the source of derivation is the cause. For example, 'ajālisu al-'ālima' implies sitting with anyone possessing knowledge, regardless of identity. This reinforces the generality of the indefinite qualified.
This principle shows that the attribute generates generality because it points to the underlying cause.
5
Contrast with alternative view
Briefly note that the Shafi'i school does not automatically treat such an indefinite as general without additional evidence. This highlights the distinct Hanafi methodology.
Understanding the ikhtilaf sharpens your grasp of the Hanafi principle.

وكذا النكرة الموصوفة اور اسی طرح نکرہ جو صفتہ عامہ کے ساتھ موصوف ہودہ بھی ہمارے نزدیک عام ہو جاتا ہے نحولا اجالس الارجلاً عالما تو اس مقام پر رجلاً نکرہ جو عالما کی صفت عامہ کے ساتھ موصوف ہے تو اس وقت یہ ہر رجل عالم کے ساتھ ہمنشینی اختیار کر سکتا ہے خواہ وہ ایک ہو یا کثیر التعداد جتنا کے بھاتھ چاہے مجالستہ کر سکتا ہے وہ سب اس عموم میں درج ہیں۔ كقوله تعالى ولعبد مؤمن خير من مشرك تو یہاں لفظ عبد نکرہ ہے جو ایمان کی صفت عامہ کے ساتھ موصوف ہے تو یہ عام ہو کر ہر عبد مؤمن کو شامل ہو جائے گا خواہ وہ امیر ہو یا غریب جمیل الصورۃ ہو یا قبیح الصورة اسی طرح قول معروف ہر بھلی اور خیر بات کو شامل ہے ۔ دانما یدل علی - وانما يدل على العموم یعنی یہ نکرہ موصوفہ ولعبد مؤمن اس لئے دال على العموم ہے کہ یہ مقام تعلیل میں پیش کیا گیا ہے لقولہ تعالیٰ ولا تنكحوا المشرکین یعنی اللہ تعالیٰ کا یہ قول کہ مشرکین کے ساتھ مسلم عورتوں کا نکاح نہ کر و حتی یو منوا حتی کہ وہ ایمان لے آئیں تو پھر ہو سکتا ہے اور یہ حکم عام ہے کیونکہ المشرکین جمع معرف باللام ہے جو کہ عموم میں نص ہے اور اس حکم عام کی تعلیل پیش کرتے ہوئے فرمایا العبد مؤمن خیر من مشرک تو اس حکم عام کی علیہ کا بھی عام ہونا ضروری ہے لہذا عبد مؤمن جو مقام علہ میں نکرہ موصوفہ پیش کیا گیا ہے یہ عام ہو گا ورنہ تو حکم عام کی اس کے ساتھ تعلیل صحیح نہیں ہوگی ۔ ولان النسبة الى المشتق الخ یہ نکرہ موصوفہ کے عام ہونیکی دوسری وجہ ہے کہ نسبت الحکم الى المشتق اس پر دال ہوتی ہے کہ ماخذ اشتقاق اس حکم کی علتہ ہے خواہ اس مشق کا مو صرف مذکور ہو یا مقدر اسی کے متعلق کہا کذا النبتة الى الموصوف بالمشتق یعنی اگر حکم کو موصوف بالمشتق کی طرف نسبت کیا جائے تو تب بھی ماخذ اشتقاق علیہ الحکم ہوگا مثلا جب کہا اجالس الآعالما تو یہاں عالماً کا موصوف مقدر ہے معناه الارجلا عالما فيهم اى النكرة الموصوفة
And similarly, the indefinite noun that is qualified by a general attribute (al-nakirah al-mawṣūfah bi-ṣifatin 'āmmah) also becomes general in our view. For example, 'lā ajālisu ar-rajula 'āliman' (I do not sit except with a knowledgeable man). Here 'rajulan' is indefinite, qualified by the general attribute 'āliman. At that time, it applies to every knowledgeable man, whether one or many; however many he may sit with, all are included in this generality. As in the saying of Allāh the Exalted: 'And a believing slave is better than a polytheist' (Qur'an 4:25). Here the word 'abdun is indefinite, qualified by the general attribute of faith (īmān), so it becomes general, including every believing slave, whether rich or poor, handsome or ugly. Similarly, 'qawlun ma'rūfun' (a kind word) includes every good and beneficial word. And indeed it indicates generality – this indefinite qualified (al-nakirah al-mawṣūfah) in 'wa-la-'abdun mu'minun' indicates generality because it is presented in the context of causation (ta'līl) for the saying of Allāh: 'Do not marry polytheists' (Qur'an 2:221). This command is general because 'al-mushrikīn' is a plural definite with 'al', which is explicit in generality. And in presenting the cause for this general command, He said: 'A believing slave is better than a polytheist.' So the cause (illah) for the general ruling must also be general. Hence, 'abdun mu'minun', presented in the position of cause as an indefinite qualified, is general. Otherwise, the causation of a general ruling by it would not be correct. And also because the attribution of the ruling to the derivative (nisbah al-ḥukm ilā al-mushtaq) indicates that the source of derivation (makhdh al-ishtiqāq) is the cause of the ruling, whether the subject of the derivative is mentioned or implied. Related to this is said: 'Thus the attribution is to the one described by the derivative' (kadhā al-nisbah ilā al-mawṣūf bi al-mushtaq). That is, when a ruling is attributed to something described by a derivative, then the source of derivation is the cause. For example, when it is said 'ajālisu al-'ālima' (I sit with the knowledgeable one), here the described of 'āliman' is implied, meaning 'ar-rajula al-‘ālima fīhim' (the knowledgeable man among them). That is, the indefinite qualified.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi usul ruling that an indefinite noun qualified by a general attribute (nakirah mawṣūfah bi-ṣifatin 'āmmah) is considered general ('āmm). The author provides two proofs. First, a linguistic example: 'lā ajālisu ar-rajula 'āliman' (I do not sit except with a knowledgeable man) – here 'rajulan' is indefinite, qualified by 'āliman' (knowledgeable), and it applies to all knowledgeable men, not just one. This shows the generality. The author then cites the Quranic verse (4:25): 'And a believing slave is better than a polytheist.' The word 'abdun (slave) is indefinite but qualified by 'mu'minun' (believing). According to the Hanafi view, this includes every believing slave, regardless of wealth or appearance. The second proof is the argument from ta'līl (causation). The verse (2:221) prohibits marrying polytheists in a general ruling. The cause (illah) for this prohibition is given as 'a believing slave is better than a polytheist.' For the cause to justify the general ruling, it must itself be general. Therefore, 'abdun mu'minun' must be general. The third proof is the principle of nisbah ilā al-mushtaq (attribution to the derivative). When a ruling is attributed to a word derived from a root (like 'ālim from 'ilm), the implication is that the root meaning (knowledge) is the cause of the ruling, regardless of the specific individual. Thus, 'ajālisu al-'ālima' implies I sit with anyone who is knowledgeable. This reinforces the generality of the indefinite qualified. This ruling is important in Hanafi usul because it affects how indefinite expressions in the Quran and Sunnah are interpreted – they may be understood as general when qualified by a general attribute.

Key Terms
النكرة الموصوفة (al-nakirah al-mawṣūfah): An indefinite noun that is qualified by an attribute (ṣifah). In this context, the attribute must be general ('āmmah).
صفتہ عامہ (ṣifat 'āmmah): A general attribute that applies to many instances, such as 'believing' or 'knowledgeable'.
تعلیل (ta'līl): The act of providing a cause (illah) for a ruling.
نسبة الى المشتق (nisbah ilā al-mushtaq): Attribution of a ruling to a derivative word, implying the root meaning is the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school consider 'abdun mu'minun' in Q. 4:25 to be general?
A) Because 'abdun is a general term for all slaves.
B) Because it is used in the context of ta'līl (causation) for a general ruling.
C) Because the attribute mu'minun is indefinite.
D) Because the verse uses a negative particle.

Key Takeaway
An indefinite noun qualified by a general attribute becomes general in Hanafi usul, supported by ta'līl and nisbah ilā al-mushtaq.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Three Contexts of Generality of the Indefinite Noun (Nakira)
Rule-based exposition on nakira generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three contexts in which a nakira indicates generality according to Hanafi usul and apply the rule to determine whether a given nakira is general or specific.

Core Idea
A nakira becomes general only under negation, positive condition, or general description; otherwise it indicates an individual.

In Hanafi usul, the default of an indefinite noun (nakira) is specificity—it indicates an individual or absolute essence. However, the scholars identified three exceptional contexts where the nakira overcomes its default and yields generality (umum). Understanding these contexts is crucial for correctly deriving rulings from Quran and hadith, especially when encountering commands, prohibitions, or conditions. This lesson explains each context with examples, building on the source passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three contexts
Read the source passage and list the three situations where the nakira becomes general: (1) under negation, (2) in a positive conditional, (3) when described by a general attribute.
The passage treats the first two briefly and elaborates on the third with Quranic evidence.
2
Analyze each context with examples
For each context, consider the example given: e.g., 'la rajula' (negation), 'in ja'aka rajulun' (positive conditional), 'la ajalisu rajulan 'aaliman' (described). Determine why the nakira becomes general.
For described nakira, the 'illa argument is key.
3
Distinguish from non-general contexts
Note that outside these three, the nakira is specific—it indicates an individual (in khabar) or absolute essence (in insha'). Apply this to new examples.
This prevents over-application of generality.
4
Apply the rule to a Quranic text
Using the verse 2:221, identify the nakira mawsufa and argue why it is general based on its position as 'illa.
This practice solidifies the method of istinbat.

ثابت ہو گا عند السامع اذا كانت في الاخبار یعنی جب وہ نکرہ خبر میں واقع ہو جیسے رایت رجلاً تو کوئی فرد مبہم من الرجال ثابت ہو گا عند السامع خلاصہ یہ کہ انشاء میں ماہیتہ مطلقہ اور ذات مرسلہ ثابت ہوگی اور اخبار میں فرد واحد مجہول و
It will be for the direction of generality and universal negation, and in positive conditional the indefinite noun is general and useful for universal negation, and in its opposite and negation it will be for particularity and particular affirmation. So the summary is that in the first case the indefinite noun comes under negation, therefore it is general, unlike the second case where it does not come under negation, therefore it is not general but rather specific and for particular affirmation. Likewise, the described indefinite noun: similarly, an indefinite noun that is described with a general description also becomes general in our view. For example, 'I do not sit with a man who is knowledgeable' — here 'rajulan' is an indefinite noun described with the general description 'aaliman', so at that time he can sit with every knowledgeable man, whether one or many; as many as he wants to sit with, all of them are included in this generality. Like the statement of Allah: 'And a believing slave is better than a polytheist' — here the word 'abd' is an indefinite noun described with the general description of faith, so it becomes general and includes every believing slave, whether rich or poor, handsome or ugly. Similarly, 'ma'ruf' (good words) includes every good and excellent thing. It only indicates generality. That is, this described indefinite noun 'a believing slave' indicates generality because it is presented in the context of reasoning ('illa). Due to His statement: 'Do not marry polytheists' — that is, Allah's statement: Do not marry Muslim women to polytheists until they believe, then it may happen; and this ruling is general because 'al-mushrikeen' is a definite plural with 'al' which is textually definitive for generality. And when presenting the reasoning for this general ruling, He said: 'A believing slave is better than a polytheist'. So the reasoning for this general ruling must also be general; therefore, 'a believing slave' which is a described indefinite noun presented in the position of 'illa, is general; otherwise, the reasoning together with the general ruling would not be correct. And because the attribution to the derivative... This is the second reason for the described indefinite noun being general: that the attribution of the ruling to the derivative indicates that the source of derivation is the cause of the ruling, whether the described of that derivative is mentioned or implied. Regarding this it is said: similarly, the attribution to the one described by the derivative — that is, if the ruling is attributed to the one described by the derivative, then also the source of derivation is the cause of the ruling. For example, when it is said: 'I sit with those who are knowledgeable' — here the described of 'aaliman' is implied; its meaning is: the men who are knowledgeable among them, i.e., the described indefinite noun, due to the generality of the 'illa which is the source of derivation. That is, when the source of derivation 'ilm (knowledge) is established, this source of derivation is taken in its general capacity, so the indefinite noun is also general. If we make the described apparent and say: 'I do not sit with the men who are knowledgeable', then this mentioned described is also general due to the generalization of the description. As for the indefinite noun... The summary of the question is: the described indefinite noun has been restricted by the restriction of faith, and the restricted is among the specific types, so how can there be generality within it? That would contradict its specificity. We say: it is specific in one respect. Summary of the answer: it is restricted and specific in one respect — that is, in relation to the absolute (unrestricted) it is specific, but it includes all individuals in which this restriction exists, so in this respect it is general. In this place, 'a believing slave' includes every believing slave, although in relation to the absolute slave it is specific. So this is relative specificity, which does not contradict generality in one respect. What contradicts it is true specificity, and that is not intended here. And the indefinite noun in other than these positions is specific — that is, if the indefinite noun occurs outside these three mentioned positions (under negation, in the context of a positive condition, described by a general description), then it is specific, because its lexical indication is for an individual. Therefore, generality and inclusiveness cannot be assumed without evidence. However, it will be absolute — that is, at that time it indicates absolute essence, and it does not indicate any additional matter beyond the essence. And this is the meaning of the famous saying: 'The absolute is that which is concerned with the essence without the attributes' — that is, it indicates only the absolute essence, and does not indicate attributes either negatively or positively. When it is in imperative/request (insha'), this indication of absolute essence occurs. For example, 'slaughter a cow' — in this the absolute cow was intended. When the Children of Israel imposed difficulty through questions, then Allah imposed difficulty upon them, as narrated by [???] with a sound chain from Ibn Abbas (marfu'an): 'If they had slaughtered any cow they intended, it would have sufficed them, but they made it difficult for themselves, so Allah made it difficult for them.' And there is established a single unknown something — that is, with this indefinite noun which occurs in other than the mentioned positions, a single unknown and ambiguous thing is established for the listener. When it is in a report (khabar), like 'I saw a man', then an ambiguous individual from among men is established for the listener. The summary is that in insha' the absolute essence and unrestricted essence are established, and in akhbar a single unknown individual.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a rule-based exposition from Hanafi usul al-fiqh on the generality (umum) of the indefinite noun (nakira) in specific contexts. The author explains three situations where a nakira indicates generality: (1) under negation (like 'la rajula' meaning 'no man'), (2) in a positive conditional (like 'in ja'aka rajulun' meaning 'if a man comes'), and (3) when described by a general attribute (like 'abdun mu'minun' — a believing slave). The first two are briefly mentioned, and the third is elaborated with Quranic evidence and rational reasoning. The key example is Qur'an 2:221: 'Do not marry polytheists until they believe, for a believing slave is better than a polytheist.' Here, 'believing slave' is a nakira mawsufa (described indefinite) that is general because it serves as the 'illa (cause) for the preceding general prohibition. This reasoning is supported by the principle that attributing a ruling to a derivative (mushtaqq) indicates that the source of derivation (makhadh al-ishtiqaq) is the cause, and that cause must be general. The author then addresses an objection: how can a described nakira be general when the description restricts it? The answer is that it is 'amm min wajh (general in one respect) and khass min wajh (specific in another). It is specific compared to a completely unrestricted noun, but it includes all individuals that share the description. Finally, the passage clarifies that a nakira outside these three contexts indicates either the absolute essence (dhat mutlaqa) when in insha' (commands) or an unknown individual when in khabar (reports). This is consistent with the Hanafi view that the nakira's default is specificity, and generality requires external evidence.

Key Terms
النكرة (al-nakira): An indefinite noun that by default indicates a single unspecified entity; it becomes general in specific contexts (negation, positive condition, general description).
النكرة الموصوفة (al-nakira al-mawsufa): An indefinite noun described by a general attribute; in Hanafi usul, it indicates generality due to the 'illa of the description.
المطلق (al-mutlaq): The absolute form that indicates the essence without any attributes; not demanding generality but merely the essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul passage, in which of the following contexts does an indefinite noun (nakira) indicate generality?
A) In a negative statement
B) In a positive conditional
C) When described by a general attribute
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
A nakira indicates generality only in three specific contexts; outside those, it indicates an individual or absolute essence.

Generality of the Qualified Indefinite and Relative Specificity
Explains how a qualified indefinite can be general due to the generality of its descriptive source

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a qualified indefinite as having relative specificity (خاص اضافي) and distinguish it from absolute specificity, applying the Hanafi principle that the generality of the derivation source conveys generality to the derived noun.

Core Idea
A qualified indefinite is not absolutely particular; it remains general with respect to all individuals sharing the description.

In Hanafi usul, a seemingly restricted term like 'a believing slave' can still be general. This lesson unpacks how the generality of the descriptive source (ماخذ اشتقاق) allows a qualified indefinite to cover all instances that match the description, while being specific only relative to the unqualified absolute. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of derivation
Recognize that the description (e.g., 'believing') has a general meaning (iman) that applies to all believers. This generality flows into the derived noun.
The 'illa or makhadh al-ishtiqaq is the source of the description.
2
Understand the inheritance of generality
Even though the noun is indefinite and qualified, it inherits the generality of its descriptive source. Therefore, 'a believing slave' includes every slave who has iman.
This is the core of the principle: al-nakirah al-mawsufah ta'umm bi 'umum al-sifah.
3
Distinguish absolute vs. relative specificity
Absolute specificity (خاص حقيقي) excludes all others entirely. Relative specificity (خاص إضافي) is narrower than the absolute but still general within its own scope.
The passage uses the example: 'a believing slave' is خاص إضافي — it does not contradict generality from one aspect.
4
Apply the principle to an example
Given the phrase 'a believing slave', derive that it includes all believing slaves in matters of rulings (e.g., freeing a slave in expiation).
This shows the practical impact of the principle on fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعموم العلة التي هي ماخذ الاشتقاق یعنی جب ماخذ اشتقاق علم بنا تو یہ ماخذ اشتقاق عمومی حیثیت میں ماخوذ ہے لہذا جو نکرہ ہے وہ بھی عام ہوگا كاشينا الموصوف یعنی اگر موصوف کو ظاہر کر دیا جائے اور کہا جائے لا اجانس الارجلا عالماً تو یہ موصوف مذکور بھی عام ہو گا لتعميم الوصف فان تيل النكرة خلاصہ سوال یہ ہے کہ نکرہ موصوفہ تو مقید بقید الایمان کر دیا گیا ہے اور مقید اقسام خاص سے ہے تو اس کے اندر عموم کیسے ہو سکتا ہے جو اس کے خصوص کیسا تھ مناقض ہے ۔ قلنا خاص من وجه المخ خلاصه جواب یه که یه مقید من وجہ خاص ہے یعنی با نسبت مطلق کے خاص ہے جس میں قید نہ ہو لیکن جن افراد میں یہ قید موجود ہوگی ان کے جمیع کو شامل ہوگا تو اس لحاظ سے عام ہے اسی مقام پر ولعبد مومن ہر ایماندار عبد کو شامل ہے اگرچہ بہ نسبت مطلق عبد کے خاص ہے تو گویا یہ خاص اضافی ہوا جو عموم من وجہ کے منافی نہیں ہے اور جو منافی ہے وہ خاص حقیقی ہے وہ یہاں مراد نہیں ہے
The generality of the cause which is the source of derivation: meaning when the source of derivation is known, this source of derivation is taken in a general sense. Therefore, the indefinite noun is also general. For example, if the described noun is made apparent and one says 'I do not know a learned man,' this mentioned described noun will also be general due to the generality of the description. As for the indefinite qualified, the summary question: the qualified indefinite has been restricted by the restriction of faith, and the restricted is among specific categories, so how can generality exist within it that would contradict its specificity? We say: it is specific from one perspective. Summary answer: it is restricted in one aspect, i.e., it is specific relative to the absolute which has no restriction, but it includes all individuals in which this restriction exists, so in that respect it is general. In this place, 'a believing slave' includes every faithful slave, even though it is specific relative to the absolute slave. Thus it is relative specificity, which is not contradictory to generality from one aspect. What is contradictory is real specificity, which is not intended here.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage discusses a subtle usul principle: when a descriptive source ('illa) is general, the derived noun carries that generality. Even if the indefinite noun is qualified by a description (like 'a believing slave'), it remains general with respect to all individuals that satisfy that description, though it is particular compared to the unqualified absolute. The objection that a qualified noun is necessarily particular is answered by distinguishing between absolute specificity and relative specificity. Relative specificity (خاص اضافی) is not incompatible with generality from the aspect of the description. The example of 'a believing slave' includes all believing slaves, even though it is narrower than 'a slave'. This is a key point in Hanafi usul about the interaction of 'umum and khusus.

Key Terms
ماخذ اشتقاق (makhadh al-ishtiqaq): The source from which a derived word is taken; the underlying meaning or attribute
نکرہ موصوفہ (nakirah mawsufah): An indefinite noun qualified by a following description
خاص اضافی (khass idafi): Relative specificity: a term is specific compared to a broader absolute but general within its own scope
خاص حقیقی (khass haqiqi): Absolute specificity: a term that is completely particular and cannot be general

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Absolute specificity (خاص حقیقی)
B) Relative specificity (خاص اضافی)
C) Both relative and absolute specificity
D) No specificity at all

Key Takeaway
A qualified indefinite is relatively specific but remains general in relation to its description; this is خاص اضافی, not خاص حقیقی.

Indefinite Nouns in Constructive vs Informative Speech
Distinguishes the meaning of indefinite nouns in constructive vs informative contexts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the meaning of an indefinite noun in insha vs ikhbar contexts and apply this distinction to derive the correct ruling.

Core Idea
An indefinite noun in insha indicates absolute essence; in ikhbar, an unknown individual.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, indefinite nouns (nakirah) are not automatically general; their interpretation depends on the sentence type. Outside three special positions (negation, condition, description), an indefinite is particular, but its exact meaning shifts based on whether the utterance is constructive (insha) or informative (ikhbar). This lesson clarifies that distinction, a key tool for accurate istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position
Recall that an indefinite noun is general only in three contexts: under negation, condition, or general description. Outside these, it is particular (khass). Confirm that your indefinite falls in the 'other positions' category.
The source text begins: 'والنكرة فى غير هذه المواضع خاص'.
2
Determine sentence type
Read the utterance and classify it as insha (constructive: command, prohibition, permission, etc.) or ikhbar (informative: statement verifiable as true/false).
The rule differs based on this classification.
3
Apply the insha rule
If the sentence is insha, the indefinite noun indicates the absolute essence (mahiyah mutlaqah) – any individual of that kind fulfills the requirement.
Source: 'تكون مطلقہ اذا كانت في الانشاء'.
4
Apply the ikhbar rule
If the sentence is ikhbar, the indefinite noun indicates a single unknown individual – a particular but unidentified entity.
Source: 'وثبت بها واحد مجهول انو اذا كانت في الاخبار'.
5
Derive the ruling
Use the distinction to interpret legal texts. For example, in a command 'أعتق رقبة', the indefinite 'رقبة' means any slave; in a statement 'رأيت رجلا', 'رجلا' means a particular unknown man.
This step operationalizes the rule for fiqh derivation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنكرة فى غير هذه المواضع خاص - لكونها دالة على فرد لكنها تكون مطلقہ اذا كانت في الانشاء - وثبت بها واحد مجهول انو اذا كانت في الاخبار
And the indefinite noun in other than these positions is particular, because it indicates an individual. But it is absolute when it occurs in constructive speech, and it establishes an unknown individual when it occurs in informative speech.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage sets the default rule for indefinite nouns when they do not fall under the three positions of generality (negation, condition, description). In such cases, the indefinite is particular. However, there is a further distinction: when the indefinite is used in constructive speech (insha, e.g., command, prohibition, or making something permissible like 'slaughter a cow'), it indicates the absolute essence (mahiyah mutlaqah) without any additional attributes. When it is used in informative speech (ikhbar, e.g., 'I saw a man'), it indicates a single unknown individual. In Hanafi usul, this distinction is crucial for understanding how indefinite nouns convey meaning in different types of sentences.

Key Terms
انشاء (insha): Constructive speech: commands, prohibitions, permissions, etc., that bring about a ruling
اخبار (ikhbar): Informative speech: statements that can be true or false

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When an indefinite noun is used in an constructive sentence (insha), what does it indicate?
A) A particular unknown individual
B) The absolute essence (mahiyah mutlaqah)
C) A known individual
D) The totality of all individuals

Key Takeaway
An indefinite noun in constructive speech indicates the essence alone, while in informative speech it points to an unknown individual.

The Rule of I'ada: Indefinite vs Definite in Repetition
Establishes the rule for whether a repeated noun refers to the same or different entity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of i'ada (repetition) to determine whether a repeated noun refers to the same or a different entity, and explain the Hanafi preference for tawkeed in the case of Quran 94:5-6.

Core Idea
A repeated indefinite indicates a different referent; a repeated definite indicates the same – but emphasis (tawkeed) can override.

This lesson explores a fundamental linguistic rule in Arabic that directly impacts legal interpretation: when a noun is repeated, does the second occurrence refer to the same thing or something else? The answer depends on definiteness. We will examine this rule through a famous Quranic example and a scholarly dispute over whether one hardship is accompanied by one ease or two.

Guided Path
1
Identify the repeated noun and its definiteness
Look at the two occurrences of the same word. Determine if each is definite (with al- or idafa) or indefinite (without). The definiteness of the second occurrence is key.
2
Apply the core rule
If the second occurrence is indefinite, it refers to a different entity than the first. If definite, it refers to the same entity.
This rule is independent of the first occurrence's definiteness.
3
Recognize the exception: tawkeed (emphasis)
When a whole phrase is repeated, it may be for emphasis, not to indicate multiplicity. This is common in Arabic rhetoric and Quranic style.
Tawkeed does not create additional entities; it reinforces the meaning.
4
Analyze the Quranic example (94:5-6)
The verse repeats 'inna ma'a al-'usri yusra'. 'al-usr' is definite and repeated as definite → same hardship. 'yusr' is indefinite and repeated as indefinite → per rule, different ease. But the Hanafi view considers the repetition of the whole sentence as tawkeed.
Ibn Abbas held the literal rule gives two eases; the author prefers tawkeed.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: does a single hardship come with one or two eases? The Hanafi view affirms a single ease strongly emphasized.
This ikhtilaf affects tafsir and spiritual reflection, not fiqh rules directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاذا اعيدت نکرة الج یعنی جب نکرہ کا ذکر کرنے کے بعد پھر اسے دوبارہ بصورت تکرہ اعادہ کیا جائے تو كانت غیر الاولی یعنی نکرہ ثانیہ سے مراد وہی نکرہ اولی نہیں ہوگا بلکہ اس کا غیر مراد ہوگا واذا اعيدت معرفة جب اس نکرہ کا اعادہ بصورة معرفہ کیا جائے خواہ لام تعریف کے ساتھ یا اضافہ کے ساتھ تو كانت عينها یعنی اس ثانیہ سے مراد بعینہ اولیٰ ہوگا کیونکہ لام میں اصل عہد ہے اور اسی طرح اضافہ میں بھی اصل عہد ہے تو جب سابقا اس کا ذکر موجود ہے تو وہ معہود ہو گا اور اس تعریف لام یا اضافہ سے اس کی طرف ہی اشارہ کرنا مطلوب ہوگا لہذا بعينها اولیٰ مراد ہوگا ۔ والمعرفة اذا اعيدت فكذلك فى الوجهين ان وجین کی توضیح کرتے ہوئے کہا کہ جب معرفہ کا اعادہ بصورت نکرہ کیا جائے تو ثانی سے مراد غیر اولیٰ ہوگا اور اگر معرفہ کا اعادہ بصورت معرفہ کیا جائے تو ثانیہ سے مراد عین اول ہوگا۔ فالمعتبر الحاصل یہ کہ اعادہ میں ثانیہ کا اعتبار ہے اگر ثانی نکرہ ہے پہلا خواہ معرفہ ہو یا نکرہ تو ثانی غیر اول ہو گا اگر شانی معرفہ ہے پہلا خواہ نکرہ ہو یا معرفہ تو ثانی عین اول ہوگا ۔ قال ابن عباس ا یہ اس کی تائید رہے کہ اعادہ نکرہ بصورت نکرہ میں ثانی غیر اول ہوتا ہے اور اعادة المعرفہ بصورت معرفہ میں عین اول ہوتا ہے اللہ تعالیٰ کے قول ان مع العسر سيراً أن مع العسر سیرا میں العسر اعادة المعرفہ بصورت معرفہ ہے تو اس سے بعینہ اول عسر مراد ہوگا لہذا عسر ایک ہوا بخلاف کیسر کے کہ یہ اعادة النکرہ بصورت نکرہ ہے لہذا یسرثانی غیر اول ہوگا تو سیسر دو ہو گئے لہذا ابن عباس نے فرمایا ہے لن يغلب عسر علی میسرین ۔ والاصح ان هذا تأكيد یعنی اصح یہ ہے کہ جملہ ثانیہ تاکید للجملة الاولی ہے لتقرير يا في النفس يعني یہ مضمون موکد ہو کر نفس میں متقرر اور ثابت و راسخ ہو جائے اور دل میں پوری طرح جگہ حاصل کرلے اور یہ تعددیسر پر دال نہیں ہے جسے ان مع زید کتاباً ان مع زید کتاباً میں جملہ ثانیہ تاکید الاولی ہے اور یہ اس پر دال نہیں کہ زید کے پاس دو کتابیں ہیں ۔ وان اقر بالف مقید بصك الخ یعنی ایک شخص ایک دستاویز تحریر کرتا ہے جس کے اندر کسی دائن کے حق میں ہزار درہم کا اقرار کرتا ہے پھر یہ دستاویز شاہدین کے سامنے پیش کر کے اسی ہزار کا اقرار کرتا ہے جو دستاویز میں لکھا ہوا ہے پھر اور دو شاہدین کے
When an indefinite is repeated, i.e., when after mentioning an indefinite, it is again repeated in indefinite form, then it is not the first: the second indefinite does not refer to the same first indefinite, but refers to something else. And when it is repeated as definite, whether with the definite article or with addition, then it is the same: the second refers to the very same first, because the definite article implies previous mention, and likewise addition implies previous mention. So when its mention has occurred previously, it becomes known, and the definite article or addition points to it. Therefore it refers to the same first. And if a definite is repeated, the same applies in both ways: when a definite is repeated as indefinite, the second refers to something other than the first; when a definite is repeated as definite, the second is the same as the first. So the effective factor is the second: if the second is indefinite, regardless of whether the first is definite or indefinite, the second is other than the first. If the second is definite, regardless of whether the first is indefinite or definite, the second is the same as the first. Ibn Abbas said, as support, that in the repetition of indefinite as indefinite, the second is other than the first; in the repetition of definite as definite, the second is the same as the first. In the saying of Allah Almighty, 'Indeed, with hardship is ease, indeed, with hardship is ease' (94:5-6), 'al-usr' is a repetition of definite as definite, so it refers to the same first hardship, so hardship is one. In contrast, 'yusr' is a repetition of indefinite as indefinite, so the second ease is other than the first, so there are two eases. Therefore Ibn Abbas said: 'A hardship will never overcome two eases.' The correct view is that this is emphasis: meaning it is more correct that the second sentence is an emphasis of the first to establish the meaning firmly in the soul, so the meaning is confirmed and firmly fixed in the mind. It does not indicate multiplicity of ease, just as in 'Indeed, Zaid has a book, indeed, Zaid has a book', the second sentence is emphasis of the first and does not indicate that Zaid has two books. And if someone acknowledges with a document bound by a contract, i.e., a person writes a document in which he acknowledges a thousand dirhams owed to a creditor, then presents this document before witnesses and acknowledges the same thousand written in the document, then two witnesses...
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage explains a key rule in Arabic discourse: when a noun is repeated in the same form, if the second is indefinite it refers to a different entity; if definite, it refers to the same. This is illustrated by the Quranic verse 'inna ma'a al-'usri yusra' (94:5-6). According to Ibn Abbas, the repetition of indefinite 'yusr' indicates two different eases, while definite 'al-usr' indicates the same hardship. However, the author notes that the sounder position (al-ashah) is that the second occurrence is emphatic (tawkeed), not indicating multiplicity, similar to saying 'Zaid has a book, Zaid has a book' which is just emphasis. This is a significant ikhtilaf among scholars. The passage ends with an incomplete example about acknowledgment (iqrar) where the principle of repetition might apply, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
اعادة (i'ada): The repetition of a word in discourse, which can indicate either the same or a different referent depending on definiteness.
تأكيد (tawkeed): Emphasis; a rhetorical device to reinforce a statement.
نكرة (nakira): Indefinite noun; a noun without the definite article or idafa.
معرفة (ma'rifa): Definite noun; a noun with al- or in idafa construction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'ashah' (most correct) view regarding the repetition of 'yusr' in Surah Inshirah?
A) It indicates two distinct eases
B) It is emphatic (tawkeed) and does not indicate multiplicity
C) It indicates one ease divided into two parts
D) It is a grammatical error

Key Takeaway
The rule of i'ada: a repeated indefinite noun indicates a different entity; a repeated definite indicates the same; however, repetition of whole sentences is often tawkeed (emphasis), not multiplicity.

The Hanafi Rule of Repetition: Nakira and Ma'rifa
Explains the rule when a definite or indefinite noun is repeated twice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of i'ada to determine whether a repeated noun refers to the same or a different entity, using the example from Surah Inshirah.

Core Idea
A repeated indefinite noun indicates a different entity; a repeated definite noun indicates the same entity.

In Hanafi usul, the effect of repeating a noun depends on its definiteness. This rule has profound implications for understanding Qur'anic verses and legal documents. We'll explore the rule and its proof from the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of repetition
Distinguish between repetition of an indefinite noun as indefinite, and repetition of an indefinite as definite.
The same applies when the first noun is definite.
2
State the rule for each type
When an indefinite is repeated as indefinite, the second is a different entity. When repeated as definite, the second is the same. Similarly for definite repeated as definite or indefinite.
3
Examine the Quranic example
Analyze the verse 'Indeed, with hardship is ease; Indeed, with hardship is ease' (94:5-6) and apply the rule to 'al-'usr' (definite) and 'yusr' (indefinite).
Ibn Abbas concluded hardship is one, ease is two.
4
Understand the tawkid alternative
Some scholars consider the repetition as tawkid (emphasis) of the entire sentence, not indicating multiplicity of ease.
The correct opinion according to the commentator is tawkid, but the rule for noun repetition still stands.
5
Determine the implication for legal interpretation
Apply the rule to fiqh cases like confession (iqrar) where definiteness affects the number of obligations.
This prepares for the next lesson on iqrar.

غیر مراد ہوگا واذا اعيدت معرفة جب اس نکرہ کا اعادہ بصورة معرفہ کیا جائے خواہ لام تعریف کے ساتھ یا اضافہ کے ساتھ تو كانت عينها یعنی اس ثانیہ سے مراد بعینہ اولیٰ ہوگا کیونکہ لام میں اصل عہد ہے اور اسی الحاصل یہ کہ اعادہ میں ثانیہ کا اعتبار ہے اگر ثانی نکرہ ہے پہلا خواہ معرفہ ہو یا نکرہ تو ثانی غیر اول ہو گا اگر شانی معرفہ ہے پہلا خواہ نکرہ ہو یا معرفہ تو ثانی عین اول ہوگا ۔ قال ابن عباس ا یہ اس کی تائید رہے کہ اعادہ نکرہ بصورت نکرہ میں ثانی غیر اول ہوتا ہے اور اعادة المعرفہ بصورت معرفہ میں عین اول ہوتا ہے اللہ تعالیٰ کے قول ان مع العسر سيراً أن مع العسر سیرا میں العسر اعادة المعرفه بصورت معرفہ ہے تو اس سے بعینہ اول عسر مراد ہوگا لہذا عسر ایک ہوا بخلاف کیسر کے کہ یہ اعادة النکرہ بصورت نکرہ ہے لہذا یسرثانی غیر اول ہوگا تو سیسر دو ہو گئے لہذا ابن عباس نے فرمایا ہے لن يغلب عسر علی میسرین ۔ والاصح ان هذا تأكيد یعنی اصح یہ ہے کہ جملہ ثانیہ تاکید للجملة الاولی ہے لتقرير يا في النفس يعني یہ مضمون موکد ہو کر نفس میں متقرر اور ثابت و راسخ ہو جائے اور دل میں پوری طرح جگہ حاصل کرلے اور یہ تعددیسر پر دال نہیں ہے جسے ان مع زید کتاباً ان مع زید کتاباً میں جملہ ثانیہ تاکید الاولی ہے اور یہ اس پر دال نہیں کہ زید کے پاس دو کتابیں ہیں ۔ وان اقر بالف مقید بصك الخ یعنی ایک شخص ایک دستاویز تحریر کرتا ہے جس کے اندر کسی دائن کے حق میں ہزار درہم کا اقرار کرتا ہے پھر یہ دستاویز شاہدین کے سامنے پیش کر کے اسی ہزار کا اقرار کرتا ہے جو دستاویز میں لکھا ہوا ہے پھر اور دو شاہدین کے سامنے یہی صک و دستاویز پیش کر کے اسی ہزار کا اقرار کرتا ہے جو مکتوب فی ہذا الفك ہے تو اس مقر پر صرف ایک ہزار واجب ہو گا کیونکہ یہ اعادہ معرفہ بصورت معرفہ ہے لہذا ثانی عین اول ہوگا ۔ و ان اقربه منكر آ یعنی ہزار درہم منکر کا اقرار کرتا ہے یعنی اگر ہزار کو مقید بقید الاک نہیں کرتا بلکہ ایک مجلس میں روبرو شابدین الف منکر کا اقرار کرتا ہے پھر مجلس آخر میں بحفرة شاہدین الف منکر کا اقرار کرتا ہے تو ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اس مقر پر دو ہزار واجب ہو جائیں گے یہ اعادہ نکرہ بصورت نکرہ ہے لہذا ثانی غیر اول ہوگا ۔ الا ان يتحد المجلس اگر مجلس ایک ہی میں اعادة الف کرتا ہے تو بالاتفاق الف واحد واجب ہوگا اس سے سابقہ صورت میں صاحبین کا اختلاف ہے کہ وہ ایک ہی الف واجب قرار دیتے ہیں کیونکہ اس اعادہ کلام کو وہ صرف تاکید پر محمول کرتے ہیں ۔ فالاقسام العقلية اربعة ایک نکرہ کا اعادہ بصورت معرفہ مراد عین اول ہو گا جیسے كما ارسلنا الى فرعون رسولاً فعصی فرعون الرسول تو یہاں ثانی رسول سے مراد بعینہ اول ہے وہ حضرت موسی علیہ السلام دوسرا یہ کہ اعاده تكره بصورت نکرہ اور ثانی سے مراد غیر اول ہو گا جیسے یسرا یسرا میں تیسرا یہ کہ اعاده المعرفه بصورت معرفه مراد عین اول ہوگا جیسے العمر اللہ میں فی قولہ تعالیٰ فان مع العسر يسراً ان مع ان مع العسر یسرا اور چوتھا احتمال اعادة المعرفة بصورت نکرہ اس کی مثال غیر مذکو بہ البتہ اس کی مثال اپنی طرف سے یہ دی جا سکتی ہے وهو ما اذا اقر بالف مقيد بصك یعنی بزار در هم جو مکتوب فی الصک ہے اسی کا اقرار کرتا ہے تو یہ معرفہ کی صورت ہوئی تم اترتی مجلس آخر پھر دوسری مجلس میں الف منکر کا اقرارہ کرتا ہے یعنی مقید بالصک نہیں کرتا ہے تو اس صورت میں اعادة المعرفہ بصورت نکرہ ہوا لا رواية لهذا اس مسئلہ کے متعلق ائمہ سے کوئی روایت تو نہیں ہے لکن ینبغی لیکن منا یہی نظر آتا ہے کہ امام رحمہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے نزدیک دو ہزار واجب ہونے چانہیں کیونکہ اعاده المعرفه بصورت نکرہ ہے لہذا ثانی غیر اول ہوگا ومنها الى وهى نكرة یعنی لفظ اس اصل میں نکرہ ہے کیونکہ باعتبار اصل وضع کے یہ خصوص اور قصد الی الفرد کے لئے ہے جیسے باقی نکرات کی حالت ہے تعم بالصفة یعنی اس کا عموم صفہ کی وجہ سے ہوگا جیسے رجلا عالما میں صفتہ کی وجہ سے عموم تھا اور اس کی استعمال بالاضافہ ہوتی ہے اگر اصافۃ الی النکرہ ہے تو پھر اس
Mentioning the issue he said: 'When an indefinite noun (nakira) is repeated [as indefinite], then the second is other than the first. When that indefinite is repeated as definite (ma'rifa), whether with the definite article or by idafa, then the second is identical to the first, because the definite article implies [prior] mention, and similarly idafa implies mention.' So when the first is known, the second refers back to it. And if a definite is repeated, similarly: if repeated as indefinite, the second is other than the first; if repeated as definite, the second is identical to the first. The rule is that the second is what matters: if the second is indefinite, whether the first is definite or indefinite, the second is other; if the second is definite, the second is identical. Ibn Abbas said, supporting this: in the verse 'Indeed, with hardship [is] ease; Indeed, with hardship [is] ease' (Quran 94:5-6), 'hardship' (al-'usr) is a definite repeated as definite, so it refers to the same hardship, so hardship is one. In contrast, 'ease' (yusr) is an indefinite repeated as indefinite, so the second yusr is other than the first, giving two eases. Hence Ibn Abbas said: 'One hardship will never overcome two eases.' The correct opinion is that the second sentence is a confirmation (tawkid) of the first, to settle it in the soul, and does not indicate multiplicity of ease, just as in 'Indeed, with Zaid is a book; Indeed, with Zaid is a book', the second sentence is confirmation and does not indicate two books. And if someone confesses to a thousand dirhams specified in a document, then produces the document before witnesses and confesses to the thousand written in it, then again before two other witnesses presents the same document and confesses to the thousand written in it, then only one thousand is due, because this is a definite repeated as definite. If he confesses to an unspecified thousand (munakkar) before two witnesses in one session, then in another session before two witnesses to an unspecified thousand, then according to Abu Hanifa two thousands are due, because this is an indefinite repeated as indefinite. Unless the session is the same, then by consensus one thousand is due. The Sahibayn differ in the former case, considering it a confirmation. So the rational divisions are four: (1) indefinite repeated as definite – refers to same, e.g., 'We sent to Pharaoh a messenger, but Pharaoh disobeyed the messenger' (the second 'messenger' is the same Moses). (2) indefinite repeated as indefinite – refers to other, e.g., 'ease, ease'. (3) definite repeated as definite – refers to same, e.g., 'Indeed, with hardship is ease; Indeed, with hardship is ease'. (4) definite repeated as indefinite – no example given, but one could give: if he confesses to a thousand specified in a document (ma'rifa) and then in another session confesses to a thousand unspecified (nakira), then this is definite repeated as indefinite. There is no transmitted narration about this, but it seems that according to the Imam, two thousands would be due because it is definite repeated as indefinite. And 'ila' is originally indefinite because its original placing is for specification and pointing to an individual like other indefinites; it becomes general by description, like 'a learned man' where the description gives generality, and it is used with idafa; if added to an indefinite, then...
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is an extensive commentary on the usul rule regarding the repetition (i'ada) of nouns. It clarifies that when an indefinite noun (nakira) is repeated as an indefinite, the second occurrence refers to a different entity (ghayr al-ula). If the indefinite is repeated as a definite (with alif lam or idafa), then the second refers to the same entity ('ayn al-ula). The same rule applies to the repetition of a definite noun. The commentator then provides a Quranic proof from Surah Inshirah (94:5-6) where 'al-'usr' is repeated as definite, so hardship is one, while 'yusr' is repeated as indefinite, so ease is two. Ibn Abbas concluded 'one hardship will never overcome two eases'. The commentator also notes that some consider the repetition as mere tawkid (emphasis) and not indicating multiplicity. To illustrate the rule in fiqh, the passage discusses the case of iqrar (confession): if someone confesses to a thousand dirhams with a document (definite) in multiple sessions, only one thousand is due (definite repeated as definite). If he confesses to an unspecified thousand (indefinite) in separate sessions, Abu Hanifa rules two thousands are due (indefinite repeated as indefinite), unless the sessions are the same, then it is one. The Sahibayn disagree in the latter case, considering it confirmation. Finally, the passage lists four rational divisions of repetition and applies the rule to a hypothetical scenario of confessing first with a document (definite) then without (indefinite), suggesting Abu Hanifa would rule two thousands. The passage ends discussing the word 'ila' (to) as originally indefinite, becoming general by description or idafa.

Key Terms
اعادة (i'ada): Repetition of a noun in a subsequent clause, with a change or maintenance of definiteness.
نكرة (nakira): An indefinite noun, not specified by alif lam or idafa.
معرفة (ma'rifa): A definite noun, specified by alif lam or idafa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule discussed, if an indefinite noun is repeated as indefinite, what does the second occurrence refer to?
A) The same entity as the first
B) A different entity from the first
C) It is always tawkid and does not refer to a specific entity
D) It depends on context

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the definiteness of the second occurrence determines whether a repeated noun refers to the same entity (if definite) or a different entity (if indefinite).

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Applying I'ada to Iqrar: Separate Confessions and the Hanafi-Sahibayn Dispute
Applies the rule of i'ada to iqrar and discusses the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of i'ada to the fiqh case of iqrar (confession) and explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the number of debts.

Core Idea
When an indefinite confession is repeated in a separate council, it creates a second debt according to Abu Hanifa.

The rule of i'ada has direct fiqh applications, particularly in confessions (iqrar). If a person confesses to an unspecified thousand dirhams in two separate council sessions, does he owe one thousand or two? Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ based on their interpretation of repetition. Let's analyze the case.

Guided Path
1
Understand the confession scenario
Consider two cases: confession with a document (definite) and without (indefinite), in separate councils.
The document makes the thousand definite.
2
Apply the rule of i'ada
For the definite confession, the second is same debt (one thousand). For the indefinite confession, the second is a different debt (two thousands) according to Abu Hanifa.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's objection
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider the repetition as tawkid, so only one debt is due.
They analogize to the sentence 'Indeed, with Zaid is a book' repeated.
4
Examine the four rational divisions
The passage lists four possibilities: indefinite→definite, indefinite→indefinite, definite→definite, definite→indefinite, each with its ruling.
5
Determine the outcome for the hypothetical case
If first confession is definite (with document) and second is indefinite (without), it is definite→indefinite. Abu Hanifa would rule two thousands due, though no explicit narration exists.
This shows the systematic application of the rule.

اس مقر پر دو ہزار واجب ہو جائیں گے یہ اعادہ نکرہ بصورت نکرہ ہے لہذا ثانی غیر اول ہوگا ۔ الا ان يتحد المجلس اگر مجلس ایک ہی میں اعادة الف کرتا ہے تو بالاتفاق الف واحد واجب ہوگا اس سے سابقہ صورت میں صاحبین کا اختلاف ہے کہ وہ ایک ہی الف واجب قرار دیتے ہیں کیونکہ اس اعادہ کلام کو وہ صرف تاکید پر محمول کرتے ہیں ۔ فالاقسام العقلية اربعة ایک نکرہ کا اعادہ بصورت معرفہ مراد عین اول ہو گا جیسے كما ارسلنا الى فرعون رسولاً فعصی فرعون الرسول تو یہاں ثانی رسول سے مراد بعینہ اول ہے وہ حضرت موسی علیہ السلام دوسرا یہ کہ اعاده تكره بصورت نکرہ اور ثانی سے مراد غیر اول ہو گا جیسے یسرا یسرا میں تیسرا یہ کہ اعاده المعرفه بصورت معرفه مراد عین اول ہوگا جیسے العمر اللہ میں فی قولہ تعالیٰ فان مع العسر يسراً ان مع ان مع العسر یسرا اور چوتھا احتمال اعادة المعرفة بصورت نکرہ اس کی مثال غیر مذکو بہ البتہ اس کی مثال اپنی طرف سے یہ دی جا سکتی ہے وهو ما اذا اقر بالف مقيد بصك یعنی بزار در هم جو مکتوب فی الصک ہے اسی کا اقرار کرتا ہے تو یہ معرفہ کی صورت ہوئی تم اترتی مجلس آخر پھر دوسری مجلس میں الف منکر کا اقرارہ کرتا ہے یعنی مقید بالصک نہیں کرتا ہے تو اس صورت میں اعادة المعرفہ بصورت نکرہ ہوا لا رواية لهذا اس مسئلہ کے متعلق ائمہ سے کوئی روایت تو نہیں ہے لکن ینبغی لیکن منا یہی نظر آتا ہے کہ امام رحمہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے نزدیک دو ہزار واجب ہونے چانہیں کیونکہ اعاده المعرفه بصورت نکرہ ہے لہذا ثانی غیر اول ہوگا ومنها الى وهى نكرة یعنی لفظ اس اصل میں نکرہ ہے کیونکہ باعتبار اصل وضع کے یہ خصوص اور قصد الی الفرد کے لئے ہے جیسے باقی نکرات کی حالت ہے تعم بالصفة یعنی اس کا عموم صفہ کی وجہ سے ہوگا جیسے رجلا عالما میں صفتہ کی وجہ سے عموم تھا اور اس کی استعمال بالاضافہ ہوتی ہے اگر اصافۃ الی النکرہ ہے تو پھر اس
Mentioning the issue he said: 'When an indefinite noun (nakira) is repeated [as indefinite], then the second is other than the first. When that indefinite is repeated as definite (ma'rifa), whether with the definite article or by idafa, then the second is identical to the first, because the definite article implies [prior] mention, and similarly idafa implies mention.' So when the first is known, the second refers back to it. And if a definite is repeated, similarly: if repeated as indefinite, the second is other than the first; if repeated as definite, the second is identical to the first. The rule is that the second is what matters: if the second is indefinite, whether the first is definite or indefinite, the second is other; if the second is definite, the second is identical. Ibn Abbas said, supporting this: in the verse 'Indeed, with hardship [is] ease; Indeed, with hardship [is] ease' (Quran 94:5-6), 'hardship' (al-'usr) is a definite repeated as definite, so it refers to the same hardship, so hardship is one. In contrast, 'ease' (yusr) is an indefinite repeated as indefinite, so the second yusr is other than the first, giving two eases. Hence Ibn Abbas said: 'One hardship will never overcome two eases.' The correct opinion is that the second sentence is a confirmation (tawkid) of the first, to settle it in the soul, and does not indicate multiplicity of ease, just as in 'Indeed, with Zaid is a book; Indeed, with Zaid is a book', the second sentence is confirmation and does not indicate two books. And if someone confesses to a thousand dirhams specified in a document, then produces the document before witnesses and confesses to the thousand written in it, then again before two other witnesses presents the same document and confesses to the thousand written in it, then only one thousand is due, because this is a definite repeated as definite. If he confesses to an unspecified thousand (munakkar) before two witnesses in one session, then in another session before two witnesses to an unspecified thousand, then according to Abu Hanifa two thousands are due, because this is an indefinite repeated as indefinite. Unless the session is the same, then by consensus one thousand is due. The Sahibayn differ in the former case, considering it a confirmation. So the rational divisions are four: (1) indefinite repeated as definite – refers to same, e.g., 'We sent to Pharaoh a messenger, but Pharaoh disobeyed the messenger' (the second 'messenger' is the same Moses). (2) indefinite repeated as indefinite – refers to other, e.g., 'ease, ease'. (3) definite repeated as definite – refers to same, e.g., 'Indeed, with hardship is ease; Indeed, with hardship is ease'. (4) definite repeated as indefinite – no example given, but one could give: if he confesses to a thousand specified in a document (ma'rifa) and then in another session confesses to a thousand unspecified (nakira), then this is definite repeated as indefinite. There is no transmitted narration about this, but it seems that according to the Imam, two thousands would be due because it is definite repeated as indefinite. And 'ila' is originally indefinite because its original placing is for specification and pointing to an individual like other indefinites; it becomes general by description, like 'a learned man' where the description gives generality, and it is used with idafa; if added to an indefinite, then...
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is an extensive commentary on the usul rule regarding the repetition (i'ada) of nouns. It clarifies that when an indefinite noun (nakira) is repeated as an indefinite, the second occurrence refers to a different entity (ghayr al-ula). If the indefinite is repeated as a definite (with alif lam or idafa), then the second refers to the same entity ('ayn al-ula). The same rule applies to the repetition of a definite noun. The commentator then provides a Quranic proof from Surah Inshirah (94:5-6) where 'al-'usr' is repeated as definite, so hardship is one, while 'yusr' is repeated as indefinite, so ease is two. Ibn Abbas concluded 'one hardship will never overcome two eases'. The commentator also notes that some consider the repetition as mere tawkid (emphasis) and not indicating multiplicity. To illustrate the rule in fiqh, the passage discusses the case of iqrar (confession): if someone confesses to a thousand dirhams with a document (definite) in multiple sessions, only one thousand is due (definite repeated as definite). If he confesses to an unspecified thousand (indefinite) in separate sessions, Abu Hanifa rules two thousands are due (indefinite repeated as indefinite), unless the sessions are the same, then it is one. The Sahibayn disagree in the latter case, considering it confirmation. Finally, the passage lists four rational divisions of repetition and applies the rule to a hypothetical scenario of confessing first with a document (definite) then without (indefinite), suggesting Abu Hanifa would rule two thousands. The passage ends discussing the word 'ila' (to) as originally indefinite, becoming general by description or idafa.

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrar): A legal confession or admission, often of a debt.
منكر (munakkar): An indefinite noun; in the context of iqrar, a confession not specified by a document or other qualifier.
صك (sikk): A document, deed, or written instrument that specifies the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a person confesses to an unspecified thousand dirhams in two separate councils, how many thousands are due?
A) One, because it is tawkid
B) Two, because the second confession is an indefinite repeated as indefinite
C) One, because the councils are separate
D) Two, only if the councils are the same

Key Takeaway
When applying the rule of i'ada to confessions, indefinite repetition creates separate debts unless the session is the same; definite repetition always refers to the same debt.

The Four Categories of Repetition (I'ada) in Hanafi Usul
Exposition of four rational types of word repetition and their legal effects in iqrar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a repeated word as ma'rifa or nakira, determine whether the second occurrence is identical or new, and apply the Hanafi rulings on obligation in iqrar based on the four rational categories of i'ada.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a repeated indefinite creates a new entity; a repeated definite retains identity; assembly may override.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi usul principle concerning the repetition (i'ada) of words, particularly in the context of iqrar (acknowledgment). The author presents four rational categories based on the definiteness of the first and second occurrences. Understanding these categories is essential for determining whether a second acknowledgment creates a separate obligation or merely emphasizes the first.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiteness of each occurrence
For each repeated term, classify the first and second as ma'rifa (definite) or nakira (indefinite).
Use the presence/absence of 'al-' or idafah to determine definiteness.
2
Apply the Hanafi i'ada rule
If both are ma'rifa, the second is identical; if both nakira (in different assemblies), the second is new; if the forms differ (one ma'rifa, one nakira), the second is other than the first.
This is the core principle derived from the text.
3
Consider the assembly condition
Same assembly may cause even nakira repetition to be considered emphasis (tawkid) according to Sahibayn; Abu Hanifa's view differs. Determine which assembly scenario applies.
The text notes that by agreement, same-assembly repetition of a definite yields one obligation, but for indefinite there is a dispute.
4
Determine the number of obligations in iqrar
Based on the category and assembly, state whether one or two amounts are due on the acknowledger.
The example of 'thousand' dirhams is used throughout the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سامنے یہی صک و دستاویز پیش کر کے اسی ہزار کا اقرار کرتا ہے جو مکتوب فی ہذا الفك ہے تو اس مقر پر صرف ایک ہزار واجب ہو گا کیونکہ یہ اعادہ معرفہ بصورت معرفہ ہے لہذا ثانی عین اول ہوگا ۔ و ان اقربه منكر آ یعنی ہزار درہم منکر کا اقرار کرتا ہے یعنی اگر ہزار کو مقید بقید الاک نہیں کرتا بلکہ ایک مجلس میں روبرو شابدین الف منکر کا اقرار کرتا ہے پھر مجلس آخر میں بحفرة شاہدین الف منکر کا اقرار کرتا ہے تو ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اس مقر پر دو ہزار واجب ہو جائیں گے یہ اعادہ نکرہ بصورت نکرہ ہے لہذا ثانی غیر اول ہوگا ۔ الا ان يتحد المجلس اگر مجلس ایک ہی میں اعادة الف کرتا ہے تو بالاتفاق الف واحد واجب ہوگا اس سے سابقہ صورت میں صاحبین کا اختلاف ہے کہ وہ ایک ہی الف واجب قرار دیتے ہیں کیونکہ اس اعادہ کلام کو وہ صرف تاکید پر محمول کرتے ہیں ۔ فالاقسام العقلية اربعة ایک نکرہ کا اعادہ بصورت معرفہ مراد عین اول ہو گا جیسے كما ارسلنا الى فرعون رسولاً فعصی فرعون الرسول تو یہاں ثانی رسول سے مراد بعینہ اول ہے وہ حضرت موسی علیہ السلام دوسرا یہ کہ اعاده تكره بصورت نکرہ اور ثانی سے مراد غیر اول ہو گا جیسے یسرا یسرا میں تیسرا یہ کہ اعاده المعرفه بصورت معرفه مراد عین اول ہوگا جیسے العمر اللہ میں فی قولہ تعالیٰ فان مع العسر يسراً ان مع ان مع العسر يسرا اور چوتھا احتمال اعادة المعرفة بصورت نکرہ اس کی مثال غیر مذکو بہ البتہ اس کی مثال اپنی طرف سے یہ دی جا سکتی ہے وهو ما اذا اقر بالف مقيد بصك یعنی بزار در هم جو مکتوب فی الصک ہے اسی کا اقرار کرتا ہے تو یہ معرفہ کی صورت ہوئی تم اترتی مجلس آخر پھر دوسری مجلس میں الف منکر کا اقرارہ کرتا ہے یعنی مقید بالصک نہیں کرتا ہے تو اس صورت میں اعادة المعرفہ بصورت نکرہ ہوا لا رواية لهذا اس مسئلہ کے متعلق ائمہ سے کوئی روایت تو نہیں ہے لکن ینبغی لیکن منا یہی نظر آتا ہے کہ امام رحمہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے نزدیک دو ہزار واجب ہونے چانہیں کیونکہ اعاده المعرفه بصورت نکرہ ہے لہذا ثانی غیر اول ہوگا
If he presents the same document and acknowledges the same thousand (dirhams) that are written in this document, then only one thousand is obligatory on this acknowledger, because this is repetition of a definite (ma'rifa) in the form of a definite, so the second is identical to the first. And if he acknowledges a 'thousand' indefinite – meaning he does not restrict the thousand with the restriction of 'the document' but acknowledges an indefinite thousand in one assembly in front of witnesses, then in another assembly acknowledges an indefinite thousand in front of witnesses, then according to Abu Hanifa two thousands become obligatory on this acknowledger – this is repetition of an indefinite (nakira) in the form of an indefinite, so the second is other than the first. Unless the assembly is the same: if he repeats (acknowledging) a thousand in the same assembly, then by agreement one thousand is obligatory. This differs from the previous case where the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they rule that only one thousand is obligatory, because they consider this repetition of speech as merely an emphasis. So the rational categories are four: (1) Repetition of a nakira in the form of a ma'rifa – the intended meaning is the exact first, as in {Indeed We sent to Pharaoh a messenger, then Pharaoh disobeyed the messenger} – here 'the messenger' (second) refers exactly to the first, i.e. Moses, peace be upon him. (2) Repetition of a nakira in the form of a nakira – the second refers to something other than the first, as in 'yusra yusra' (ease ease). (3) Repetition of a ma'rifa in the form of a ma'rifa – the intended meaning is the exact first, as in 'al-umr Allah'? in His statement {For indeed with hardship comes ease, indeed with hardship comes ease}. (4) The possibility of repetition of a ma'rifa in the form of a nakira – its example is not mentioned; rather its example can be given from oneself: it is when someone acknowledges a thousand restricted by a document, i.e. a thousand dirhams written in the document, he acknowledges that same – this is the form of ma'rifa. Then in another assembly he acknowledges an indefinite thousand, i.e. not restricted by the document, then in this case it is repetition of a ma'rifa in the form of a nakira. There is no transmission on this matter from the Imams, but it is appropriate that according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) two thousands should be obligatory, because it is repetition of a ma'rifa in the form of a nakira, so the second is other than the first.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the usul principle regarding the repetition (i'ada) of words and its effect on the obligation in iqrar (acknowledgment). The author discusses four rational categories of repetition based on whether the repeated word is definite or indefinite. He begins with a practical example: if someone acknowledges a specific amount mentioned in a document (ma'rifa) and later repeats the same acknowledgment in the same form, only one amount is due because the second is identical to the first. Conversely, if he acknowledges an unrestricted thousand (nakira) in one assembly and again an unrestricted thousand in a different assembly, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa two thousands are due because both are indefinite and thus distinct, unless the assembly is the same, in which case it is considered emphasis. The author then lists four categories with Quranic and Arabic usage examples: (1) nakira repeated as ma'rifa – second is the same as first (e.g., 'a messenger' then 'the messenger'); (2) nakira repeated as nakira – second is other (e.g., 'yusra yusra'); (3) ma'rifa repeated as ma'rifa – second is same (e.g., the repetition of 'al-usr' in Surah Inshirah); (4) ma'rifa repeated as nakira – example from iqrar with a document. He notes that the fourth case has no explicit transmission from the founders but reasoning dictates that two thousands be obligatory because the second is indefinite and thus a new obligation.

Key Terms
اعادہ (i'ada): Repetition of a word; here analyzed into four categories based on definiteness of first and second occurrence.
معرفہ (ma'rifa): A definite noun; repetition retains identity.
نکرہ (nakira): An indefinite noun; repetition as nakira indicates a different entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if someone acknowledges 'a thousand dirhams' (indefinite) in one assembly and then acknowledges 'a thousand dirhams' (indefinite) in a different assembly, how many thousands are due according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) One thousand.
B) Two thousands.
C) Three thousands.
D) It depends on the witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the definiteness of a repeated word determines whether it is identical or new; assembly only matters for indefinite repetition in the view of the two companions.

Generality of 'Ayyu' Through Descriptive Clauses
Explaining that 'ayyu' is inherently indefinite and gains generality through description

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how the particle 'ayyu' acquires generality through a descriptive clause and apply this analysis to distinguish between two types of conditional statements in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
'Ayyu' is indefinite; its generality springs from the descriptive clause, not the word itself.

The particle 'ayyu' (whoever/which) is a key element in interpreting general and specific texts in Hanafi usul. This lesson clarifies that 'ayyu' is inherently indefinite (nakira) and only becomes general when a semantic description (sifa) follows, which can be a relative clause or condition. Two examples involving slave manumission illustrate the practical difference in rulings depending on how the description is attached.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base nature of 'ayyu'
Recognize that 'ayyu' is indefinite in origin, used for a vague individual. It does not convey generality by itself.
This differs from particles like 'man' (who) which are inherently general in some views.
2
Understand the role of the descriptive clause (sifa)
The description attached to 'ayyu' provides the attribute that makes the statement general. This description is semantic, not just a grammatical adjective; it includes relative clauses and conditions.
The text emphasizes that 'sifa' here is wasf ma'nawi, not na't nahwi.
3
Analyze the first example: description on the slave
If the description (e.g., 'strikes you') is attached to the slave via a relative clause, it qualifies all slaves. Thus, all slaves who fulfill the condition become free.
The generality arises because the description is connected to the indefinite 'ayyu 'ibadi'.
4
Analyze the second example: description detached from the slave
If the description is attached to the addressee's action (e.g., 'you strike him'), the description no longer qualifies the slave. The statement remains indefinite, and only one slave is freed (first struck or chosen).
The difference hinges on whether the description qualifies the subject of 'ayyu' or the action of another.
5
Resolve the grammatical difficulty
The author notes that if 'sifa' were taken as a grammatical adjective, neither example would contain one. Thus, the intended meaning is a semantic description, aligning with Hanafi usul's broader interpretation.
This clarifies that the principle is not strictly grammatical but based on meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الى وهى نكرة یعنی لفظ اس اصل میں نکرہ ہے کیونکہ باعتبار اصل وضع کے یہ خصوص اور قصد الی الفرد کے لئے ہے جیسے باقی نکرات کی حالت ہے تعم بالصفة یعنی اس کا عموم صفہ کی وجہ سے ہوگا جیسے رجلا عالما میں صفتہ کی وجہ سے عموم تھا اور اس کی استعمال بالاضافہ ہوتی ہے اگر اصافۃ الی النکرہ ہے تو پھر اس کی تنکیر ظاہر ہے اور اگر اس کی اضافة الى المعرفہ ہے تو بھی کہا جاتا ہے کہ انها لواحد مبهم مصلح لكل واحد من الاحاد علی سبیل البدل بعض نے کہا کہ اسے غلو فی الایہام ہے اضافہ الی المعروفہ کے ساتھ اسے تعریف حاصل نہیں ہوتی جیسے لفظ مثل دیر کے متعلق کہا جاتا ہے اور صنف نے جو تعم بصفہ کہا ہے اس صفہ سے مراد نعت نحوس نہیں ہے بلکہ وصف معنوی مراد ہے یعنی معنی قائم بالغیر جو کہ صلہ اور شرط وغیرہ کو شامل ہے کیونکہ جملہ جو اس کے بعد واقع ہوتا ہے وہ صلہ ہوتا ہے اگر اسی موصولہ ہو تو اگر شرطیہ ہو تو پھر جملہ شرط کہلاتا ہے نحویوں کے نزدیک وہ حقیقی صفہ نہیں ہوتا ۔ فان قال انى عبيدى ضربك نهو جدا تو اس صورت میں اگر جمیع عبید نے مارا ہے خواه مجتمعا ما را سویا متعاقبا سب آزاد ہو جائیں گے کیونکہ ضرب ایک صفہ عامہ ہے لہذا اس کی وجہ سے عام ہو کرائی جمیع عبید کو شامل ہو جائیگا وان قال اتی عبیدی ضربته فهو حد یعنی اگر یوں کہا کہ میرے جس غلام کو تو ماریگا وہ آزاد ہے تو اس صورت میں صرف ایک ازاد ہو گا اگرچہ اس نے جمیع کو مارا ہو اگر بالترتیب تمام کو مارا تو صرف وہی ازاد ہوگا جس کو اولاً مارا اگر اس نے بلا ترتیب یکبارگی تمام کو ضرب کی ازاد تو صرف ایک ہوگا البتہ اس میں مولیٰ کو خیار ہوگا جس کی حریت کو قبول کرے قالوا ان دونوں صورتوں کے اندر فرق کرنے کے متعلق وہ کہتے ہیں کہ اول میں عبد کی وصف ضرب کے ساتھ کی گئی جو کہ عام ہے لہذا عموم صفہ کی وجہ سے امتی عبیدی عام و شامل لجميع الافراد ہے لہذا جمیع افراد ازاد ہو جائیں گے
