اس سے منقطع کرکے اس کی نسبت الى المخاطب کر دی گئی ہے۔ لہذا اس صورت میں وہ عام نہیں ہوگا ۔ وهذا الفرق مشكل من جهة النحو الم به فرق مذکور نحوی لحاظ سے مشکل کیونکہ اگر وصف سے مراد نعت نحومی ہے تو ان دونوں صورتوں میں سے کسی میں نعت نحوی نہیں ہو سکتی کیونکہ ان دونوں صورتوں میں اسی موصولہ ہے یا شرطیہ تو جملہ جو اس کے بعد ہے وہ صلہ بنے گا یا شرط نعت نحوی کسی صورت میں نہیں ہو سکتی اگر وصف سے مراد
And among them (the particles) is 'ayyu' (which) – it is a nakira (indefinite) in origin, because according to its original usage it is for specification and intention towards an individual, like the state of other indefinite nouns. It becomes general by a descriptive clause (sifa). That is, its generality occurs due to a description, as in 'rajulan 'aliman' (a learned man) – there was generality due to the description. And it is used with idafa (construct). If it is added to a nakira, then its indefiniteness is obvious. And if it is added to a ma'rifa, it is also said that it is for a single vague (one), suitable for each individual on the basis of substitution (badal). Some said that it is an exaggeration in ambiguity; by addition to a ma'rifa it does not acquire definiteness, like the word 'mithl' (like) is said regarding 'deyr'? And the author said 'it becomes general by a description' – by this description he does not mean the grammatical na't (adjective), but rather a semantic description (wasf ma'nawi), i.e., a meaning subsisting in something else, which includes the silla (relative clause) and condition (shart) etc. Because the sentence that occurs after it is a silla if it is a relative clause (ism mawsul?). If it is a conditional (shartiyya), then the sentence is called shart. According to grammarians, it is not a true adjective. So if he says: 'My slave, whoever strikes you, is free' – then in this case, if all slaves struck, whether collectively or separately or successively, all become free, because 'strikes' is a general description, so due to the generality of the description it includes all slaves. And if he says: 'My slave, you strike him, he is free' – i.e., he says: 'my slave whom you strike is free' – then only one becomes free, even if he struck all. If he struck all successively, then only the one he struck first becomes free. If he struck all at once without order, then only one becomes free, but the master has a choice as to which one to accept as free. They (the scholars) say regarding the difference between the two cases: in the first, the slave is described with the strikes which is general, so due to the generality of the description, 'my slaves' is general and includes all individuals, so all become free. In the second, the description is not from the word 'ayyu 'ibadi' but rather its relation is to the addressee, not to 'ayyu 'ibadi' which is indefinite. Because in the first case, the word 'ayyu 'ibadi' is described by the attribute of striking, and in the second case, this description is detached from it and related to the addressee. Therefore, in this case it is not general. And this difference is problematic from the grammatical perspective, because if by 'description' they mean the grammatical na't, then in neither of the two cases can there be a grammatical na't, because in both cases there is a relative pronoun (ism mawsul) or condition (shart), so the sentence after it becomes a silla or a condition, not a grammatical na't in either case. If by description they mean...
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the usul principle regarding the particle 'ayyu' (which/whoever) and its generality. The author establishes that 'ayyu' is inherently indefinite (nakira) because its primary function is to refer to a specific individual in a vague sense. It gains generality (umum) through a descriptive clause (sifa). This description is not the grammatical adjective (na't) but a semantic attribute that includes relative clauses (silla) and conditions (shart). The author then provides two contrasting examples of manumission (i'taq). In the first example: 'My slave, whoever strikes you is free' – the description 'strikes' is attributed to the slave (through the relative clause), making it general; hence all slaves who strike become free. In the second: 'My slave, you strike him, is free' – here the description is attached to the addressee's action, not to the indefinite 'ayyu 'ibadi', so it does not create generality; only one slave (the first struck or chosen by master) becomes free. The author then notes a grammatical difficulty: if 'description' were taken as grammatical na't, neither example would have a true na't because relative and conditional clauses are not adjectives. Thus the intended meaning is semantic description, not grammatical. This passage illustrates the intersection of usul al-fiqh and Arabic grammar (nahw) in interpreting texts.

Key Terms
الى (ayyu): A particle that is indefinite in origin, used for a vague individual, becomes general through a descriptive clause.
صفة (sifa): A semantic description that includes relative clauses and conditions, not limited to grammatical adjectives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master says: 'My slave, whoever strikes you is free.' If all his slaves strike the addressee, what happens according to the passage?
A) Only the first slave to strike becomes free.
B) All slaves become free because the description 'strikes' is general and includes all.
C) Only one slave, chosen by the master, becomes free.
D) None become free because the condition is not fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'ayyu' gains generality only through a semantic descriptive clause attached to it, not from itself.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

10 min
The Effect of 'Ayyu' in Conditional Manumission
Explaining the difference between two conditional manumission formulas using 'ayyu'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi linguistic principle regarding 'ayyu' to derive the ruling in conditional manumission and evaluate the grammatical objection.

Core Idea
Ayyu remains indefinite singular unless a general attribute (sifah 'ammah) extends its scope.

This lesson explores a classic uṣūlī problem: how does the word 'ayyu' function in conditional manumission? We examine two different formulas – one where the slave is described as beater, another where the addressee is the beater – and see how the attribute's connection determines whether all slaves or only one are freed. The discussion reveals deep interaction between Arabic grammar and legal reasoning in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two formulas
Locate the two Arabic sentences in the passage: one with 'ḍarbuka' (your beating) and one with 'ḍarabtahu' (you beat him). Note their translations.
First: 'ayyu 'abīdī ḍarbuka fahuwa ḥurrun' – 'Any of my slaves that beats you is free'. Second: 'ayyu 'abīdī ḍarabtahu fahuwa ḥurrun' – 'Whichever of my slaves you beat is free'.
2
Determine the rulings
From the passage, state what happens in each case. In the first, all slaves become free if all beat. In the second, only one becomes free, with the master's choice.
The ruling difference hinges on the scope of 'ayyu'.
3
Analyze the linguistic basis
Examine how the attribute (ṣifah) affects 'ayyu'. In the first, beating is a general attribute, making 'ayyu' general. In the second, the attribute is disconnected, so 'ayyu' remains indefinite singular.
The key is whether the attribute describes the slave or relates to the addressee.
4
Scrutinize the grammatical objection
Understand why taking 'ṣifah' as a grammatical adjective is problematic: the clause after 'ayyu' is a relative or conditional clause, not an adjective. The resolution is that 'ṣifah' means a semantic attribute.
The author offers a unique distinction: each slave individually qualifies in the first case because each can be the beater, so all are freed.
5
Extract the usul principle
State the principle: 'Ayyu' in conditional contexts denotes one indefinite individual, but if a general semantic attribute (meaning) attaches to it, it becomes general and includes all individuals that satisfy the attribute.
This principle applies to other similar constructs in fiqh.

وهذا الفرق مشكل من جهة النحو الم به فرق مذکور نحوی لحاظ سے مشکل کیونکہ اگر وصف سے مراد نعت نحومی ہے تو ان دونوں صورتوں میں سے کسی میں نعت نحوی نہیں ہو سکتی کیونکہ ان دونوں صورتوں میں اسی موصولہ ہے یا شرطیہ تو جملہ جو اس کے بعد ہے وہ صلہ بنے گا یا شرط نعت نحوی کسی صورت میں نہیں ہو سکتی اگر وصف سے مراد
نعت نحوی نہیں بلکہ وصف معنوی ہے تو یہ اپنی دونوں صورتوں میں موصوفہ ہے پہلی صورت میں اس کی وصف بالضاربة للمخاطب ہے اور دوسری صورت میں اس کی وصف بالمضروبیت للمخاطب ہے یعنی پہلی صورت میں اتنی عبیدی ضارب للمخاطب بنتا ہے دوسری میں مخاطب کا مضروب بنتا ہے تو سیلی صورت میں اس جملہ کو وصف قرار دینا اور دوسری صورت میں قطع عن الوصف کرنا یہ صرف تحکم ہے دھنا فرق آخر تفرد به المصنف وهو ان ايا لا يتناول الا الواحد المنكر یعنی لفظ استی در حقیقت واحد منکر کو شامل ہوتا ہے یعنی ایک فرد لا علی التعیین کو فی الاول پس پہلی صورت میں جب کہا اسی عبیدی ضریب فھو حرہ میں چونکہ اس واحد منکر کا عشق اس کی اپنی ضرب سے متعلق ہے یعنی وہ ضارب للمخاطب ہو اور دوسرے سے قطع نظر ہے خواہ وہ مارے یا نہ مارے اس کی طرف کوئی التفات نہیں ہے بلکہ اس سے قطع نظر ہے نیعتق کل واحد توجب تمام عبید نے مخاطب کو کو مارا تو اس وقت ہر ایک ایک غلام آزاد ہو جائیگا باعتباراته منفرد با عتبار اس لحاظ کے کہ وہ ہر ایک اپنی ضرب میں منفرد ہے اور دوسرے سے قطع نظر ہے اور اس کا معنی جو واحد منکر تھا وہ وحدۃ بھی برقرار ہے۔
ولو لم يثبت هذا یعنی اگر ہر ایک کا عتق ثابت نہ ہو بعض کیلئے ثابت ہو اور بعض کیلئے ثابت نہ ہو یہ ترجیح بلا مرچ ہے کیوں کہ اولو یہ کیسی میں نہیں ہے یا یہ کہ کوئی بھی ازاد نہ ہو تو اس صورت میں مبطل الكلام بالکلیہ کہ کلام من كل الوجوہ باطل ہو جائیگی کہ اس سے کچھ بھی ثابت نہ ہوا ں وفی الثانی اور دوسری صورت میں جب کہ اس نے کہا اسی عبیدی ضربته تو ثبت الواحد ويتيخر فيه الفاعل تو ایک کی حریت ثابت ہوگی اور اس میں فاعل مخاطب کو اختیار ہو گا اور فاعل مخاطب سے اس میں تخیر ممکن ہے پس جس کی ضرب کو مخاطب اختیار کرے گا تو وہی اولی بالعتق ہوگا کیونکہ یہاں مفاعیل یعنی مضربین متعدد ہیں لہذا مخاطب کے لئے کسی ایک کو مضروب بنانا ممکن ہے اور یہ اس کے اختیار میں ہے نخلاف صورة اولی کے
اس میں یہ تخییر ممکن نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس میں مفعول و مضروب ایک ہے اور فاعل وئی متعین نہیں ہے تخیر تو وہاں ممکن ہو سکتی ہے جہاں مضروب متعدد ہوں اور فاعل متعین تو اس صورت میں لا تعدد في المضروب فلا تخيير نحو ايتما اهاب ربع فقد ظهر هذا نظير الاول یعنی یہ پہلی صورۃ کی نظیر ہے جس میں فاعل کا تعین نہیں بلکہ اصحاب کی طہارہ کو فعل دباغتہ
Its nakirah is apparent, and if it is added to a definite, it is also said that it is for a vague single one, suitable for each one of the individuals on a substitutional basis. Some said that it is an exaggeration in ambiguity; with addition to the definite, it does not acquire definiteness, like the word 'mithl' regarding 'dar'. And the author said that it is general by the attribute; by 'sifah' he does not mean grammatical adjective, rather the semantic attribute, i.e., a meaning subsisting in another, which includes the relative clause and condition, etc., because the sentence that occurs after it is the relative clause if it is a relative pronoun; if it is conditional, then the sentence is called the condition; according to grammarians, it is not a real adjective. So if he says: 'Ayyu 'abīdī ḍarbuka nahu fahuwa ḥurrun' then in this case if all the slaves beat him, whether together or successively, all become free, because beating is a general attribute; therefore due to its generality it becomes general and includes all the slaves. And if he says: 'Ayyu 'abīdī ḍarabtahu fahuwa ḥurrun' i.e., if he says: 'Whichever of my slaves you beat, he is free,' then in this case only one becomes free, even if he beat all; if he beat them in sequence, only the one he beat first becomes free; if he beat all at once, only one becomes free, but the master has a choice as to whose freedom to accept. They said: Regarding the difference between the two cases, they say that in the first, the slave is described with beating, which is general, so due to the generality of the attribute, 'ayyu 'abīdī' is general and includes all individuals, hence all individuals become free. In the second case, the attribute is from the word 'ayyu 'abīdī' but rather its relation is to the addressee, not to 'ayyu 'abīdī' which is indefinite, because in the first case the word 'ayyu 'abīdī' is described as beating, and in the second case this attribute is disconnected from it and related to the addressee. Therefore in this case it is not general. And this difference is problematic from the grammatical perspective; because if by attribute is meant the grammatical adjective, then in neither of the two cases can there be a grammatical adjective, because in both cases it is a relative pronoun or conditional, so the sentence after it becomes the relative clause or condition; it cannot be a grammatical adjective in any case. If by attribute is meant not the grammatical adjective but the semantic attribute, then in both cases it is described: in the first case its description is 'beating the addressee', and in the second case its description is 'being beaten by the addressee'. So in the first case making that sentence an attribute, and in the second case disconnecting it from the attribute, is mere arbitrariness. Another difference uniquely held by the author is that 'ayyu' only encompasses the indefinite single one, i.e., the word 'ayyu' in reality includes one indefinite individual, i.e., one unspecified person. So in the first case, when he said 'ayyu 'abīdī ḍarbuka fahuwa ḥurrun', since the connection of that indefinite single one is to his own beating, i.e., he is the beater of the addressee, and disregard of others, whether he beats or not, there is no attention to that, rather disregard. So each one becomes free when all the slaves beat the addressee; at that time every single slave becomes free, considered individually, in the sense that each one is independent in his beating and disregard of others, and its meaning which was the indefinite single one remains as singularity. And if this is not established, i.e., if the freeing of each one is not established, but established for some and not for others, this is preference without a cause, because it is not in [؟] or that none becomes free, then in that case the speech is completely invalid, becoming entirely void, establishing nothing. In the second case, when he said 'ayyu 'abīdī ḍarabtahu', then one freedom is established, and the agent (the master) has a choice, and choice is possible for the addressee (master). So whichever beating the addressee chooses, that one is more deserving of freedom, because here the objects (i.e., those beaten) are multiple, so it is possible for the addressee to make any one the beaten, and that is in his choice, unlike the first case. In that, such choice is not possible, because in it the object (beaten) is one, and the agent is not specified. Choice is possible where the beaten are multiple and the agent is specified; in this case there is no multiplicity in the beaten, so no choice. For example: 'ayyumā ihābin dubigha' i.e., this is similar to the first case where the agent is not specified, rather the tanning of hides.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a detailed uṣūlī analysis of the word 'ayyu' (أي) when used in conditional manumission (ʿitq) statements. The author (al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah or similar) discusses two scenarios:

First scenario: The master says, 'Ayyu ʿabīdī ḍarbuka fahuwa ḥurrun' – 'Any of my slaves that beats you is free.' Here, the attribute (ṣifah) of beating is attached to the slave. Since beating is a general attribute (because any slave who beats triggers the condition), the word 'ayyu' becomes general, covering all slaves. Therefore, if all slaves beat the addressee, all become free.

Second scenario: The master says, 'Ayyu ʿabīdī ḍarabtahu fahuwa ḥurrun' – 'Whichever of my slaves you beat is free.' Here, the attribute (being beaten) is related to the addressee, not to the slave. The word 'ayyu' remains indefinite and singular, so only one slave becomes free, and the master may choose which one.

The commentator then addresses a grammatical objection: if 'ṣifah' is taken as a grammatical adjective, neither sentence qualifies because the clause after 'ayyu' is a relative clause or condition, not an adjective. So the intended 'ṣifah' is a semantic attribute (meaning-based), not grammatical. But even with that interpretation, the difference between the two cases seems arbitrary. To resolve this, the author offers another distinction: 'ayyu' in origin denotes a single indefinite individual. In the first case, since each slave individually can be the beater, each is considered independently, so all are freed. In the second case, the beating is done by the addressee to a single slave, so only one is freed, with the master's choice.

This lesson illustrates how uṣūl al-fiqh applies linguistic principles (ʿumūm, ṣifah, tanwīn) to derive nuanced fiqh rulings. The discussion reflects the Hanafi tradition's deep engagement with Arabic grammar and semantics in legal reasoning.

Key Terms
أي (ayyu): A word that denotes an indefinite single individual when used in conditional contexts, but can become general when associated with a general attribute.
صفة (ṣifah): Here, a semantic attribute (meaning) that qualifies the antecedent, not necessarily a grammatical adjective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second scenario ('ayyu 'abīdī ḍarabtahu'), why does only one slave become free?
A) Because 'ayyu' is a general word and the master can choose one.
B) Because the attribute of being beaten is related to the addressee, so 'ayyu' remains indefinite singular, allowing only one freedom with choice.
C) Because grammatically the clause is a relative clause, which restricts the meaning.
D) Because the beating is done by the master, so only one slave can be beaten at a time.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle: 'Ayyu' in conditional statements denotes one indefinite individual; its scope becomes general only when a general semantic attribute attaches to the subject, not when the attribute relates to an external agent.

Two Scenarios of 'Ayy' in Conditionals
Analyzing the legal effect of 'ayy' in two conditional statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two conditional constructions of 'ayy' and apply the Hanafi rule to determine whether the ruling is general or specific.

Core Idea
The legal scope of 'ayy' depends on whether the agent is specified: unspecified agent → general; specified agent → specific.

In Hanafi usul, the word 'ayy' is not always general. Its scope varies based on the syntactic construction. This lesson analyzes two conditional sentences: one where 'ayy' introduces a description of the agent, and one where it introduces the object. Understanding this distinction is key to correctly deriving rulings from such statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional constructions
Locate the two key sentences: 'ayy abidi daribun fahuwa hurr' (first scenario) and 'ayy abidi darabtuhu' (second scenario). Note the difference in verb form: first uses a verbal noun (daribun) as predicate, second uses a finite verb (darabtuhu) with an object.
The first describes the slave as striker; the second describes the slave as struck.
2
Observe the syntactic role of 'ayy'
In the first construction, 'ayy' functions as the subject (the doer of the striking). In the second, 'ayy' functions as the object (the one who is struck). This distinction is crucial.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule about 'ayy' as indefinite singular
Recognize that 'ayy' originally denotes one unspecified individual. The scope depends on whether the agent is specified: if the agent is unspecified (first scenario), each individual is considered independently, leading to generality. If the agent is specified (second scenario), the indefinite singular remains singular, giving the agent a choice among multiple objects.
This is the core insight of the passage.
4
Test with the examples of hide tanning and bread eating
Analyze the parallel examples: 'ayyuma ihabin dubigha' (first scenario parallel: no specific agent, general) and 'kullu ayyin khubzin turid' (second scenario parallel: agent specified, specific). Confirm that the same rule applies.
Exceptions for pig and human in hide tanning are due to other reasons, not the general nature of the construction.

It is not a grammatical attribute but a semantic description, so it is described in both forms: in the first form it is described as striking the addressee, and in the second form it is described as being struck by the addressee. That is, in the first form the slave becomes a striker of the addressee, in the second he becomes the struck of the addressee. So in the first form, considering this sentence as a description, and in the second form cutting off the description, this is mere arbitrariness. Another difference that the author uniquely mentioned is that 'ayy' does not include except the indefinite singular, i.e., the word 'ayy' in reality includes an indefinite singular, meaning one unspecified individual. Thus in the first form, when he said 'ayy abidi daribun fahuwa hurr', since this indefinite singular's connection is to his own striking, i.e., he is the striker of the addressee, and there is no regard for others whether they strike or not, rather it is disregarded. Therefore every single slave is freed when all the slaves strike the addressee, because each one is independent in his striking and the indefinite singular meaning of 'ayy' is preserved. And if this is not established, meaning if the manumission of each is not established, but it is established for some and not for others, this is a preference without preference, because it is not in the meaning, or if none is freed, then in that case the speech is completely invalid. And in the second form, when he said 'ayy abidi darabtuhu', then one is established, and the agent has choice. So one manumission is established, and the addressee has choice in the agent. So whichever strike the addressee chooses, that one is more deserving of manumission, because here the objects (the ones struck) are multiple, so it is possible for the addressee to make one of them the one struck, and that is in his choice, unlike the first form. In the first form, this choice is not possible, because the object (the one struck) is one, and the agent is not determined. Choice is only possible where the ones struck are multiple and the agent is determined. So in that case there is no multiplicity in the one struck, so no choice. Like the example: 'ayyuma ihabin dubigha faqad tahura' – this is analogous to the first form. That is, this is a parallel to the first scenario, in which the agent is not specified, but rather the purification of hides is suspended on the act of tanning. The agent of tanning is not specified such that choice could be exercised from the agent, so it is general. Therefore there is generality in it, and this ruling applies to every hide without specification, except the pig because it is impure by essence, and humans due to respect and honor are excluded from it. And like the example: 'kullu ayyin khubzin turid' – this is a parallel to the second scenario, where the agent is specified, i.e., the addressee, and the object, i.e., bread, is multiple. So for the addressee, it is not possible to eat every single one, but rather he may eat one bread. However, the addressee has choice as to which single bread he eats. This type of speech in custom is for concession, not for generality.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the word 'ayy' in two conditional constructions, demonstrating how its syntactic role determines legal scope. The author begins by dismissing a previous opinion that the difference lies in whether the sentence is descriptive or not, calling it arbitrary. Instead, he presents the real distinction: 'ayy' primarily denotes an indefinite singular (one unspecified individual). In the first scenario, 'ayy abidi daribun fahuwa hurr' (whichever of my slaves strikes [the addressee], he is free), the indefinite singular is linked to the slave's own act of striking the addressee. Since each slave can individually be the striker, if all strike, each is freed individually, preserving the singular meaning. In the second scenario, 'ayy abidi darabtuhu' (whichever of my slaves I struck), the object (the one struck) is singular but the agent (the addressee) has choice among multiple potential objects. Thus only the one chosen is freed. The author then illustrates with examples from tanning hides (parallel to first scenario: no specific agent, so general) and eating bread (parallel to second: specific agent, so specific). This analysis is crucial for understanding how generality and specificity are determined in conditional statements in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
أي (ayy): A word denoting an indefinite singular individual, used in conditionals to express a choice among multiple individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi analysis of 'ayy' in conditionals shows that its scope is determined by whether the agent is specified, leading to general or specific rulings.

The Four Usages of 'Man' and Generality
Explaining the four usages of 'man' and when it indicates generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four usages of the word 'man' and determine whether it indicates generality or specificity in each usage based on Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
'Man' is general when conditional or interrogative; it may be general or specific when relative or descriptive.

In this lesson, we explore the word 'man' as one of the words of generality (alfaz al-umum) in Hanafi usul. The author lists four distinct usages of 'man': conditional, interrogative, relative, and descriptive. Understanding how each usage affects the scope of generality is crucial for correctly interpreting Quranic verses and Prophetic statements that contain this word.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four usages
List the four ways 'man' is used: conditional (shartiyya), interrogative (istifhamiyya), relative (mawsula), and descriptive (mawsufa).
The first two always indicate generality; the latter two are ambiguous.
2
Distinguish between general and specific usages
For conditional and interrogative, 'man' is always general. For relative and descriptive, it can be either general or specific depending on context.
The passage provides a Quranic example where 'man' is specific (referring to specific hypocrites).
3
Apply the rule to an example
Given a conditional statement like 'man dakhala dar Abi Sufyan fa-...', note that 'man' is general for all rational beings who enter the house.
This is the principle: conditional 'man' is general for rational beings.
4
Understand the exception
When 'man' is relative or descriptive, it may refer to a specific subset, as in the Quranic verse about hypocrites.
The context determines whether it is general or specific.

And among them is 'man', and it occurs as specific. The word 'man' is among the words of generality, but it also occurs as specific; at that time it does not have generality. Actually, the usage of the word 'man' is in four ways: first, conditional; second, interrogative; in both these forms it is for generality. For example, 'man ja'ani falahu dirham' – its meaning is: if Zayd comes to me, then for him this; if Amr comes to me, then for him that, up to all individuals. And like 'man fi al-dar' – this is interrogative, its meaning is: is Zayd in the house or Amr? and so on. Third, relative; fourth, descriptive; and these two are sometimes for generality and sometimes for specificity, i.e., intending some individuals. Among these four forms, it is for rational beings. So the author gave an example of specificity: 'wa minhum man yastami'una ilayka wa minhum man yanzuru ilayk'. In this verse, 'man' is either relative or descriptive. The following sentence is its clause or description. Since 'man' is singular in form but plural in meaning, the plural pronoun in 'yastami'una' refers back to it, while the singular pronoun from 'yanzuru' refers back to it as well. Because the meaning of this word 'man' is some specific hypocrites. And it occurs as general for rational beings when it is conditional, i.e., if 'man' is used in the sense of condition, then at that time it is general for rational beings, encompassing all that fall under it. And the ruling attached to it applies to everyone. For example, 'man dakhala dar Abi Sufyan fa-...' – here 'man' is conditional, which is for generality, and whoever enters…

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the word 'man' as one of the words of generality. The author lists four usages: conditional (shartiyya), interrogative (istifhamiyya), relative (mawsula), and descriptive (mawsufa). The first two always indicate generality, while the latter two can be either general or specific. The author cites Quranic verses (from Surah al-Tawba) as examples of specific usage, where 'man' refers to specific hypocrites. He then states the principle: when 'man' is conditional, it is general for rational beings. This is a foundational rule in Hanafi usul for understanding the scope of conditional statements involving rational individuals.

Key Terms
من (man): A word used for rational beings; it is general when conditional or interrogative, and may be general or specific when relative or descriptive.

Types of 'Man' and Its Generality in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the word 'man' as a general term and its specific usages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four usages of 'man' and distinguish when it indicates generality vs particularity, and apply the Hanafi rule that 'min' defaults to partition (tab'id) when the object is divisible.

Core Idea
The word 'man' is not always general; its meaning depends on its grammatical function and context.

This passage examines the word 'man' as one of the general terms in Arabic, crucial for usul al-fiqh. You will learn its four grammatical forms and how each affects the scope of a ruling. The key legal example of freeing slaves illustrates a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Two Companions over the meaning of the particle 'min'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four usages of 'man'
The text states that 'man' is used in four ways: shartiyya (conditional), istifhamiyya (interrogative), mawsula (relative), and mawsufa (descriptive).
2
Recognize always-general usages
'Man' as shartiyya and istifhamiyya always indicate generality. For example, 'man ja'ani falahu darahim' (whoever comes to me has dirhams) includes all individuals.
3
Understand context-dependent usages
'Man' as mawsula or mawsufa can be general or specific depending on context (qarina). The Quranic example 'wa minhum man yastami'una ilayka' refers to specific hypocrites, showing particularity.
4
Analyze the dispute over 'min' in slave manumission
In 'ni man shi'ta min 'abidi 'itqahu', the dispute is whether 'min' indicates partition (tab'id) or clarification (bayan). Abu Hanifa says tab'id, so not all slaves are freed; the Sahibayn say bayan, so all are freed.
The Hanafi position relies on the default rule: 'min' with divisible objects is for partition unless a qarina indicates otherwise.
5
Apply the principle of certainty over probability
Abu Hanifa argues that partiality (some slaves) is certain, while generality (all slaves) is only probable. In usul, certainty (yaqin) takes precedence over probability (zann).

کے ساتھ معلق کیا گیا فاعل دباغت کی تعیین نہیں ہے جس سے تخیر ہو سکے تخییر من الفاعلي نہ ہوئی تو یہ عام ہو گا لہذا اس کے اندر عموم ہے ہراھاب کو یہ حکم شامل ہو جائیگا بغیر کسی تخصیص کے مگر خنزیر نجس العین ہونے کی وجہ سے اور انسان بوجہ اکرام و تکریم کے اس سے مستثنیٰ ہوں گے ۔ دنحو کل اتنی خبز ترید یہ ثانی صورت کی نظیر ہے کہ فاعل متعین ہے یعنی مخاطب اور مفعول یعنی خیز متعدد ہے لہذا فاعل مخاطب کے لئے قلا تمكن اكل كل واحد یعنی مجموعے میں سے ہر ایک شیر کو کھانے پر اسے تمکن حاصل نہیں ہوگا بل اکل واحد بلکہ ایک روٹی کھا نیکی اس کو اجازت ہوگی البتہ مخاطب کو اس میں اختیار ہے جس ایک روٹی کو کھائے ویسے اس قسم کی کلام عرف میں تسخیر کے لئے ہوتی ہے عموم کے لئے نہیں ہوتی ۔
ومنها من وهو يقع خاصا اور الفاظ عام میں سے کلمہ من ہے اور وہ خاص بھی واقع ہوتا ہے اس وقت اس کے اندر عموم نہیں ہوگا دراصل لفظ من کی استعمال چار طریقہ پر ہوتی ایک شرطیہ دوسرا استضامیہ ان دونوں صورتوں میں عموم کے لئے ہے جیسے من جامنی فلہ درهم معنی اس کا یہ ہے ان جانی زید و ان جامنی عمر دو بكذا الى جميع الافراد اور جیسے من فی الدار یہ استفہامیہ ہے معنی اس کا یہ ہے اُزید فی الدار ام عمره والى غير ذلك تیسرے موصولہ اور چوتھا موصوفہ ہے اور یہ دونوں کبھی عموم کیلئے اور کبھی خصوص کے لئے یعنی بعض افراد کا ارادہ ہو گا ان صور اربعہ میں ہوگا ذوی العقول لئے مصنف نے خصوص کی مثال پیش کرتے ہوئے کہا ومنهم من يستمعون اليك ومنهم من ینظر الیک اس آیت کے اندر من موصولہ ہے یا موصوفہ اگلا جملہ اس کا صلہ ہے یا صفہ من چونکہ لفظا مفرد ہے اور معنی جمع تو اس لئے یستمعون کی ضمیر جمع کا مرجع بن رہا ہے اور منظر سے ضمیر واحد اس کی طرف راجع ہے۔ فان المراد کیونکہ اس کلمہ من سے مراد بعض مخصوص منافقین ہیں ويقع عامانی العقلا اذا كان للشرط یعنی من اگر بمعنى الشرط استعمال ہو یعنی شرطیہ ہو تو اس وقت عام ہوگا عقلا میں یعنی ذوی العقول کو عام اور شامل ہوگا تمام اس کے تحت درج ہوں گے اور جو حکم اس کے ساتھ وابستہ کیا گیا ہے وہ ہر ایک کو شامل ہوگا نحو من دخل دار ابی سفیان الا تو اس کے اندر من شرطیہ ہے جو عموم کے لئے ہے اور جو شخص بھی
حول اختیار کرے گا خواہ انفراد ا ہو یا اجتماعاً ہر داخل ہونیوالا ذی امن ہو جائیگا۔ فان قال من شاء من عبيدى عتقه فهو حر فشاوا یعنی تمام نے اپنا اپنا عشق چاہا تو جمیع ازاد ہو جائیں گے ۔ کیونکہ اس کے اندر مشیہ کی اضافہ و نسبتہ کلمہ من کی طرف کی گئی جو عموم کے لئے ہے اور مین عبیدی میں من جاره برائے تبیین و بیان ہے لہذا جب تمام نے چاہا تمام ازاد ہو جائیں گے ۔ ونی من شئت من عبيدى عتقه فاعتقه الا تو اس مخاطب نے تمام عبید کے عشق کو چاہا تو صاحبین کے نزدیک تمام ازاد ہو جائیں گے عملاً بكلمة العموم یعنی کلمه من برائے عموم ہے اس پر عمل کرتے ہوئے جمیع عبید جب کہ مخاطب نے ان کے عشق کو چاہا تو ازاد ہو جائیں گے اور من جاره صاحبین کے نزدیک برائے تبیین و بیان ہوگا وعند ابي حنيفة تمام کو ازاد کر سکتا ہے مگر ان میں سے ایک غلام کو عشق سے خارج کرنا ضروری ہے یعنی ایک کے سوا تمام کو ازاد کر سکتا ہے اگر عتق علی الترتیب واقع کیا ہے تو آخری کے سوا سب ازاد ہو جائیں گے اگر تمام کو مجتمعا ازاد کیا ہے تو پھر مولی کو خیار ہے کہ جس ایک کو برقیہ میں باقی رکھے۔ لان من للتبعیض یہ امام کی دلیل ہے کہ جمیع ازاد نہیں ہوں گے بلکہ کم از کم ایک کو باقی رکھنا ضروری ہے خلاصہ دلیل کا یہ ہے کہ یہ من جاره برائے تبعیض ہے اور ضابطہ یہی ہے کہ جب من کا مجرور ذی العاض وذی اجزاء ہو تو شائع ذائع یہی کہ مین تبعیضہ ہوتا ہے اور برائے تبیین نہیں ہوتا الا یہ کہ کوئی قرینہ موجود ہو جو عموم کی تاکید کرے اور بیان و تبیین کو ترجیح دے لیکن بلا قرینہ تبعیض کے لئے ہوتا ہے کمافی کل من هذا الخبز چونکہ خبر دی اجزا ہو سکتی ہے اس لئے یہاں من تبعیضہ ہوگا ۔ ولانہ متیقن یہ دوسری وجہ ہے کہ من برائے تبعیض ہے کیونکہ بعض کا مراد ہونا متیقن ہے اور کل کا مراد ہونا محتمل ہے لان میں اذا كان للتبعيض كيونكہ من جب تبعيض کیونکہ کے لئے ہو تو پھر یقینا بعض ہی مراد ہو اکل کے مراد ہونے کی کوئی گنجائش نہیں ہے. وان كان للبيان اگر من بیانیہ ہے تو پھر بھی بضمن کل بعض تو ضرور مراد ہوا بہر صورت بعض کا مراد ہونا یقینی ہوا اور کل کا مراد ہونا محتمل ہے لہذا احتمالی کو ترک کرکے یقینی امر و مراد لینا بہتر اور اولی ہے لہذا بعض غلاموں کو وہ مخاطب ازاد کر سکتا ہے کیونکہ رعایت عموم اور رعایت تبعیض دونوں اس میں میں کہ جمیع کو ازاد کرے مگر
With which the subject is attached, the subject of tanning is not determined such that choice could be made; since there is no specification of the subject, it will be general. Therefore, there is generality in it, and this ruling will include every hide without any specification, except the pig due to being inherently impure (najis al-ayn) and the human due to respect and honor. So, for example, 'How many loaves do you want?' This is a case of the second type: the subject is determined (i.e., the addressee) and the object (i.e., the bread) is multiple; therefore, the addressee does not have the ability to eat each one, i.e., he does not have the ability to eat every single loaf from the total, but rather he is permitted to eat one loaf. However, the addressee has the choice of which one loaf to eat. Such speech in custom is for giving choice, not for generality.

And among them (general words) is 'man' (من), and it occurs as specific. Among the general words is the word 'man', and it also occurs as specific; at that time there is no generality in it. In fact, the usage of 'man' is in four ways: one is conditional (shartiyya), the second is interrogative (istifhamiyya); in both these cases, it is for generality, like 'man ja'ani falahu dirham' (whoever comes to me has a dirham), its meaning is: if Zayd comes, and if 'Amr comes, and so on up to all individuals. And like 'man fi al-dar?' (who is in the house?) – this is interrogative; its meaning is: is Zayd in the house or 'Amr, and so on. The third is relative (mawsula), and the fourth is descriptive (mawsufa); and these two are sometimes for generality and sometimes for particularity, i.e., intending some individuals. Among these four types, it is for rational beings. So the author gave an example of particularity, saying: 'And among them are those who listen to you, and among them are those who look at you' (Qur'an). In this verse, 'man' is relative or descriptive; the following clause is its relative clause (sila) or attribute (sifa). Since 'man' is singular in form but plural in meaning, the plural pronoun in 'yastami'una' refers to it, and the singular pronoun in 'yanzuru' refers to 'man' as well. Because the intended meaning of this word 'man' is some specific hypocrites. And it occurs as general for rational beings when it is for a condition. That is, if 'man' is used in the meaning of a condition (shart), i.e., it is conditional, then it will be general for rational beings, i.e., it will include all rational beings; all will be included under it, and the ruling attached to it will include everyone. For example, 'man dakhala dara Abi Sufyan fa-huwa amin' (whoever enters the house of Abu Sufyan is safe). In this, 'man' is conditional, which is for generality, and whoever enters, whether individually or collectively, every entrant will be safe.

If someone says: 'man sha'a min 'abidi 'itqahu fa-huwa hurr' (whoever among my slaves wishes his manumission is free), and they all wished, then all become free, because in it, the attribution of wishing (mashi'a) is to the word 'man', which is for generality, and in 'min 'abidi' the particle 'min' is for clarification (li al-tabyin/bayan). Therefore, when all wished, all become free. And if he says: 'ni man shi'ta min 'abidi 'itqahu fa-atqahu' (whoever you wish among my slaves, free him), and the addressee wished the freeing of all slaves, then according to the Two Companions (Sahibayn), all become free, acting upon the word of generality, i.e., the word 'man' is for generality, so all slaves when the addressee wishes their freeing become free, and the particle 'min' according to the Sahibayn is for clarification. According to Abu Hanifa, he can free all but it is necessary to exclude one slave from the wish, i.e., he can free all except one. If he freed them sequentially, all but the last become free; if he freed them all together, then the master has the choice to keep whichever one he wishes in slavery. Because 'min' is for partition (tab'id). This is Imam's proof: that not all become free; rather, at least one must be kept. The summary of the proof is that this particle 'min' is for partition, and the rule is that when the object of 'min' is a possessor of parts or components, the prevalent and widespread opinion is that 'min' is for partition, and not for clarification unless there is a context (qarina) that reinforces generality and prefers clarification. But without a qarina, it is for partition, as in 'min hadha al-khubz' (of this bread). Since bread can be divided into parts, here 'min' will be for partition. And because it is certain: this is the second reason that 'min' is for partition, because intending some is certain, whereas intending all is probable. Because when 'min' is for partition, then certainly only some are intended; there is no possibility of intending all. And if it is for clarification, then implied in all, some are necessarily intended; in any case, intending some is certain, and intending all is probable. Therefore, leaving the probable and taking the certain is better and more appropriate. Thus, the addressee can free some slaves, because observing generality and observing partition both are that he frees all? But...

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis from a Hanafi usul commentary discussing the word 'man' (من) as one of the general terms (alfaz al-'amm). It begins by contrasting two situations: one where the subject is indeterminate leading to generality (like tanning hides), and another where the subject is determined but the object is multiple, giving choice not generality. Then it focuses on 'man', explaining its four usages: conditional (shartiyya), interrogative (istifhamiyya) – both always general; and relative (mawsula) and descriptive (mawsufa) – which can be general or particular. The Qur'anic example 'among them are those who listen to you' is cited to show particularity, referring to specific hypocrites. Then it states that 'man' when conditional is general for rational beings, citing the hadith of Abu Sufyan's house. The key legal example is the freeing of slaves: if a master says 'whoever among my slaves wishes to be free is free', then if all wish, all are free (generality). But if he says 'free whoever you wish among my slaves', there is a dispute: the Two Companions (Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold that 'man' here is general, so if the agent wishes all, all are free; Abu Hanifa holds that 'min' in 'min 'abidi' is for partition (tab'id), so at least one slave must remain enslaved. His proof: 'min' when the object is divisible is for partition, not clarification unless there is a context. Also, partiality is certain, generality is only probable. Therefore, a probable interpretation cannot override a certain one.

Key Terms
من شرطية (man shartiyya): The word 'man' used as a conditional particle, always indicating generality for rational beings.
من استفهامية (man istifhamiyya): The word 'man' used as an interrogative particle, also general.
من موصولة (man mawsula): The word 'man' used as a relative pronoun, sometimes general, sometimes specific.
من موصوفة (man mawsufa): The word 'man' used as a described noun, similar to mawsula.
تبعيض (tab'id): Partition, indicating that only part of the whole is intended.
بيان (bayan): Clarification, indicating that the particle 'min' specifies the general term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a master says 'ni man shi'ta min 'abidi 'itqahu' (free whoever you wish among my slaves), and the addressee wishes to free all, what is the ruling?
A) All slaves are freed.
B) None are freed because the master's utterance is invalid.
C) At least one slave remains in slavery; the rest are freed.
D) The master must decide which slaves to free.

Key Takeaway
The word 'man' and particle 'min' require careful analysis of grammar and context; in Hanafi usul, certainty of partiality takes precedence over probability of generality.

The Particle 'min' Between Tab'eed and Bayan in Conditional Emancipation
Textual analysis of 'min' as tab'eed or bayan in conditional emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that 'min' defaults to tab'eed when its genitive has parts, and derive the ruling for conditional emancipation formulas, distinguishing between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions.

Core Idea
'min' defaults to partiality unless a contextual indicator shifts it to explanation.

This passage dives into a classic Hanafi usul al-fiqh problem: the function of the particle 'min' in conditional statements. Two similar expressions for conditional emancipation yield different rulings based on whether 'min' is taken as tab'eed (indicating part of a whole) or bayan (explaining the genus). Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differ on this point, and the reasoning reveals a core Hanafi principle about certainty and contextual indicators.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two expressions
Locate the two key formulas: 'man shaa min 'abeedi 'itquhu fahuwa hurr' (whoever among my slaves wishes, he is free) and 'man shi'ta min 'abeedi 'itquhu fa-a'tiqhu' (whomever of my slaves you wish, free him). Note the difference in who exercises the will.
The first attributes will to each slave; the second attributes will to the master.
2
Classify the particle 'min'
Determine whether 'min' in 'min 'abeedi' is for tab'eed (partiality) or bayan (explanation). Recall the Hanafi default: when the genitive (مجرور) has parts or individuals, 'min' is for tab'eed unless a contextual indicator (قرينہ) suggests otherwise.
The genitive 'abeedi (my slaves) clearly has individuals.
3
Apply the default rule
Without a qareenah, 'min' is tab'eed. For Abu Hanifa, this means that in the first expression, each slave's will is considered individually, so all who wish become free, preserving partiality (each is a part). In the second, the master's will is general; freeing all would eliminate partiality, so at least one must remain.
The Sahibayn view 'min' as bayan, making the expression general and all are freed.
4
Evaluate the certainty argument
Analyze the reasoning: certainty (يقين) that some are intended exists in both interpretations, but intending all is only probable. The Hanafi principle prefers certainty over probability, supporting tab'eed.
This is a talil (reasoning) from the source.
5
Extract the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: in the second expression, Abu Hanifa rules that the master can free all but one; the Sahibayn rule that all become free. This shows how a usul difference affects actual fiqh.

مشيہ کی اضافہ نسبتہ کلمہ من کی طرف کی گئی جو عموم کے لئے ہے اور في قوله من عبيدي من جاره برائے تبيين و بيان ہے لہذا جب تمام نے چاہا تمام ازاد ہو جائیں گے. واما من شئت من عبيدى عتقه فاعتقه الا تو اس مخاطب نے تمام عبيد کے عشق کو چاہا تو صاحبين کے نزديك تمام ازاد ہو جائیں گے عملاً بكلمة العموم يعني كلمه من برائے عموم ہے اس پر عمل كرتے ہوئے جمیع عبيد جب كہ مخاطب نے ان كے عشق كو چاہا تو ازاد ہو جائیں گے اور من جاره صاحبين كے نزديك برائے تبيين و بيان ہوگا وعند ابي حنيفة تمام كو ازاد كر سكتا ہے مگر ان میں سے ايك غلام كو عتق سے خارج كرنا ضرورى ہے يعني ايك كے سوا تمام كو ازاد كر سكتا ہے اگر عتق علی الترتيب واقع كيا ہے تو آخری كے سوا سب ازاد ہو جائیں گے اگر تمام كو مجتمعا ازاد كيا ہے تو پھر مولی كو خيار ہے كہ جس ايك كو بقيه میں باقی ركھے. لان من للتبعيض يہ امام كی دليل ہے كہ جمیع ازاد نہيں ہوں گے بلکہ كم از كم ايك كو باقی ركھنا ضروری ہے خلاصہ دليل كا يہ ہے كہ يہ من جاره برائے تبعيض ہے اور ضابطہ يہی ہے كہ جب من كا مجرور ذی العاض و ذی اجزاء ہو تو شائع ذائع يہی كہ من تبعيضہ ہوتا ہے اور برائے تبيين نہيں ہوتا الا يہ كہ كوئی قرينہ موجود ہو جو عموم كی تاكيد كرے اور بيان و تبيين كو ترجيح دے ليكن بلا قرينہ تبعيض كے لئے ہوتا ہے كما في كل من هذا الخبز چونكہ خبز کے اجزا ہو سكتے ہيں اس لئے يہاں من تبعيضہ ہوگا. ولانہ متيقن يہ دوسری وجہ ہے كہ من برائے تبعيض ہے كيونكہ بعض كا مراد ہونا متيقن ہے اور كل كا مراد ہونا محتمل ہے لان من اذا كان للتبعيض كيونكہ من جب تبعيض كے لئے ہو تو پھر يقينا بعض ہی مراد ہو اور كل كے مراد ہونے كی كوئی گنجائش نہيں ہے. وان كان للبيان اگر من بيانيه ہے تو پھر بھی بضمن كل بعض تو ضرور مراد ہوا بہر صورت بعض كا مراد ہونا يقينی ہوا اور كل كا مراد ہونا محتمل ہے لہذا احتمالی كو ترك كركے يقينی امر كو مراد لينا بہتر اور اولی ہے لہذا بعض غلاموں كو وہ مخاطب ازاد كر سكتا ہے كيونكہ رعايت عموم اور رعايت تبعيض دونوں اس میں ہيں كہ جمیع كو ازاد كرے مگر ايك كو ضرور باقی ركھے. وفی المسئلة الاولی يعنی جب كہا من شاء من عبيدی عتقه فھو حر میں بھی ان دونوں امور كی رعايت كی گئی ہے كيونكہ ہر ايك غلام كا عتق اس كی اپنی ہی مشيہ سے معلق ہے اور غير سے قطع نظر ہے كہ وہ چاہے يا نہ چاہے اس كا كوئی اعتبار نہيں. فكل واحد بهذا يعنی ہر ايك اس اعتبار سے يعنی باعتبار قطع النظر عن الغير بعض من المجموع ہے فیعتق كل واحد پس ہر ايك غلام آزاد ہو جائيگا اور رعايت تبعيض بھی اس میں برقرار رہے گی. بخلاف من شئت كيونكہ اس صورت میں صاحب مشيہ تو صرف مخاطب ہے اگر وہ عتق كل كو مجتمعا چاہتا ہے اور كل ازاد ہو جائيں تو معنی تبعيض اس صورت میں بالکل باطل ہو جائيگا اس لئے كل كی ازادگی نہيں ہو سكتی كم از كم ايك عبد كو بصورت عبد باقی ركھنا ضروری ہے تاكہ معنی تبعيض باقی رہے. يہ فرق اور لفظ اسی میں آخری فرق يہ دونوں مصنف كے تفردات میں سے ہیں.
Concerning the choice, whether individually or collectively, every one who enters becomes secure. So if he says, 'Whoever among my slaves wishes, he is free,' and they all wish, then all become free. Because the attribution of the will is made to the word 'man' which is for generality, and in 'min 'abeedi' the 'min' is for clarification and explanation. Therefore, when all wished, all become free. And as for 'Whomever of my slaves you wish, he is free,' and he (the addressee) wished the freedom of all slaves, then according to the Sahibayn all become free, acting upon the word of generality, meaning 'man' is for generality, so all slaves become free when the addressee wished their freedom, and 'min' in its genitive case according to the Sahibayn is for clarification and explanation. But according to Abu Hanifa, he can free all except it is necessary to exclude one slave from the emancipation, meaning he can free all but one. If the emancipation is done sequentially, then all except the last become free; if he frees all collectively, then the master has the choice to keep any one in remaining. Because 'min' is for partiality—this is the Imam's proof that not all become free, but at least one must be kept. The summary of the proof is that this 'min' in its genitive case is for partiality, and the rule is that when the genitive of 'min' is something that has parts or individuals, then the common and widespread [view] is that 'min' is for partiality and not for clarification, unless there is a contextual indicator that reinforces generality and gives preference to clarification and explanation. But without such indicator, it is for partiality, as in 'every one of this bread' because bread can have parts, so here 'min' is for partiality. And because it is certain—this is the second reason that 'min' is for partiality because intending some is certain while intending all is probable. Because 'min' when it is for partiality, then certainly only some is intended, and there is no room for intending all. And if it is for clarification, then even then, by implication, some is necessarily intended; in any case, intending some is certain and intending all is probable. Therefore, leaving the probable and taking the certain is better and superior. Therefore, that addressee can free some slaves because regard for generality and regard for partiality both are present in that he should free all but must keep one. And in the first issue, i.e., when he says 'Whoever among my slaves wishes, he is free,' both these matters are also observed because the emancipation of each slave is suspended on his own will, regardless of others; whether he wishes or not, it has no consideration. So each one is, from this perspective, i.e., from the perspective of disregarding others, a part of the whole, so each one becomes free. And regard for partiality also remains in this. Contrary to the case of 'Whomever you wish' because here the possessor of will is only the addressee; if he intends the collective emancipation of all and all become free, then the meaning of partiality becomes completely invalid. Therefore, total freedom cannot occur; at least one slave must be kept as a slave so that the meaning of partiality remains. This difference and the last difference in the word itself are both among the unique points of the author.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the Arabic particle 'min' as used in conditional emancipation formulas. The author examines two expressions: (1) 'man shaa min 'abeedi 'itquhu fahuwa hurr' (whoever among my slaves wishes, he is free) and (2) 'man shi'ta min 'abeedi 'itquhu fa-a'tiqhu' (whomever of my slaves you wish, free him). The core issue is whether the particle 'min' in the genitive construction 'min 'abeedi' indicates partiality (tab'eed) or serves as explanation/elucidation (bayan). 

Imam Abu Hanifa holds that 'min' here is for tab'eed, meaning that not every individual slave is included; at least one must remain excluded. In the first expression, since the will is attributed to each slave individually, each slave who wishes becomes free, and the partiality is observed by each being a part. In the second expression, the will is solely the master's; if he intends all slaves collectively, the partiality would be lost, so he must exclude at least one. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) view 'min' as bayaniyyah, indicating the genus, so the expression is general and all slaves are freed when the condition is met.

The author then provides the reasoning: the default rule is that when the object of 'min' has parts or individuals, 'min' is for tab'eed unless there is a contextual indicator (qareenah) supporting generality. The rationale is that certainty (yaqeen) lies in partiality (some are intended), while all being intended is only probable, and certainty takes precedence. This principle is illustrated with the example 'kull min hadha al-khubz' (every one of this bread), where 'min' is partial because bread can be divided.

The passage concludes by contrasting the two expressions, showing how both considerations (generality and partiality) are maintained in the first but require an exclusion in the second.

Key Terms
تبعيض (tab'eed): The function of 'min' indicating that part of the whole is intended, not all.
بيان (bayan): The function of 'min' to explain or specify the genus of the majroor.
قرينہ (qareenah): A contextual indicator that can change the default interpretation of 'min' from tab'eed to bayan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling when a master says, 'Whomever of my slaves you wish, free him,' and the addressee wishes freedom for all?
A) All slaves become free.
B) The master must free all but one slave.
C) Only one slave is freed.
D) No slave is freed.

Key Takeaway
The default Hanafi rule for 'min' is tab'eed when its genitive has parts; only a contextual indicator (qareenah) can shift it to bayan, as applied in conditional emancipation formulas.

The Particle 'ma' as a General Term (Amm) in Hanafi Usul
Analysis of 'ma' as a general term with a practical application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether 'ma' is used literally or figuratively in a given context and apply the principle of generality ('umoom) to derive a ruling.

Core Idea
The particle 'ma' is literally general for non-rationals; its use for rationals is figurative, and when used generally, it covers every individual in the category.

This lesson examines the Arabic particle 'ma' (ما) as one of the general words (alfaz al-amm) in Hanafi usul. Understanding the literal and figurative domains of such words is crucial for correct legal interpretation. We will see how 'ma' functions and apply it to a concrete case from classical fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the text and its subject
Recognize that the passage classifies 'ma' as a general word (min alfaaz al-'aam) and distinguishes its literal and figurative uses.
The passage states: 'wa minhaa maa li ghayr al-'uqalaa' — and among them [general words] is 'ma' for non-rationals.
2
Distinguish literal vs. figurative usage
Understand that 'ma' is haqiqah (literal) when used for non-rational beings and majaz (figurative) when used for rational beings.
Literal usage is the default; figurative usage requires a contextual indicator (qareenah).
3
Apply the principle of umum (generality)
When 'ma' is used in a general context, it covers all individuals of the category. The passage illustrates this with a condition: 'If what is in your womb is a male, you are free.'
The word 'ma' implies 'everything that is in your womb' (kull ma fi batnaki).
4
Work through the example
The slave-girl gives birth to twins—a male and a female. Since 'ma' requires all contents to be male, the condition is not fully met.
Only part of the womb's content is male, not the entirety.
5
Derive the ruling
Because the condition is not completely fulfilled, the slave-girl is not freed. This shows the legal effect of generality.
The ruling is: 'fahiya laa tu'taqu' — she is not freed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما لغير العقلاء اور ان الفاظ عام میں سے لفظ ما ہے جو غیر عقلا میں استعمال ہوتا ہے وقد يستعار اور کبھی اس کا استعارہ عقل کے لئے بھی کر لیا جاتا ہے لیکن یہ مجاز ہے اور استعمال في غیر العقلاء حقیقہ ہے. وان قال ان کان ما في بطنك غلاما الخ يہ استعمال في العقلاء کی مثال ہے اگر اپنی لونڈی کو کہتا ہے کہ اگر ما في بطنك غلام ہے تو فانت حرة فولدت اس کے بطن سے ایک بچہ اور ایک بچی پیدا ہوئی تو اس صورت میں وہ لونڈی آزاد نہیں ہوگی لان المراد يعني چونکہ ما عموم کے لئے ہے اس لئے مراد کل ما في بطنك ہوگا یعنی جمیع ما في بطنك تو صورت مذکورہ میں جمیع ما في البطن غلام نہیں ہے بلکہ بعض ما في البطن غلام ہے بعض ما في البطن جاریہ ہے اس لئے شرط حریت پوری نہیں ہوتی لہذا آزاد نہیں ہوگی.
And among them [the general words] is 'ma' for non-rational beings. It is one of the general words, used for non-rational beings, and sometimes it is used metaphorically for rational beings, but that is figurative, and its use for non-rational beings is literal. And if he says, 'If what is in your womb is a male, then you are free,' and she gives birth to a male and a female, then in that case the slave-girl is not freed, because the intended meaning is that since 'ma' is for generality, the intended meaning is everything that is in your womb, i.e., all that is in your womb. In the mentioned case, all that is in the womb is not a male; rather, some of what is in the womb is a male and some is a female. Therefore, the condition of freedom is not fulfilled, so she is not freed.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the Arabic word 'ma' as a general term (min alfaaz al-'aam). The author states that 'ma' is primarily used for non-rational beings (ghayr al-'uqalaa') and its use for rational beings is metaphorical (majaaz). An example is given: a man says to his slave-girl, 'If what (ma) is in your womb is a male, then you are free.' She gives birth to a male and a female. The ruling is that she is not freed because 'ma' indicates generality (umoom), meaning all that is in the womb must be male. Since not all the contents are male, the condition is not fully met. This demonstrates the principle that general words must be applied to all individuals of the category they cover.

Key Terms
ما (ma): A general word (min alfaaz al-'aam) primarily used for non-rational beings; its use for rationals is metaphorical.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal usage: 'ma' for non-rational beings is haqiqah.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative usage: using 'ma' for rational beings is majaz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The particle 'ma' is a general term whose literal domain is non-rationals; when used generally, it applies to all individuals of the category, and failure to meet the full condition renders the condition void.

The Particle 'min' and Its Impact on Delegated Divorce: A Hanafi Usul Dispute
Usul dispute over the particle 'min' in divorce delegation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle regarding the particle 'min' to determine the number of divorces in a delegation case, and evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The particle 'min' defaults to tab'eed (partiality) unless a qareenah indicates bayan (explanation) — this changes the number of divorces allowed.

This passage examines a classic usul dispute: when a husband delegates divorce by saying 'Divorce yourself from three,' does the wife have the right to all three divorces or only some? The answer depends on the interpretation of the particle 'min'. Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differ on this, with each side drawing on a consistent approach applied across different legal contexts. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa: the wife may give only one or two divorces, not three. This is because 'min' here indicates partiality (tab'eed).
The total number of prescribed divorces is three.
2
Identify the opposing view
Identify the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani): the wife may give all three divorces because 'min' is for explanation (bayan).
They interpret 'min' as clarifying the category.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Sahibayn's argument: 'min' is bayaniyyah, so the phrase means 'divorce yourself from the set of three' — i.e., the entire set.
This is the same reasoning they used in the emancipation case.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Imam Abu Hanifa's rebuttal: 'min' defaults to tab'eed; there is no qareenah (contextual indicator) to shift it to bayan. Therefore, the wife only gets authority over a part.
Consistency across contexts is key.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the wife can execute one or two divorces; under the Sahibayn, she can execute three.
This affects the finality of divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قال طلقني نفسك من ثلث الا تو امام رحمہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے نزدیک وہ عورت اپنے آپ کو تین طلاق سے کم طلاق دے سکتی ہے یعنی ایک یا دو صرف عندھما اور صاحبین کے نزدیک وہ عورت تین طلاق دے سکتی ہے وقد مر وجوھما اور ان کے وجوہ گذر چکے ہیں کہ امام کے نزدیک من تبعیضہ ہے اور کل طلاق مشروعہ تین ہے لہذا ان میں سے اس کو بعض کا اختیار ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک من بیانیہ ہے لہذا من
And if he says, 'Divorce yourself from three,' then according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) may Allah have mercy on him, that woman can give herself less than three divorces, i.e., one or two only, according to them; and according to the two companions (Sahibayn), that woman can give three divorces. And their proofs have already been mentioned: that according to the Imam 'min' is for partiality, and the total prescribed divorces is three, so she has authority over some of them; and according to the two companions 'min' is for explanation, so she can give three.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding the interpretation of 'min' in the phrase 'talliqni nafsak min thalath' (divorce yourself from three). The context is a husband delegating divorce to his wife by saying, 'Divorce yourself from three [divorces].' Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the wife may give herself one or two divorces, but not three, because 'min' here indicates partiality (tab'eed), meaning she can take some from the three. The total number of prescribed divorces is three, so she has the right to part of that. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that 'min' is for explanation (bayan), clarifying that the divorce is from the category of three counts, so she can give all three. This dispute mirrors the earlier one on emancipation, showing the consistent application of the usul principle regarding 'min'.

Key Terms
من (min): In the phrase 'min thalath', it is used for either tab'eed (partiality) or bayan (explanation).
تفويض الطلاق (tafweed al-talaq): Delegation of the power to divorce by the husband to the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) She cannot give any divorce because it is unclear.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over 'min' in divorce delegation illustrates the Hanafi usul principle that 'min' defaults to tab'eed (partiality) unless a qareenah indicates bayan (explanation).

The Particle 'Ma' as a Tool of Generality in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the usage of 'ma' for non-rational beings and its metaphorical use, with an example from conditional emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the particle 'ma' as a general term (عام) in Hanafi usul, classify its usage as true or metaphorical, and derive the legal consequence of its generality in a conditional statement.

Core Idea
'Ma' indicates comprehensiveness — its scope embraces all individuals of its category unless metaphorically shifted.

In Hanafi usul, words of generality (ألفاظ العموم) have precise implications. One such word is 'ma' (ما), which primarily applies to non-rational beings but can be metaphorically extended to rational beings. This lesson dissects the ruling of 'ma' through a classical example, showing how its generality determines the outcome of a conditional emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle and its general usage
Recognize 'ma' as a particle of generality (حرف عموم) and note its primary application to non-rational beings (غير العاقل).
2
Distinguish true vs. metaphorical usage
Understand that 'ma' can be used metaphorically for rational beings, but this does not change its general nature; context determines the shift.
metaphorical use is an exception, not a default
3
Analyze the conditional example
Examine the statement: 'If what is in your belly is a male slave, you are free.' The phrase 'ma fi batniki' (ما في بطنك) is a general expression covering every entity in the belly.
4
Apply the generality principle to the facts
Since 'ma' indicates comprehensiveness, the condition requires that ALL contents be male slaves. The birth of both a boy and a girl means the condition is not fulfilled.
5
Derive the legal result
Conclude that the wife is not freed because the condition (full generality) was not satisfied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ماني غير العقلاء وقد يستعار نان قال ان كان مانی بطنك غلاما الخز فولدت لان المراد یعنی چونکہ ما عموم کے لئے ہے اس لئے مراد کل مانی بطنك ہوگا یعنی جمیع ما فی بطنك تو صورت مذکورہ میں جمیع ما فی البطن غلام نہیں ہے بلکہ بعض ما فی البطن غلام ہے بعض مافی البطن جاریہ ہے اس لئے شرط حریت پوری نہیں ہوتی لہذا آزاد نہیں ہوگی ۔
And among them (the particles of generality) is 'ma' for non-rational beings, and sometimes it is used metaphorically. If he said: 'If what is in your belly is a male slave, then you are free', and she gave birth... because 'ma' is for generality, so the intended meaning is everything in your belly, i.e., all that is in your belly. In the mentioned situation, all that is in the belly is not a male slave, rather some is a male slave and some is a female slave. Therefore the condition for freedom is not fulfilled, so she is not free.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage explains the particle 'ma' (ما) as one of the words of generality in Hanafi usul. The author first states that 'ma' is primarily used for non-rational beings, but it may be used metaphorically for rational beings. He then provides an example to illustrate the ruling when 'ma' is used in a conditional statement: a man says to his concubine, 'If what is in your belly is a male slave, you are free.' She gives birth to both a boy and a girl. The author explains that since 'ma' indicates generality, the condition requires that everything in the belly be a male slave. Since not all is male, the condition fails and she is not freed. This shows the importance of understanding the scope of 'ma' as a general term in legal rulings.

Key Terms
ما (ma): A particle of generality primarily for non-rational beings, but can be used metaphorically for rational beings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Dispute Over 'Min' in Delegated Divorce
Intra-Hanafi usul dispute on 'min'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute over the meaning of 'min' in delegated divorce and apply the partitive vs. explanatory distinction to similar textual ambiguities.

Core Idea
Whether 'min' is partitive or explanatory changes the scope of delegated authority in divorce.

In a husband's delegation of divorce to his wife, the precise wording determines the extent of her authority. A single particle—'min'—can either limit her to a fraction of the full three divorces or grant her the entire range. This lesson unpacks the linguistic analysis behind the Hanafi school's internal dispute on this point, revealing how usul principles govern fiqh outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested phrase
The husband says: 'Tallaqni nafsaki min thalath' (Divorce yourself from me from three). The key term is 'min thalath'.
The particle 'min' is the locus of the dispute.
2
State the two interpretations
Imam Abu Hanifa: 'min' is partitive (tab'idiyya) → she may give some of the three, i.e., one or two. The Sahibayn: 'min' is explanatory (bayaniyya) → she may give the full three.
Partitive indicates a part; explanatory clarifies the type.
3
Trace the linguistic basis
Abu Hanifa: 'min' primarily denotes a part of a whole (the whole being three divorces). The Sahibayn: 'min' can also introduce a specification; here it specifies the kind of divorce.
Both rely on established usages of 'min' in Arabic.
4
Evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If she gives fewer than three, the marriage may be revocable; if three, it becomes irrevocable (ba'in). This directly affects the possibility of reconciliation.
The dispute is thus consequential, not merely academic.
5
Recognize it as an intra-Hanafi dispute
Both positions are within the Hanafi school; the text does not definitively favor one. Later Hanafi muftis may adopt either depending on context.
This exemplifies legitimate internal ikhtilaf.

وان قال طلقني نفسك من ثلث الا تو امام رحمہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے نزدیک وہ عورت اپنے آپ کو تین طلاق سے کم طلاق دے سکتی ہے یعنی ایک یا دو صرف دعند ھما اور صاحبین کے نزدیک وہ عورت تین طلاق دے سکتی ہے وقد مر وجبهما اور ان کے وجوہ گذر چکے ہیں کہ امام کے نزدیک من تبعیضہ ہے اور کل طلاق مشرفعہ تین ہے لہذا ان میں سے اس کو بعض کا اختیار ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک من بیانیہ ہے لہذا من ثلث بیان ہوگا اور کل طلاق مشروعہ دے سکتی ہے ۔
And if he said: 'Divorce yourself from me from three' – according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), that woman can give herself less than three divorces, i.e., one or two only [in the view of] the two. And according to the two companions, that woman can give three divorces. Their arguments have passed: according to Imam, 'min' is partitive, and the total prescribed divorce is three, so she has authority over some of them. According to the two companions, 'min' is explanatory, so 'min thalath' is explanatory, and she can give all the prescribed divorces.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents a classic usul dispute on the meaning of 'min' (من) in the husband's statement to his wife, 'Tallaqni nafsaki min thalath' (Divorce yourself from me from three). Imam Abu Hanifa interprets 'min' as partitive (tab'idiyya), meaning 'some of three' – thus the wife can give one or two divorces, but not three. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) interpret 'min' as explanatory (bayaniyya), meaning 'of three' clarifying the type, so she is authorized to give up to three divorces. The dispute stems from whether 'min' indicates division or explanation, a key Hanafi usul principle that has practical fiqh consequences.

Key Terms
من (min): A particle that can be partitive (تبعيضية) or explanatory (بيانية).
تبعيضية (tab'idiyya): Partitive: indicating a part of a whole.
بيانية (bayaniyya): Explanatory: clarifying or specifying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what does 'min' mean in 'min thalath'?
A) Partitive – she can give some of the three.
B) Explanatory – she can give exactly three.
C) Conjunctive – she can give both these and others.
D) Negative – she cannot give any divorce.

Key Takeaway
The same particle can yield different rulings depending on its linguistic interpretation; 'min' in delegated divorce is a classic example of legitimate intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

The Decisive Generality of 'Kull' and 'Jamee'
Explaining the usul rule that 'kull' and 'jamee' are decisive in generality and their differential behavior with indefinite/definite nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify 'kull' and 'jamee' as muhkam tools of generality, distinguish their operation with indefinite vs definite nouns, and apply the rule to determine whether 'kull' indicates distribution over individuals or totality.

Core Idea
Kull and jamee are decisive in generality—they never refer to a single individual, but their meaning splits between 'every' (indefinite) and 'all together' (definite).

In Hanafi usul, words of generality vary in strength. The most authoritative are 'kull' (كل) and 'jamee' (جميع), which are muhkam—decisive and unchallengeable in their generality. This lesson parses their behavior with indefinite and definite nouns, clarifies the limits of specification (takhsees), and shows the practical difference between distributing over individuals (عموم الأفراد) and indicating totality (عموم الأجزاء).

Guided Path
1
State the core rule
Understand that 'kull' and 'jamee' are muhkam: they cannot be interpreted as a single individual. Their generality is decisive.
This is the highest level of clarity in the Hanafi text hierarchy (muhkam).
2
Analyze the specification issue
Recognize that 'kull' and 'jamee' can be specified (takhsees) but never reduced to a single individual. Examine Quranic examples like 'wa khalaqa kulla shay'' and the Hanafi resolution that 'shay'' here means 'possible things' so no specification is needed.
Specification does not weaken the muhkam status; it only restricts the scope to a subset.
3
Distinguish indefinite vs definite
When 'kull' enters upon a nakira (indefinite noun), it indicates distribution over individuals (every single one). When it enters upon a ma'rifa (definite noun), it indicates totality (all together). Use the examples: 'kull rajul' (every man) vs. 'kull al-rijal' (all the men).
This distinction is crucial for correct interpretation of texts.
4
Apply to the hypothetical
Consider the commander's statement: 'Kull man yadkhulu hadha al-hisn awwalan falahu kadha' (every person who enters this fort first gets reward). Determine whether 'kull' here is on a nakira or ma'rifa and what it indicates.
'Man' is a relative pronoun—treat it like a nakira? Actually 'man' is mubham but the rule applies similarly.

And among them are 'kull' and 'jamee'. They are decisive (muhkaman) in the generality of what they enter upon, i.e., the word kull and jamee create generality in that which they enter, and they are decisive in this matter, i.e., they can never become specific such that only a single individual is intended from them; this is not possible. Therefore, one cannot say kull rajul or jamee' al-rijal and intend only one. As for being subject to specification (takhsees), it is said that this is possible, like 'wa khalaqa kulla shay'' and 'Allahu khaliq kulli shay'' are specified to exclude Allah and His speech. Similarly, 'wa uteetu min kulli shay'' is also specified. But a better explanation is that 'shay'' is a masdar meaning 'something' and in this context it is mabni li'l-maf'ul meaning 'wished existence', so it becomes specific to possible things and does not include Allah and His attributes; thus no need for specification. And in 'uteetu min kulli shay'' there is specification by reason, so it is not problematic. So they are decisive in generality, unlike other particles of generality which are not decisive and are subject to specification. If kull enters upon a definite noun, it indicates distribution over individuals, like 'kull rajul yushbihu hadha al-ragheef' – this is generality of individuals, i.e., every single man can be satisfied by this loaf; taking it as a collective is not valid. And if it enters upon a definite noun, it indicates totality, like 'kull al-rijal yahmilu hadha al-hajar' – meaning all men together can carry this stone; here taking it as individual is not valid because no single individual can carry it. In this case, kull indicates generality of parts, not generality of individuals. They said: its generality is on the basis of individual (ala sabeel al-infiraad). The author said that their statement applies when kull enters upon a definite noun? Actually, the text is somewhat garbled. The scholars said: its generality is on the basis of individual. The author said that their statement applies when kull enters upon an indefinite noun, not when it enters upon a definite noun, because in that case it means totality. For example, if the commander says: 'Every person who enters this fort first will be given such-and-such extra reward' – then he entered...
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage explains the characteristics of the words 'kull' (كل) and 'jamee' (جميع) as tools of generality. The author begins by stating that these two words are muhkaman (decisive) in creating generality; meaning they cannot be interpreted as referring to a single individual—if you say 'kull rajul' or 'jamee' al-rijal', you cannot intend only one man. However, they can be subject to specification (takhsees), as seen in Quranic verses like 'wa khalaqa kulla shay'' which are specified by consensus to exclude Allah. But the author offers a better explanation: the word 'shay'' here means 'possible things', so no specification is needed. Then he discusses the difference when 'kull' enters upon a nakira (indefinite) vs. a ma'rifa (definite) noun. With a nakira, it indicates distribution over individuals: 'kull rajul yushbihu hadha al-ragheef' means every single man (each individually). With a ma'rifa, it indicates totality: 'kull al-rijal yahmilu hadha al-hajar' means all men together collectively. He refutes the claim that 'kull' always indicates individuality; rather that is only when it enters upon a nakira. The final example from a commander promising reward to the first to enter a fort illustrates the usage of 'kull' in a conditional statement.

Key Terms
كل (kull): A word of generality that is decisive; when on a nakira it means 'every single one', on a ma'rifa it means 'all together'.
محكم (muhkam): Decisive in its generality; cannot be abrogated or narrowed to a single reference.
عموم الأفراد (umum al-afrad): Generality of individuals – the word covers each single individual separately.
عموم الأجزاء (umum al-ajza'): Generality of parts – the word covers the collective whole, not individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When 'kull' enters upon an indefinite noun (e.g., 'kull rajul'), what does it indicate?
A) The totality of all men together.
B) Distribution over individuals (every single man).
C) A single man.
D) Either depending on context.

Key Takeaway
Kull and jamee are decisive in generality (muhkam) but their interpretation varies: on indefinite nouns they distribute over individuals; on definite nouns they indicate totality.
