The Definitive Generality of 'Kull' and 'Jami'' in Hanafi Usul
Analysis of 'kull' and 'jami'' as definitive words of generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that 'kull' and 'jami'' are muhkam (definitive) in creating generality, distinguish between 'umum al-afrad and 'umum al-ajza' depending on the noun type, and apply this principle to analyze conditional sentences like 'kull man dakhala awwalan'.

Core Idea
'Kull' and 'jami'' are definitive in generality: they never mean a single individual, and their force depends on whether the noun is indefinite (each individual) or definite (collective whole).

This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary clarifies the nature of 'kull' (كل) and 'jami'' (جميع) as tools of generality. Unlike other adawat al-'umum, these two are muhkam (definitive) and can never be used to refer to a singular. However, they differ in how they affect the noun: when entering an indefinite noun, they yield 'umum al-afrad (each individual), but when entering a definite plural, they yield 'umum al-ajza' (the collective). The passage also demonstrates a subtle application in a conditional reward scenario, showing how adding 'kull' can transform the meaning of 'awwal' from singular to multiple.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on 'kull' and 'jami''
State that these two words are muhkam (definitive) in creating generality: they can never be used to mean a single individual. They are unlike other adawat al-'umum which are zanni.
This is a distinctive Hanafi view; other madhhabs may treat them as zanni.
2
Distinguish between 'umum al-afrad and 'umum al-ajza'
When 'kull' enters upon a nakirah (indefinite noun), it yields 'umum al-afrad (each individual separately). When it enters upon a ma'rifah (definite plural), it yields 'umum al-ajza' (the collective whole). This distinction is crucial for correct interpretation.
Example: 'kull rajul' = each man; 'kull al-rijal' = all the men together.
3
Examine the issue of takhsis (specification)
Although 'kull' is definitive in its general application, it can be subject to takhsis by external evidence, such as in 'wa khalaqa kulla shay'' where Allah is excluded. The author offers a refined interpretation to minimise the need for takhsis.
Takhsis does not undermine the definitiveness; it is an operation on the scope.
4
Apply to the conditional reward example
Consider 'man dakhala awwalan falahu kaza' vs 'kull man dakhala awwalan falahu kaza'. In the first, 'man' gives generic generality (badal), so if ten enter simultaneously, no one is uniquely 'awwal', and no one gets the reward. In the second, 'kull' forces multiplicity in 'awwal', so each of the ten is considered 'first' relative to later entrants, and each gets the reward.
This shows how 'kull' transforms the logical structure of the conditional.
5
Derive the Hanafi usul principle
The addition of 'kull' to a conditional particle like 'man' creates a new type of generality: it requires the predicate to apply to each individual meeting the condition, even when the condition is simultaneous.
This principle is uniquely articulated by the author (مصنف).

ومنها كل وجميع وهما محكمان في عموم ما دخلا عليه يعنى لفظ كل وجميع جس پر داخل ہوں گے اس کے اندر عموم پیدا کر دیں گے اور یہ اس امر میں محکم ہیں یعنی یہ کبھی خاص نہیں ہو سکتے کہ ان سے صرف واحد مراد ہو ایسے نہیں ہو سکتا لہذا كل رجل او جميع الرجال بول کر صرف واحد مراد نہیں ہو سکا۔ باقی رہا قابل تخصیص ہونا تو کہا جاتا ہے کہ یہ ہو سکتا ہے جیسے وخلق كل شئ اور اللہ خالق كل شئ سے اللہ تعالی اور کلام الہی کی تخصیص کی جاتی ہے اور اسی طرح و اوتيت من كل شئ بھی مخصص ہے لیکن اس کی بہتر توجیہہ یہ ہے کہ شئ مصدر ہے شانیشا کی اور یہ مصدر اس مقام پر مبنی للمفعول ہے جو بمعنی مشی وجودہ کے ہے اس وقت یہ ممکن کے ساتھ خاص ہوگی اور باری تعالٰی اور اس کے اوصاف کو شامل نہیں ہوگی لہذا ضرورت تخصیص نہیں ہے اور اوتيت من كل شئ میں تو عقلاً تخصیص ہے لہذا یہ قابل اشکال نہیں ہے لہذا یہ محکم فی العموم ہیں بخلاف سائر ادوات العموم یعنی باقی حروف عموم محکم نہیں ہیں بلکہ وہ قابل تخصیص ہیں فان دخل الكل على النكرة اگر لفظ كل داخل على النكرة ہو تو یہ عموم افراد کے لئے ہوگا جیسے كل رجل يشبعه هذا الرغيف تو یہ عموم افراد کے لئے ہے یعنی ہر ایک ایک رجل کو رغیف سیر کر سکتی ہے اور مجموع رجال مراد لینا صحیح نہیں ہے وان دخل على المعرفة اگر لفظ كل داخل على المعرفة ہو تو اس وقت بمعنی مجموع کے ہو گا جیسے كل الرجال تحمل هذا الحجر یعنی مجموع رجال مل کر اس حجر کو اٹھا سکتے ہیں اور یہاں ہر فرد لینا صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ ہر ایک فرد تنہا اس حجر کو نہیں اٹھا سکتا تو اس صورت میں لفظ كل عموم اجزا کے لئے ہوگا لا لعموم الافراد۔ قالوا عمومه على سبيل الانفراد یعنی حضرات علماء نے کہا ہے کہ لفظ كل کا عموم علی سبیل الانفراد ہے یعنی ہر ایک فرد مراد ہوگا اور غیر سے قطع نظر ہوگی مصنف نے فرمایا کہ ان کی یہ بات اس وقت ہے جب لفظ كل داخل علی النكرة ہے نہ کہ اس وقت جب داخل على المعرفة ہو کیونکہ اس صورت میں بمعنی المجموع ہوتا۔ فان قال كل من دخل هذا الحصن اولا فله كذا یعنی جب امیر جیش کہتا ہے کہ ہر وہ شخص جو اس حصن میں اولا داخل ہوگا اسے اتنا نفل یعنی زیادہ علی الہم دی جائیگی - فدخل عشرة معا پس دس آدمی اولا اکھٹے اور معا داخل فی الحصن ہوئے ہیں تو ان میں سے ہر شخص نفل نام کا مستحق ہوگا معا کی قید اس لئے ہے کہ اگر متعاقبا داخل ہوں تو پھر نفل صرف اول کے لئے ہوگی اور معا کی صورت میں گویا ہر فرد با نسبت متخلف کے اول ہے یعنی جو شخص بعد فتح الحصن داخل ہوگا اس کی نسبت ان عشرہ داخلین کا ہر فرد اول و سابق ہے لہذا ہر ایک نفل تام کا مستحق ہوگا تو اس صورت میں من موصوفہ ہوگا تاکہ مدخول كل نكرة هو بخلاف من دخل اولا یعنی بخلاف اس صورت کے کہ جب کہتا ہے من دخل هذا الحصن اولا فله كذا اور لفظ كل من پر داخل نہیں کرتا تو اس صورت میں اگر عشرة معاً داخل ہوتے ہیں تو نفل کسی ایک کو نہیں ملے گی اور نہ مجموع عشرہ کو کیونکہ لفظ من میں عموم علی سبیل الانفراد نہیں ہے بلکہ عموم جنسی ہوا کرتا ہے اور یہاں کوئی داخل اول متحقق نہیں ہے ۔ یہاں فرق آخر اور اس مقام پر ان دونوں عبارت میں ایک اور فرق بھی ہے وہ یہ کہ من دخل اولاً یہ عام علی سبیل البدل ہے یعنی ایک ایک کو علی سبیل البدل شامل ہونے کا امکان ہے کہ جو بھی ہو ہو سکتا ہے کہ اول داخل وہی ہو اور عموم علی سبیل الاجتماع اس کے اندر نہیں ہے اب اس صورت میں اگر دس آدمی معا داخل ہوتے ہیں تو ان کو نفل میں سے کچھ نہیں ملے گا نہ مجموعی طور پر عشرہ کو کچھ ملے گا اور نہ کسی فرد واحد کو۔ ناذا اضاف الكل اليه پھر جب اس من کی طرف لفظ كل مضاف ہوگا تو عموم علی سبیل البدل کے علاوہ اقتضی عموماً آخر تو ایک اور عموم لفظ كل کے ساتھ پیدا ہو جائیگا تا کہ لفظ كل لغو نہ ہو جائے فیقتضى العموم في الاول تو لفظ اول میں مقتضی عموم ہوگا فیتعدد الاول تو لفظ اول کا مصداق متعدد ہو جائیگا اور یہ فرق ایسا ہے جس کے ساتھ مصنف متفرد ہے پھر اس فرق کی تحقیق اور توضیح کرتے ہوئے کہا تحقیقہ ان الاول الخ حاصل تحقیق یہ ہے کہ لفظ اول کا حقیقی معنی ہے ہر ایک کی نسبت فرد سابق یعنی سابق علی کل ما عداه جسے سابق غیر مسبوق کہا جاتا ہے نفی قول من دخل هذا الحصن اولا میں یہی معنی حقیقی لینا ممکن ہے کیونکہ اس صورت میں فرد واحد سابق غیر مسبوق ہو سکتا ہے اور اس میں تعدد نہیں ہو سکتا۔ اما في قوله كل من دخل اولا میں لفظ كل داخل کیا گیا ہے من دخل اولا پر فاقتضى التعدد تو یہ لفظ كل اپنے مضاف الیہ من دخل اولا کے اندر تعدد کا مقتضی ہو جائیگا تو اس
And among them are 'kull' and 'jami''; these two are definitive (muhkaman) in the generality of what they enter upon. That is, the words 'kull' and 'jami'' will create generality (umum) in whatever they enter upon, and they are definitive in this matter, meaning they can never become particular (khas) such that only one individual is intended from them; it is not possible, so by saying 'kull rajul' or 'jami' al-rijal' only one individual cannot be intended. As for being susceptible to specification (takhsis), it is said that this can occur, like in 'wa khalaqa kulla shay'' and 'Allahu khaiqu kulli shay'' where Allah the Exalted and His speech are specified; and similarly 'wa ooteeta min kulli shay'' is also specified (mukhasas). But the better interpretation is that 'shay'' is a masdar from 'shan'isha' and this masdar in this context is used in the sense of the object (mabni li al-maf'ul) meaning 'that whose existence is intended'; then it becomes specific to what is possible (mumkin) and does not include the Creator and His attributes, so there is no need for specification. And in 'ooteeta min kulli shay'' there is rational specification, so it is not problematic. Therefore these are definitive in generality, unlike other tools of generality (adawat al-umum), i.e., the remaining letters of generality are not definitive; rather they are subject to specification (takhsis). If 'kull' enters upon a indefinite noun (nakirah), then it yields generality of individuals (umum afrad), like 'kull rajul yushbi'uhu hadha al-ghuraf', meaning this loaf satiates each individual man; taking a totality of men is not correct. If it enters upon a definite noun (ma'rifah), then it yields the meaning of totality (majmu'), like 'kull al-rijal tahmilu hadha al-hajar', meaning the totality of men together can lift this stone; here taking each individual is not correct because each individual alone cannot lift it. So in this case 'kull' yields generality of parts (umum ajza'), not generality of individuals. They (scholars) said: its generality is on the basis of each individual (ala sabil al-infiraq). That is, the scholars said that 'kull' has generality on the basis of each individual, meaning each individual is intended, disregarding others. The author said that this statement applies when 'kull' enters upon a nakirah, not when it enters upon a ma'rifah, because in that case it means totality. Suppose he says: 'kull man dakhala hadha al-hisna awwalan fahuwa lahu kaza', i.e., when the army commander says: 'Every person who enters this fortress first will be given a bonus (nafl) beyond the promised reward. Then ten people enter simultaneously (ma'an). Then each person among them will deserve that bonus. The qualification 'simultaneously' is because if they enter consecutively (mutataabi'an), then the bonus is only for the first. In the case of simultaneous entry, each individual in relation to those who delayed is considered 'first', because relative to those who enter after the fortress is captured, each of the ten entrants is first and preceding; therefore each deserves the full bonus. In this situation, 'man' is a substantive (mawsufah) so that the object of 'kull' is a nakirah, unlike the case of 'man dakhala awwalan' — i.e., unlike the case when he says: 'man dakhala hadha al-hisna awwalan falahu kaza' and he does not enter 'kull' upon 'man'. In this case, if ten enter simultaneously, none of them gets the bonus, nor does the whole group collectively, because the word 'man' does not have generality on the basis of each individual; rather it has generic generality (umum jinsi), and here there is no single person verified as 'first' (awwal). There is another difference: that 'man dakhala awwalan' is general on the basis of substitution (ala sabil al-badal), meaning it has the possibility of covering each individual on the basis of substitution — whoever might be the first entrant — and it does not have generality on the basis of collective (ala sabil al-ijtima'). In this case, if ten enter simultaneously, they get nothing from the bonus, neither collectively nor individually. Then when 'kull' is attached to 'man', in addition to the generality on the basis of substitution, it requires another generality, so another generality comes into existence with the word 'kull', so that 'kull' is not void (laghw). So it requires generality in the term 'awwal', so 'awwal' becomes multiple. The author is unique in this difference. Then investigating this difference, he said: its investigation is as follows: the real meaning of the word 'awwal' is 'preceding in relation to each one' — i.e., 'that precedes everything else (sabiq ghayr masbuq)'. In the statement 'man dakhala hadha al-hisna awwalan', taking this real meaning is possible because in that case a single individual can be the 'awwal' without being preceded, and multiplicity (ta'addud) is not possible. But in his statement 'kull man dakhala awwalan', the word 'kull' is entered upon 'man dakhala awwalan', so it requires multiplicity; so this word 'kull' necessitates multiplicity in its object (mudaf ilayh) 'man dakhala awwalan'.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary (likely Tawdih/Talwih) discusses the words 'kull' (كل) and 'jami'' (جميع) as tools of generality ('umum). The author first states that these two words are muhkam (definitive) in creating generality: unlike other adawat al-'umum, they can never be used to mean a single individual; they always yield generality. However, they can be subject to takhsis (specification) by external evidence, as in Quranic verses like 'wa khalaqa kulla shay'' where Allah is excluded by consensus, or 'ooteeta min kulli shay'' which is rationally limited. The author suggests a better interpretation for the latter by analyzing 'shay'' as referring to possible things only. Then he explains a crucial distinction: when 'kull' enters a nakirah (indefinite), it gives 'umum al-afrad (generality of individuals) — each individual is included separately. When it enters a ma'rifah (definite plural), it gives 'umum al-ajza' (generality of parts) meaning the collective whole. This refutes the mistaken notion that 'kull' always means each individual separately. The author then illustrates with a vivid example from military reward: a commander promises a bonus to 'whoever enters the fortress first' (من دخل أولاً) versus 'everyone who enters first' (كل من دخل أولاً). In the first phrase, if ten enter simultaneously, none gets a bonus because 'awwal' is singular and no single person is uniquely first. In the second phrase, 'kull' forces a multiplicity in the meaning of 'awwal', so each of the ten is considered 'first' relative to those who enter later, and each gets the full bonus. This shows how 'kull' can transform the meaning of its object through a subtle usul mechanism. The author's analysis is detailed and uniquely Hanafi, emphasizing that the addition of 'kull' to 'man' creates a new type of generality beyond the original badal (substitutive) generality of 'man'.

Key Terms
كل (kull): A word of generality that is muhkam (definitive) in creating 'umum; never used for a single individual but can be specified.
جميع (jami'): Synonym of 'kull' in being a definitive tool of generality.
العموم (al-'umum): Generality; covering all individuals of a kind or all parts of a whole.
محكم (muhkam): Definitive in meaning; not subject to reinterpretation as something other than generality.
النكرة (an-nakirah): Indefinite noun; when 'kull' enters upon it, gives 'umum al-afrad.
المعرفة (al-ma'rifah): Definite noun (plural); when 'kull' enters upon it, gives 'umum al-ajza' (collective meaning).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a commander says 'kull man dakhala hadha al-hisna awwalan falahu kaza' and ten soldiers enter simultaneously, how many receive the reward?
A) None, because no single person entered first.
B) Only the first one to cross the threshold.
C) All ten, because 'kull' makes each considered 'first' relative to later entrants.
D) The group collectively receives one reward.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle that 'kull' and 'jami'' are muhkam in 'umum means they can never be used for a single individual, and their precise operation depends on whether the noun is indefinite (each individual) or definite (collective whole).

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

How 'Kull' Transforms the Generality of 'Man Dakhala Awwalan'
Core textual analysis of two decree phrasings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings under 'man dakhala awwalan' and 'kullu man dakhala awwalan' by applying the Hanafi principle that 'kull' necessitates multiplicity (t'addud).

Core Idea
Adding 'kull' to 'man dakhala awwalan' forces a metaphorical reading of 'first' to allow multiple claimants.

This passage examines a hypothetical decree about spoils. The difference between saying 'whoever enters first' and 'everyone who enters first' is not just verbal—it changes the entire ruling because it forces us to reinterpret the word 'first' from its literal meaning (un-preceded precedent) to a figurative one (preceding those who lag behind).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrases
Contrast 'man dakhala awwalan' (whoever enters first) with 'kullu man dakhala awwalan' (everyone who enters first). Recognize that the addition of 'kull' is the only difference.
2
Analyze the generality of 'man'
Determine that 'man' has generic generality (عموم جنسي) and operates on a substitutive basis (على سبيل البدل). It includes one individual at a time, not all simultaneously.
This is distinct from collective generality.
3
Apply the literal meaning of 'awwalan'
The literal meaning of 'first' is 'un-preceded precedent' (سابق غير مسبوق), which can only apply to one person. Therefore, if ten enter simultaneously under 'man dakhala awwalan', no one qualifies as the sole first, so no one gets the nafl.
4
Add 'kull' and derive necessity of multiplicity
When 'kull' is prefixed to 'man dakhala awwalan', it necessitates multiplicity in its attached term. Since the literal meaning of 'first' cannot be multiple, the word must be interpreted metaphorically as 'preceding over those who lag behind' (سابق على المتخلف), which can be multiple.
This avoids redundancy of 'kull'.
5
Derive the ruling for simultaneous entrants
Under 'kullu man dakhala awwalan', multiple simultaneous entrants each qualify as 'first' in the metaphorical sense, so each gets a full nafl. Under 'man dakhala awwalan', no one gets anything when entering together.
The difference hinges on the interpretation of 'first'.

اس میں تعدد ممکن نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ صرف فرد واحد ہو سکتا ہے فیراد معناه المجازی تو

اس صورت میں لفظ اول کا معنی مجازی مراد ہوگا۔ وهو السابق بالنسبة الى المتخلف يعني سابق على المتخلف یعنی جو لوگ متخلف ہے کر فتح حصن کے بعد داخل ہوتے ان سے جو سابق ہو وہ اول ہوگا تو اس وقت اول کا معنی سابق علی کل ما عداہ نہیں بلکہ سابق علی غیرہ ہوگا بہر حال متخلف في الدخول پر سابق ہو یہ متعدد ہو سکتا ہے جب اس صورت میں عشرہ رجال معا داخل ہوتے ہیں تو یہ عبارت ان تمام کو شامل ہو جائیگی لہذا عشرہ کا ہر فرد نفل نام کا مستحق ہوگا۔ اور اگر عشره سر حال فراری داخل ہوتے ہیں تو صرف فرد واحد جو اولاً داخل ہوا ہے وہی بطریق اولی حق نفل تام ہو گا کیا سیاتی فی لفظ الجميع وجميع عمومه على سبيل الاجتماع یعنی لف جمیع کے اندر عموم علی سبیل الاجتماع ہے علی سبیل الانفراد نہیں ہے خان قال جميع من دخل هذا الحصن اولاً فلہ ہذا تو اس حصن میں دس آدمی مجتمعا و معا داخل ہوئے ہیں تو یہ دس اجتماعی طور پر نفل واحد کے مستحق ہوں گے ہر ایک ایک کو نفل نام نہیں ملے گی وان دخلو افرادي بستحق الاول فيصير مستعاراً نکل اگر اس صورت میں معاداخل نہیں بلکہ فرادی یعنی ایک ایک ہو کر داخل ہوتے ہیں تو پھر ان میں سے جو سب سے پہلے داخل ہوا ہے وہی نفل نام کا مستحق ہوگا باقیوں کو کچھ نہیں ملیگا اس وقت لفظ جميع مستعار ہوگا برائے لفظ کل یعنی مجازاً بمعنی کل ہو جائیگا جیسے کل من دخل کی صورت میں جو منفرداً اول داخل ہو ا مستحق ہوتا تھا یہاں بھی ایسے ہوگا ۔ کذا ذکرہ فخر الاسلام في اصوله ويرد عليه انه يلزم الجمع بين ارادة الحقيقة والمجاز یعنی اس پر یہ اشکال دارد ہوتا ہے کہ اس صورت میں حقیقی و مجاز دونوں مراد ہو رہے ہیں اور حقیقہ نہ مجاز دونوں کا بیک وقت ارادہ کرنا اور مراد لینا جائز نہیں ہے ولا يمكن ان يقال بعض حضرات نے اس اشکال کا جواب دیا تھا کہ جمع بین الحقيقة والمجاز واقع نہیں ہوتی کیونک ان اتفق الدخول على سبیل الاجتماع یعنی اگر اتفاقی طور پر دخول علی سبیل الاجتماع واقع ہوا تو اس وقت لفظ جمیع محمول علی المعنی الحقیقی ہوگا اگر اتفاقا ایسی صورت پیدا ہوتی کہ وہ فرار می داخل ہوتے ہیں تو لفظ جمیع محمول علی المجاز ہوگا یعنی بمعنی کل ہو گا ہر ایک کا
Ten men simultaneously entered the fort together; then among them which person will be entitled to the nafl? The restriction 'together' is because if they enter successively, then the nafl will be only for the first. And in the case of 'together', each individual, relative to those who come later, is earlier. That is, each of these ten entrants is earlier and preceding compared to the one who enters after the conquest. Therefore each will be entitled to the full nafl. So in this case, 'man' will be specified such that the meaning includes everyone. Unlike 'man dakhala awwalan', i.e., unlike the case where one says 'whoever enters this fort first, he gets such-and-such' and does not include the word 'kull' over 'man'. In that case, if ten enter together, then the nafl will not be given to any one of them, nor to the group, because the word 'man' does not have generality on the basis of individuality, rather it has generic generality, and here there is no actual first entrant. There is another difference: 'man dakhala awwalan' is general on the basis of substitution, meaning it can include each individually on a substitutive basis - whoever it may be, it is possible that he is the first entrant. And generality on the basis of simultaneity is not within it. Now in this case, if ten men enter together, they will not get anything from the nafl, neither collectively nor individually. But when 'kull' is added to 'man', then besides generality on the basis of substitution, another generality is required, so that with the word 'kull' another generality is produced so that the word 'kull' is not rendered meaningless. Thus it requires generality in the word 'first', so the referent of 'first' becomes multiple. And this difference is unique to the author. Then, while verifying and explaining this difference, he said: the research is that the literal meaning of 'first' is, relative to each, the preceding individual, i.e., preceding over all others, which is called un-preceded precedent. In the statement 'man dakhala hadha al-hisn awwalan', this literal meaning can be taken because in this case only one individual can be un-preceded precedent, and multiplicity is not possible. But in the statement 'kullu man dakhala awwalan', the word 'kull' is entered upon 'man dakhala awwalan', so it requires multiplicity. So then the word 'kull' necessitates multiplicity in its added term 'man dakhala awwalan'. In this case, the word 'first' cannot be interpreted in its literal meaning (preceding over all others) because multiplicity is not possible in that; rather it can only refer to a single individual. So its metaphorical meaning is intended, which is: the one who is preceding relative to those who lag behind, i.e., preceding over those who lag behind, meaning those who enter after the conquest. So then 'first' means not preceding over all others, but preceding over others. This can be multiple. So when ten men enter together, this expression covers all of them, hence each of the ten becomes entitled to the full nafl. And if the ten enter individually, then only the one who entered first is entitled to the full nafl. Similarly, in the expression 'al-jami'': 'jami'' has generality on the basis of simultaneity, not individuality. If someone says 'jami'u man dakhala hadha al-hisn awwalan falahu kadha', and ten men enter collectively together, then these ten collectively will be entitled to one nafl; each individual will not receive a nafl. If they enter individually, then the first among them is entitled, and then the word 'jami'' becomes metaphorical, meaning 'kull', like in the case of 'kullu man dakhala' where the one who enters first individually is entitled. Thus Fakhral-Islam mentioned in his Usul, and it is objected to him that this necessitates combining literal and figurative meaning. The objection is that in this case both literal and figurative are intended, and intending both simultaneously is not permissible. Some scholars have responded to this objection that the combination of literal and figurative does not occur because if the entry occurs collectively, then the word 'jami'' is interpreted in its literal meaning; if it happens that they enter individually, then 'jami'' is interpreted figuratively meaning 'kull'.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of how the word 'kull' (every) affects the generality of the phrase 'man dakhala awwalan' (whoever enters first). The context is the distribution of spoils (nafl) in military expeditions. The author explains that if the ruler says 'man dakhala hadha al-hisn awwalan falahu kadha', the word 'man' (whoever) has generic generality but not individual generality; it operates on a substitutive basis (على سبيل البدل), meaning only one person can be the 'first'. Therefore, if ten men enter simultaneously, none qualifies as the sole 'first', so no one gets the nafl. However, if the ruler says 'kullu man dakhala awwalan', the addition of 'kull' necessitates multiplicity in the meaning of 'first'. Since 'kull' cannot be redundant, it forces the interpretation that 'first' here means 'preceding relative to those who lag behind' (the metaphorical meaning), which can apply to multiple individuals if they enter together. Hence each of the ten gets a full share. The same logic is applied to the word 'jami'' (all): it normally denotes collective generality, so if ten enter together, they share one nafl; but if they enter individually, it is reinterpreted metaphorically as 'kull' to give the first entrant the whole nafl. The passage ends with a known objection from Fakhral-Islam al-Pazdawi that interpreting a word as both literal and metaphorical simultaneously is invalid. The author responds that the interpretation depends on the actual situation: if they enter collectively, the literal meaning applies; if individually, the figurative meaning applies, so no combination occurs at the same time.

Key Terms
عموم (umum): Generality; the inclusion of all individuals that the word can cover. The text distinguishes between umum 'ala sabil al-badal (substitutive generality, i.e., one individual at a time) and umum 'ala sabil al-ijtima' (collective generality, i.e., all together).
بدل (badal): Substitution; the word includes individuals one at a time, not all simultaneously. For example, 'man dakhala' means whoever enters, but only one person qualifies as 'the first'.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning; in this passage, the literal meaning of 'awwalan' is 'preceding over all others' (سابق على الكل).
مجاز (majaz): Figurative meaning; in this passage, the figurative meaning of 'awwalan' is 'preceding over those who lag behind' (سابق على المتخلف).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a commander says 'man dakhala hadha al-hisn awwalan falahu naflun', and ten soldiers enter simultaneously, what is the correct Hanafi ruling?
A) Each of the ten gets a full nafl.
B) The ten share one nafl equally.
C) No one receives the nafl because there is no single 'first' entrant.
D) The nafl is awarded by lot among the ten.

Key Takeaway
The addition of 'kull' to 'man dakhala awwalan' transforms the interpretation of 'first' from literal (un-preceded precedent) to metaphorical (preceding those who lag behind), allowing multiple simultaneous entrants to each claim the reward.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
Resolving the Objection: Combining Literal and Figurative Meaning in 'Jami''
Extension of analysis to 'jami'' and resolution of the literal/figurative objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate Fakhral-Islam's objection to the analysis of 'jami'u man dakhala awwalan' and state the Hanafi response that the context determines which meaning applies.

Core Idea
Literal and figurative meanings do not combine simultaneously; the actual context selects one, avoiding the objection.

This lesson extends the previous analysis to the word 'jami'' (all) and addresses a key objection from Fakhral-Islam al-Pazdawi. He argues that interpreting 'jami'' both literally (collective generality) and figuratively ('kull') in the same decree leads to an impermissible combination of literal and figurative meanings. The Hanafi response clarifies that the interpretation depends on the actual situation of entry—collective or individual—so no simultaneous combination occurs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the phrase 'jami'u man dakhala awwalan'
The word 'jami'' (جميع) denotes collective generality (عموم اجتماعي). If ten enter together, they are treated as one collective entity, receiving a single nafl to be shared.
2
Recognize the shift when entry is individual
If the ten enter individually (one by one), the collective meaning cannot apply to the first alone. So 'jami'' is reinterpreted figuratively as 'kull' (every), giving the nafl solely to the first entrant.
This is a departure from its literal meaning.
3
Identify the objection
Fakhral-Islam objects that this analysis requires intending both literal (collective share) and figurative (individual share) meanings of 'jami'' at the same time, which is invalid because a word cannot be used in both its haqiqah and majaz simultaneously.
4
Know the Hanafi response
The combination does not occur because the actual circumstances of entry determine which meaning applies. If the entry is collective, the literal meaning is used; if individual, the figurative meaning is used. They are never both intended at the same time.
The ruling is context-dependent, not a simultaneous dual intention.
5
Apply the distinction
Thus, under 'jami'u man dakhala awwalan': if ten enter together, they share one nafl (literal); if they enter individually, only the first gets the nafl (figurative 'kull'). This avoids the objection.

جا سکتا
اس میں تعدد ممکن نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ صرف فرد واحد ہو سکتا ہے فیراد معناه المجازی تو

And in the case of 'together', each individual, relative to those who come later, is earlier. But in the statement 'kullu man dakhala awwalan', the word 'kull' is entered upon 'man dakhala awwalan', so it requires multiplicity. So then the word 'kull' necessitates multiplicity in its added term 'man dakhala awwalan'. In this case, the word 'first' cannot be interpreted in its literal meaning (preceding over all others) because multiplicity is not possible in that; rather it can only refer to a single individual. So its metaphorical meaning is intended, which is: the one who is preceding relative to those who lag behind, i.e., preceding over those who lag behind, meaning those who enter after the conquest. So then 'first' means not preceding over all others, but preceding over others. This can be multiple. So when ten men enter together, this expression covers all of them, hence each of the ten becomes entitled to the full nafl. And if the ten enter individually, then only the one who entered first is entitled to the full nafl. Similarly, in the expression 'al-jami'': 'jami'' has generality on the basis of simultaneity, not individuality. If someone says 'jami'u man dakhala hadha al-hisn awwalan falahu kadha', and ten men enter collectively together, then these ten collectively will be entitled to one nafl; each individual will not receive a nafl. If they enter individually, then the first among them is entitled, and then the word 'jami'' becomes metaphorical, meaning 'kull', like in the case of 'kullu man dakhala' where the one who enters first individually is entitled. Thus Fakhral-Islam mentioned in his Usul, and it is objected to him that this necessitates combining literal and figurative meaning. The objection is that in this case both literal and figurative are intended, and intending both simultaneously is not permissible. Some scholars have responded to this objection that the combination of literal and figurative does not occur because if the entry occurs collectively, then the word 'jami'' is interpreted in its literal meaning; if it happens that they enter individually, then 'jami'' is interpreted figuratively meaning 'kull'.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of how the word 'kull' (every) affects the generality of the phrase 'man dakhala awwalan' (whoever enters first). The context is the distribution of spoils (nafl) in military expeditions. The author explains that if the ruler says 'man dakhala hadha al-hisn awwalan falahu kadha', the word 'man' (whoever) has generic generality but not individual generality; it operates on a substitutive basis (على سبيل البدل), meaning only one person can be the 'first'. Therefore, if ten men enter simultaneously, none qualifies as the sole 'first', so no one gets the nafl. However, if the ruler says 'kullu man dakhala awwalan', the addition of 'kull' necessitates multiplicity in the meaning of 'first'. Since 'kull' cannot be redundant, it forces the interpretation that 'first' here means 'preceding relative to those who lag behind' (the metaphorical meaning), which can apply to multiple individuals if they enter together. Hence each of the ten gets a full share. The same logic is applied to the word 'jami'' (all): it normally denotes collective generality, so if ten enter together, they share one nafl; but if they enter individually, it is reinterpreted metaphorically as 'kull' to give the first entrant the whole nafl. The passage ends with a known objection from Fakhral-Islam al-Pazdawi that interpreting a word as both literal and metaphorical simultaneously is invalid. The author responds that the interpretation depends on the actual situation: if they enter collectively, the literal meaning applies; if individually, the figurative meaning applies, so no combination occurs at the same time.

Key Terms
جميع (jami'): All; indicates collective generality (عموم اجتماعي). It treats multiple individuals as a single group entity.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning. In this passage, the literal meaning of 'jami'' is collective generality.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative meaning. Here, 'jami'' is used figuratively to mean 'kull' (every) when entry is individual.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is Fakhral-Islam's objection about combining literal and figurative meaning invalid?
A) Because the literal and figurative meanings of 'jami'' are actually the same.
B) Because the actual circumstances of entry determine which meaning applies, so they are not combined simultaneously.
C) Because the objection is not taken seriously by the Hanafi school.
D) Because 'jami'' never takes a figurative meaning.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi resolution to the objection of combining literal and figurative meaning is that the meaning of 'jami'' is context-dependent, triggered by the actual entry pattern, so no simultaneous combination occurs.

Combining Haqiqah and Majaz: The Hanafi Position
Usul analysis of combining haqiqah and majaz using 'jami''

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule against combining haqiqah and majaz in one utterance and evaluate the role of intention at the time of speech using the example of 'jami''.

Core Idea
Intention at speech decides meaning, not external events — you cannot intend literal and figurative simultaneously.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi usul principle: the impossibility of combining literal (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings in a single utterance. Using the example of a reward for the first entrant into a fortress, we examine how the word 'jami'' (جميع) triggers a debate about intention. You will learn why the Hanafi school insists that the speaker's intention at the moment of speech is the only valid reference, not later events.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Identify the facts: a ruler says 'جميع من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً فله هذا' (all who enter first get a reward). Ten men may enter together or individually. How does the word 'jami'' apply?
The literal meaning of 'jami'' is collectivity, but it can be used metaphorically for 'kull' (each).
2
Recognize the two interpretations
If they enter together, 'jami'' is literal — they share one reward. If they enter individually, the first one gets the reward; 'jami'' becomes metaphorical for 'kull' meaning 'each'.
Fakhr al-Islam al-Pazdawi supported this dual interpretation.
3
Identify the objection
The objection: this would require intending both literal (collective) and figurative (distributive) meanings at the same time, which is impermissible.
Combining haqiqah and majaz is a well-known usul prohibition.
4
Evaluate the attempted answer
Some scholars argued that since the two entry modes occur at different times, no combination happens. The author refutes this: intention at the time of speech is what matters, not actual occurrence.
The time of speech is a single moment; the speaker must have one intention.
5
Understand the author's resolution
The author explains that when 'jami'' is metaphor for 'kull', it only refers to the first meaning of 'kull' (the first entrant, whether one or many), not the second meaning (each individual gets a full reward). Thus only one meaning is intended.
This avoids the objection because only one intention is present.

المحصن اولاً میں یہی معنی لیا گیا تھا۔ نبهنا يراد الامر الاول اس مقام پر جہاں لفظ جمیع مستعار للکل ہے تو کل سے مراد وہی معنی اول ہے یعنی داخل اول خواہ بصورت واحد ہو یا بصورة جماعة ولا يراد المعنى الحقیقی یعنی یہاں لفظ جمیع سے معنی حقیقی مراد نہیں ہوگا جس کے اندرا جتماع امر ضروری تھا جس کے لحاظ سے الواحد المنفرد مستحق نہیں ہو سکتا ولا الامر الثانی یعنی کل کا جو معنی ثانی پیش کیا گیا ہے یہ بھی مراد نہیں یعنی لفظ جمیع کا استعارہ کل بالمعنی الثانی کے لئے نہیں ہے ورنہ تو مجتمعا معا داخل ہونیکی صورت میں ہر ایک نفل نام کئے ہو جاتا بلکہ اس صورت میں تو جمیع کے جمیع نفل واحد کے مستحق ہیں تو معلوم ہواکہ کل بالمعنی الثانی کے لئے مستعار نہیں ہے
In such a case, the word 'first' is interpreted in its literal meaning, i.e., 'preceding over everything else'. In this, multiplicity is not possible; rather it can only be a single individual. Then if its figurative meaning is intended, then the meaning of the word 'first' in this case would be figurative, meaning 'the one preceding relative to those who lag behind', i.e., preceding over the laggards. That is, those who lag behind and enter after the conquest of the fortress, whoever precedes them is 'first'. So then the meaning of 'first' is not 'preceding over everything else' but 'preceding over others'. In any case, it is possible for multiple persons to be preceding over those who lag in entry. When in this case ten men enter simultaneously, this expression includes all of them, so every individual of the ten deserves the reward. And if ten men enter individually, then only the single individual who entered first will deserve the complete reward by way of precedence. This will be discussed under the word 'jami''. And 'jami' has general meaning collectively, i.e., in the word 'jami'' there is generality on the basis of collectivity, not individually. The author said: 'All who enter this fortress first, for him is this.' So if ten men enter together simultaneously, then these ten collectively will be entitled to a single reward; each one will not receive a reward. And if they enter individually, the first one deserves the reward, then it becomes metaphorical. If in this case they do not enter together but individually, i.e., one by one, then among them the one who entered first is entitled to the reward, and the rest get nothing. At that time, the word 'jami'' becomes figurative for the word 'kull', i.e., metaphorically meaning 'each' like in the case of 'kull man dakhala' where the one who entered first individually deserved. Here too it will be similar. Thus Fakhr al-Islam mentioned it in his Usul. And it is objected against him that it entails combining the intention of literal and figurative meaning, i.e., in this case both literal and figurative are intended, and intending both literal and figurative simultaneously is not permissible. And it cannot be said that some people answered this objection by saying that combining literal and figurative does not occur because if entry occurs collectively, then the word 'jami'' is interpreted literally; if it happens that they enter individually, then the word 'jami'' is interpreted figuratively, i.e., meaning 'each' – their occurrence cannot be simultaneous, they occur at separate times, so combining literal and figurative does not occur. The author refuted this answer by saying, 'It cannot be said', and refuted it, stating the reason: 'because at the time of speech, consideration is given to what is intended by the word; ultimately, at the time of speech, it is necessary that a specific meaning among the two is intended, and intending a specific meaning precludes intending the other specific meaning. If you intend each of the two meanings, then combining literal and figurative becomes necessary. In any case, consideration is given to the intention of meaning at the time of speech, not to external occurrence. And your answer is based on external occurrence, which is not considered. Therefore the objection is not resolved.' So I say: the meaning of his statement 'it is metaphorical for 'kull'' is that the author gave his own explanation of this metaphor to repel the mentioned objection. In summary, what Fakhr al-Islam said – that it is metaphorical for 'kull' – its meaning is that 'kull' individual indicates two matters: first, the first entering person deserves the reward, whether that first entrant is one or a group; second, the second signification of 'kull' is that if the first entrant is a group, then each individual of them deserves a complete reward, as in the case when the word 'kull' enters on a nakira, and in 'kull man dakhala hadha al-hisn awwalan' this meaning was taken. We note: intended is the first matter – here where the word 'jami'' is metaphorical for 'kull', what is meant by 'kull' is that first meaning, i.e., the first entrant whether individually or as a group, and the literal meaning is not intended – which required collectivity, such that a single individual cannot deserve – nor the second meaning, i.e., the second meaning of 'kull' is not intended either. So the metaphor of 'jami'' is not for 'kull' with the second meaning; otherwise, in the case of entering together, everyone would become deserving of a reward, but in that case, they are collectively deserving of a single reward. So it is clear that it is not metaphorical for 'kull' with the second meaning.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the usul issue of combining literal and figurative meanings. It uses the example of a reward (nafl) for the first entrant into a fortress. The discussion revolves around the word 'jami'' (جميع) and its interpretation when applied to a group entering simultaneously or individually. Fakhr al-Islam (al-Pazdawi) had stated that if ten men enter together, they collectively deserve one reward; if individually, only the first deserves. He further claimed that 'jami'' becomes metaphorical for 'kull' in the individual case. The objection raised is that this would entail intending both literal (collective) and figurative (individual) meanings at the same time, which is not permissible. Some attempted to answer that the occurrence is separate, so no combination occurs. The author refutes this by asserting that the intention at the time of speech is what matters, not the external occurrence. Therefore, the objection remains. The author then provides his own explanation: when 'jami'' is used metaphorically for 'kull', it refers only to the first meaning of 'kull' (the first entrant, whether one or many) and not the second meaning (each individual deserving a separate reward). This avoids the problem because only one meaning is intended at the time of speech. The passage is a classic Hanafi usul discussion on the limits of metaphor and the impossibility of combining literal and figurative intentions.

Key Terms
جمیع (jami'): A word indicating all individuals collectively, not individually.
کل (kull): A word indicating each individual, whether one or many, in a distributive sense.
حقیقت (haqiqah): Literal meaning that is originally coined for a word.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative meaning used in place of literal due to a relationship and a preventer from literal meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul position in this passage, what is the ruling if a ruler says 'جميع من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً فله هذا' and ten men enter together simultaneously?
A) Each of the ten deserves a separate reward.
B) The ten collectively deserve a single reward.
C) Only the first among them deserves the reward.
D) The reward is divided equally among them.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the intention at the time of speech determines meaning, and it is impossible to intend both literal and figurative meanings in one utterance.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Meaning of 'جمیع' in Reward for Early Entry
Analyzing the word 'جمیع' to determine if collective entry is required for reward

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the concept of 'عموم المجاز' (generality of metaphor) and identify how a contextual indication (قرینہ) shifts a word from literal to metaphorical meaning in a text.

Core Idea
The contextual indication (قرینہ) reveals that the literal meaning of 'جمیع' is not intended; the metaphorical generality includes the lone entrant.

This passage analyzes a statement about entering a fort first and receiving supererogatory reward. The word 'جمیع' (all) is used, but the commentator argues that the literal meaning requiring collective entry is not intended. Instead, the context of encouragement shows that even a single person entering alone is entitled. This leads to the concept of 'عموم المجاز' (generality of metaphor), a subtle usul tool where a metaphorical expression covers multiple cases including the literal. Understanding this tool helps you read texts with more precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key word and context
Locate the word 'جمیع' in the text and note that the speech is for incitement and encouragement (تحریض و ترغیب). This sets the stage for interpreting the word not as a strict requirement but as a motivation.
The context of speech is crucial in usul al-fiqh to determine intended meaning.
2
Recognize the contextual indication (قرینہ)
The commentator argues that if a group entering together is rewarded, then by a fortiori reasoning (طریق اولیٰ), a single person entering alone is also rewarded. This reasoning shows that collective entry is not a condition, forming a clear قرینہ that the literal meaning requiring all is not intended.
A قرینہ can be textual, contextual, or rational.
3
Determine the intended meaning as metaphorical
Since the قرینہ contradicts the literal meaning, the word 'جمیع' must be understood metaphorically. The metaphorical meaning is that the first entrant (سابق فی الدخول) is entitled to reward, whether alone or in a group.
Metaphorical interpretation is valid when a قرینہ is present.
4
Apply the concept of عموم المجاز
The metaphorical expression is general ('umum al-majaz): it covers both the single entrant (the intended case) and the group entrant (the literal case), including the literal meaning as one of its instances. However, the ruling is not derived from the literal word but from the metaphorical generality.
عموم المجاز is a Hanafi concept; other schools may treat it differently.
5
Appreciate the precision of the derivation
The passage concludes that the reward is for the first entrant, regardless of being alone or in a group. This is a precise usul analysis showing how a contextual indication can override the apparent literal meaning to give a more nuanced ruling.
This demonstrates the importance of قرینہ in interpreting legislative texts.

ذلك لان هذا الكلام للتحرنی يہ لفظ جمیع کے معنی حقیقی مراد نہ ہونیکی دلیل ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ یہ کلام تحریض اور ترغیب کے لئے ہے یعنی حسن میں اولاً داخل ہونے کیلئے رغبت اور شوق دلانے کے لئے پیش کی جاتی ہے تو اس کا مقتضا یہ ہے کہ جو سابق فی الدخول ہو وہ نفل کا مستحق ہوگا خواہ وہ منفرد ہو یا مجتمع اور اجتماع کوئی ضروری شرط نہیں ہے کیونکہ جب کوئی ایک فرد اقدام علی الدخول کرتا ہے اور دوسرا متخلف رہ جاتا ہے تو اس کا تخلف اس اول کو محروم از استحقاق نہیں کر سکتا اور قرینہ بھی موجود ہے جو اجتماع فی الدخول کے شرط نہ ہونے پر دلالت کرتا ہے کیونکہ جب چند افراد مجتمعا اور معا داخل ہونے پر جرات کر کے مستحق نفل ہوتے ہیں تو جب ایک آدمی تن تنها منفرد أداخل ہونیکی جرات کرتا ہے تو بطریق اولیٰ مستحق ہوگا۔ فلابراد المعنی الحقیقی تو اس قریہ کی بنا پر لفظ جمیع کا معنی حقیقی جس کے اندر اجتماع شرط ہے مراد نہیں ہوگا ۔
وايضا لا دلیل یہ کل بالمعنی الثانی کے مراد نہ ہو نیکی وجہ ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ اس پر بھی کوئی دلیل موجود نہیں ہے کہ جب حصن میں اولاً ایک جماعة مجتمعاً ومعا داخل ہو تو اس جماعت کا ہر ہر فرد نقل نام کا مستحق ہو۔ بل الکلام دال بلکہ یہ کلام اس پر دال ہے کہ مجموعہ افراد نقل واحد کے مستحق ہوں گے ۔ فصار الكلام مجازاً تو اب یہ کلام مجاز ہوگی جس کا معنی مجازی یہ ہوگا کہ سابق فى الدخول مستحق نفل ہے خواہ یہ سابق منفردا ہو یا مجتمعا یہ عموم المجاز ہے جس کے اندر حقیقی مفہوم مندرج ہے تو اس عموم المجاز کے لحاظ سے سابق فی المدخول مجتمعا ہو تو مستحق ہوں گے ۔ اس لئے نہیں کہ لفظ جمیع کا معنی حقیقی مراد ہے بلکہ اس لئے کہ عموم المجاز میں مندرج ہے اور اس میں داخل ہے بذا بحث فی غایتة التدقيق
That is because this speech is for encouragement. This word 'جمیع' is evidence that the literal meaning is not intended. Summary: This speech is for incitement and encouragement, i.e., to induce desire and eagerness to enter the fort first. So its requirement is that whoever is first in entry becomes entitled to the supererogatory reward, whether alone or in a group, and gathering is not a necessary condition. Because when one individual undertakes entry and another remains behind, his remaining behind cannot deprive the first of entitlement. And there is also a contextual indication that gathering is not a condition for entry. Because when several individuals gather together and enter, they become entitled to supererogatory reward, then when one person alone enters, he is a fortiori entitled. Therefore, based on this contextual indication, the literal meaning of the word 'جمیع' in which gathering is a condition is not intended. Also, there is no evidence that the second meaning is intended. Summary: There is no evidence that when a group enters the fort together, each individual becomes entitled to a supererogatory reward. Rather, the speech indicates that the whole group is entitled to a single reward. So the speech becomes metaphorical. Its metaphorical meaning is that the one who precedes in entry is entitled to supererogatory reward, whether he precedes alone or in a group. This is generality of metaphor (عموم المجاز), which includes the literal meaning. Therefore, according to this generality of metaphor, if one precedes in entry in a group, they are entitled, not because the literal meaning of 'جمیع' is intended, but because it is included in the generality of metaphor.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a detailed textual analysis of a phrase (likely a hadith or statement) that uses the word 'جمیع' (all) in the context of reward for entering a fort. The commentator argues that the literal meaning of 'جمیع' requiring collective entry is not intended because the speech is meant to encourage individuals to enter early. The contextual indication (قرینہ) shows that even a single person entering alone is entitled to reward, so the word must be understood metaphorically. The metaphor (مجاز) yields the general rule that the first entrant, whether alone or in a group, deserves reward. This is called 'عموم المجاز' (generality of metaphor), where the metaphorical meaning encompasses the literal cases but is not limited to them. The commentator concludes by noting that this is a very precise discussion.

Key Terms
عموم المجاز (umum al-majaz): The generality of a metaphorical expression that includes the literal meaning as one of its instances.
قرینہ (qarina): A contextual indication that shifts the meaning from literal to metaphorical.
تحریض و ترغیب (tahrid wa targhib): Incitement and encouragement; a rhetorical purpose that often suggests a non-literal interpretation.
بطریق اولیٰ (bi-tariq al-awla): By a fortiori reasoning; if a stronger case applies, then a weaker case applies even more.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason the literal meaning of 'جمیع' is not intended?
A) The word 'جمیع' is ambiguous and has multiple meanings.
B) The speech is for encouragement and a contextual indication (قرینہ) shows that a single person entering alone is also entitled.
C) The group reward is only for those who enter together, so each individual gets a separate reward.
D) The word 'جمیع' is a metaphor for 'the one who enters first' exclusively.

Key Takeaway
When a contextual indication (قرینہ) contradicts the literal meaning, the word is interpreted metaphorically, and the resulting generality (عموم المجاز) includes the literal case without being limited to it.

Shuf'a for Neighbor: The Qawl vs Fi'l Debate
Hanafi rebuttal to Shafi'i objection on using hadith of shuf'a for neighbor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a hadith as either a saying (qawl) or an act (fi'l) and apply the Hanafi rule of lam al-istighraq to derive a general ruling for shuf'a.

Core Idea
A report of the Prophet's act can become general through linguistic analysis, especially lam al-istighraq.

The Hanafi school relies on the hadith 'qada bi al-shuf'a li al-jar' to establish pre-emption for an adjacent neighbor, even if not a partner. The Shafi'i school objects that this is merely a report of an act, which does not generalize. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi response: first, that the hadith is actually a saying transmitted with meaning, and second, that even if treated as an act, the definite article 'al' in 'al-jar' indicates comprehensiveness (istighraq), covering every neighbor. This dispute highlights a crucial usul principle about the interpretation of reports and definiteness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
State the difference: Hanafis hold that shuf'a is due for a non-partner neighbor; Shafi'is hold it is only for a partner. Identify the key proof: the hadith.
2
Classify the hadith report
Determine whether the hadith is a hikayat al-qawl (report of a saying) or hikayat al-fi'l (report of an act). This classification affects generality.
Hanafis argue it is a saying transmitted with meaning; Shafi'is consider it a report of a specific act.
3
Apply the Hanafi first answer
If it is a saying, the original wording 'al-shuf'a thabit li al-jar' is general and applies to all neighbors.
The narrator paraphrased as 'qada bi al-shuf'a li al-jar', but the essence is a general statement.
4
Apply the Hanafi second answer
Even if it is an act, analyze the word 'al-jar'. Since there is no previously mentioned specific neighbor, the 'al' is for istighraq (comprehensiveness), meaning every neighbor.
This uses the usul principle: lam al-ta'rif becomes istighraq in the absence of a covenant (ma'hud).
5
Derive the ruling
Conclude that the hadith establishes shuf'a for all neighbors, including non-partners, according to the Hanafi school.
The Shafi'i position restricts it to the specific partner-neighbor in the original incident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
د اما نحو قضى بالشفعة للجار فليس من هذا القبيل التم [؟] جیسے کہ مصنف نے فرمایا کہ درحقیقت یہ جواب از اشکال ہے تفصیل مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ شریک فی نفس المبیع اور شریک فی حقوق المبیع یعنی وسائل آبپاشی وغیرہ میں جو شریک ہے ان کے لئے شفعہ بالاتفاق ثابت ہے جو شخص شریک فی نفس المبيع وحقوق المبيع نہیں صرف مسیج [؟] جو از قسم اراضی یا دار ہے اس کا وہ جار ملاصق اور ہمسایہ بلا فصل ہے اس کے لئے احناف کے نزدیک حق شفعہ ثابت ہے اور امام شافعی کے نزدیک ثابت نہیں ہے احناف نے اپنے مسلک پر اس حدیث سے استدلال کیا کہ قضیٰ النبی صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم بالشفعة للجار شوافع کی جانب سے اس استدلال پر یہ اشکال وارد کیا گیا کہ یہ حکایت فعل ہے جو عام نہیں ہوا کرتی لہذا ہر جار کے لئے شفعہ کا حق ثابت نہیں ہوگا بلکہ یہ اس جار کے لئے ہے جو شریک بھی ہو تو شوافع نے اس حدیث کا مطلب یہ سمجھا کہ رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کی مجلس میں ایک شخص جو جار تھا اس نے شفعہ کا دعویٰ دائر کیا رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے اس کے حق میں فیصلہ صادر فرمایا لہذا یہ ایک فعل ہے جس کی حکایت کی گئی ہے اور حکایت الفعل لا یعم تو مصنف نے اس اشکال کا جواب دیتے ہوئے کہا کہ لیس من هذا القبيل یعنی از قبیل حکایت فعل نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ نقل الحدیث بالمعنی ہے اصل میں حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا یہ قول تھا الشفعہ ثابت للجار اور راوی نے اسی کو تعبیر بالمعنی کرتے ہوئے کہا قضیٰ بالشفعة للجار ولكن سلمنا یہ جواب ثانی ہے یعنی اگر ہم تسلیم کر لیں کہ یہ حکایت فعل ہے تو تب بھی ہمارا استدلال نام ہے کیونکہ انجار کا لام تعریف استغراق جنس کے لئے ہے کیونکہ یہاں معہود کوئی نہیں لہذا لام عہد کا نہیں ہو سکتا لام استغراق کا ہو کر ہر قسم کے جار کو شامل ہو گا خواہ وہ شریک نہو یا نہ ہو لہذا جار غیر شریک پر بھی استدلال صحیح ہے فصام [؟] کانہ قضی بالشفعة لكل جار سواء كان شریکا او غیر شریک
As for the example 'He ruled in favor of shuf'a for the neighbor', it is not of this category. As the author said: In reality this is a response to an objection. The detailed ruling is: The partner in the very sold property and the partner in the rights of the property (e.g., irrigation rights) — for them shuf'a is established by consensus. The person who is neither a partner in the property nor in its rights, but merely an adjacent neighbor without a dividing wall — for him, according to the Hanafis, the right of shuf'a is established, but according to Imam al-Shafi'i, it is not. The Hanafis used this hadith as evidence for their position: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) ruled in favor of shuf'a for the neighbor.' The Shafi'is objected: This is a report of an act, which does not generalize. Therefore, the right of shuf'a is not established for every neighbor; rather it is for that specific neighbor who was also a partner. They understood the hadith to mean that during the Prophet's gathering, a person who was a neighbor filed a claim for shuf'a, and the Prophet issued a ruling in his favor. Thus it is a reported act, and a reported act does not generalize. The author replied: This is not of that category (i.e., it is not a reported act); rather it is a transmission of meaning. Originally the Prophet's saying was 'shuf'a is established for the neighbor', and the narrator paraphrased it as 'qada bi al-shuf'a li al-jar'. But even if we concede that it is a reported act, our evidence still stands because 'al-jar' has the lam of definition for istighraq (comprehensiveness) of the genus, since there is no specific antecedent. Therefore it cannot be a lam of covenant; it is a lam of istighraq, covering every type of neighbor, whether a partner or not. Hence the evidence is valid even for a non-partner neighbor. So it is as if he ruled in favor of shuf'a for every neighbor, whether partner or not.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of a key Hanafi proof for shuf'a (pre-emption) for a non-partner neighbor. The Hanafis use the hadith 'qada bi al-shuf'a li al-jar' (He ruled in favor of shuf'a for the neighbor) to establish shuf'a for all neighbors. The Shafi'is object that this is a report of an act (hikayat al-fi'l) which does not indicate generality; thus it only applies to the specific neighbor in the incident. The Hanafi response consists of two answers. First: the hadith is actually a saying (qawl) transmitted with meaning; the original was 'al-shuf'a thabit li al-jar' (shuf'a is established for the neighbor), which is a general statement. Second: even if we accept it as an act, the word 'al-jar' contains the definite article 'al' which here indicates istighraq (comprehensiveness) because there is no previously mentioned specific neighbor (ma'hud). Thus it means 'every neighbor', including non-partners. This displays the Hanafi usul principle of interpreting lam al-ta'rif as istighraq in the absence of a covenanted referent. The dispute shows the importance of distinguishing between qawl and fi'l in deriving rulings.

Key Terms
حكاية الفعل (hikayat al-fi'l): A report of an act of the Prophet without quotation of his words; such reports do not indicate generality.
لام الاستغراق (lam al-istighraq): The definite article 'al' that indicates inclusion of all individuals of a genus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi second answer, why does 'al-jar' in the hadith include all neighbors?
A) Because the Prophet's act was performed in the mosque.
B) Because there is no specific antecedent, so 'al' indicates istighraq.
C) Because all scholars agree the hadith is mutawatir.
D) Because the word 'jar' is indefinite.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes shuf'a for a non-partner neighbor by analyzing the hadith as a general saying or by interpreting 'al-jar' with lam al-istighraq.

Words After a Question or Event: Four Types
Classification of utterances based on their independence and relation to context

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify utterances after a question or event into four types and apply the correct particle ('na'am' vs 'bala') in legal acknowledgment scenarios.

Core Idea
Context determines meaning: 'na'am' affirms the proposition, 'bala' reverses a negation.

In legal contexts, the meaning of a response often depends on whether it is independent of the preceding question or event. This lesson classifies such utterances into four types, with a crucial distinction between the particles 'na'am' and 'bala' that directly affects legal acknowledgments (iqrar). Understanding this classification prevents critical errors in interpreting courtroom responses.

Guided Path
1
Identify non-independent words
Recognize words like 'na'am' and 'bala' that have no meaning without reference to the preceding question or event. These are classified as 'ghair mustaqill'.
Non-independent words rely entirely on context for their semantic and legal effect.
2
Distinguish 'na'am' and 'bala'
Note that 'na'am' affirms the truth of the preceding proposition regardless of its form (positive or negative). 'Bala' specifically follows a negation and reverses it, affirming the opposite.
'Na'am' after a negative question affirms the negation, leading to denial; 'bala' affirms the positive.
3
Classify independent words into three subtypes
Independent words (mustaqill) have three probabilities: (a) definitively a response, (b) apparently a response but with possibility of independent speech, (c) apparently independent but with possibility of response.
The legal effect varies: if it is definitively a response, the acknowledgment is binding only if it matches the context.
4
Apply to legal acknowledgment
Use the classification to determine whether a debtor's answer 'na'am' or 'bala' constitutes an acknowledgment (iqrar) or denial.
Example: If creditor asks 'A laysa li alayka kaza?' (negative question), 'na'am' affirms the negation and thus denies the debt; 'bala' affirms the debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسئلہ - اللفظ الذي ورد بعد سوال او حادثة الخ یعنی وہ لفظ جو بعد از سوال واقع ہو یا کسی حادثہ میں واقع ہوا اور اس کو اس سوال وحادثہ سے تعلق بھی ہے تو اس کے اقسام اربعہ ہو سکتی ہیں اول یہ کہ ان لا یکون مستقلا یعنی وہ لفظ کلام مستقل نہ ہو اور جبتک سوال یا حادثہ کا اعتبار نہ کیا جائے تو وہ کسی معنی نام کا مفید نہیں ہے جیسے لفظ " نعم " بلی " لفظ نعم اپنے ماسبق کو ثابت رکھتا ہے خواہ وہ کلام موجب نہو یا منفی اور متقی بعید استفہام ہو یا بصورت خبر اور لفظ بلی بعد از نفی واقع ہوتا ہے خواہ یہ نفی بصورت استفہام ہو یا بصورت خبر اس کا اثبات کر دے گا جیسے الیس اللہ بقادر تو اس کا جواب غلط ہے اور بلی سے صحیح ہے تو بلی کی صورت میں معنی ہوگا بلی دھو قادر - اريكون مستقلا و خینشن یعنی مستقل ہو نیکی صورت میں اس کے اندر احتمالات ثلاثہ میں اما ان خرج مخرج الجواب قطع یعنی قطعی طور پر ماقبل کا جواب ہو گا اور اس کے اندر کلام مستانف ہونیکا احتمال نہیں ہو گا یہ ایک احتمال ہوا احتمالات ثلثہ میں سے اد الظاهرانه جواب با ظاہر یہ ہے کہ ماقبل کا جواب ہے مع احتمال الابتداء یعنی ساتھ ساتھ احتمال ابتدا بھی موجود ہے یعنی از سر نو اور مستانف کلام ہو اور ماسبق کا جواب نہ ہو یہ احتمال قائم ہے یہ احتمال ثانی من الثلثہ ہے ۔ ادبا لعکس یہ احتمال ثالث من الثلثہ ہے کل اربعہ اقسام ہوئے خلاصہ اس احتمال کا یہ ہے کہ الظاهرانه ابتداء یعنی ظاہر یہ ہے کہ یہ ابتدائی اور مستانف کلام ہے اور ساتھ احتمال جواب ہو نیکی بھی گنجائش. نحو آليس لي عليك كذا کیا میرا آپ پر اتنا فرض نہیں ہے وہ مخاطب کہتا ہے بلی یعنی ہاں ہے یہاں جواب بلفظ نعم صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس کا معنی ہوگا کہ نہیں پھر اقرار ثابت نہیں ہوگا یا ایک شخص کہتا ہے کہ کان کی عليك كذا کہ میرا تیرے اوپر اتنا قرض ہے مخاطب کہتا ہے نعم تو اس صورت میں اقرار ثابت ہو جائیگا هذا نظير غير المستقل
Issue: The word that occurs after a question or an event — i.e., a word that occurs after a question or occurs in an event, and it has a relation to that question and event — then it can have four divisions. First: that it is not independent, meaning that the word/speech is not independent, and unless the question or event is considered, it does not convey any meaning, like the word 'na'am' (yes) and 'bala' (yes indeed). The word 'na'am' affirms what precedes it, whether the preceding speech is positive or negative, and whether the preceding is a question or a statement. The word 'bala' occurs after negation, whether that negation is in the form of a question or a statement; it affirms it. For example, 'A laysa Allahu biqadir?' (Is not Allah capable?). The answer with 'na'am' is wrong, and with 'bala' is correct. So in the case of 'bala', the meaning is 'Yes, He is capable.' Second: that it is independent — then there are three probabilities within it: Either (a) it appears definitively as a response to the preceding, with no possibility of being an independent new speech; this is the first probability. Or (b) the apparent meaning is that it is a response to the preceding, with a possibility of it being a new beginning, i.e., starting anew and independent speech, not a response to the preceding; this second probability. Or (c) the opposite: the apparent meaning is that it is an independent beginning, with a possibility of it being a response; this third probability. Thus there are four total types. The summary: In the case of independence, there are three probabilities. Example: 'Is it not that I have such and such right upon you?' The addressee says 'bala' meaning yes. Here answering with 'na'am' is not correct because its meaning would be 'no', then the acknowledgment would not be established. Or a person says: 'I have such and such debt upon you.' The addressee says 'na'am.' In this case, the acknowledgment is established. This is similar to the non-independent category.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule about interpreting words that come in response to a question or event. The author classifies such words into four types based on their independence. The first type: non-independent words like 'na'am' and 'bala' that derive meaning only from context. 'Na'am' affirms the proposition regardless of its form. 'Bala' specifically follows negation and confirms the opposite. Examples: if asked 'A laysa Allahu biqadir?', saying 'na'am' would incorrectly deny capability; 'bala' correctly affirms. The second category: independent words that have three sub-types: (a) definitively a response with no chance of independent speech; (b) apparently a response but with possibility of independent beginning; (c) apparently independent beginning but with possibility of response. This classification helps in fiqh applications like acknowledgment of debt: if a creditor says 'You owe me this' and the debtor says 'na'am', acknowledgment is valid (independent response). The passage uses examples from legal acknowledgment to illustrate the rule. This shows how usul al-fiqh provides nuanced rules for linguistic interpretation that affect practical legal outcomes.

Key Terms
غير مستقل (ghair mustaqill): A word that does not convey complete meaning without reference to the preceding question or event (e.g., 'na'am', 'bala').
بلى (bala): A particle used to affirm a negative proposition, establishing the opposite of what the negation suggests.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone asks 'A laysa li alayka kaza?' (Don't I have such right upon you?), and the respondent says 'Na'am', what is the legal effect?
A) The right is affirmed because 'na'am' means yes.
B) The right is denied because 'na'am' affirms the negative question.
C) The answer is invalid and no legal effect.
D) It depends on intention.

Key Takeaway
The meaning and legal effect of a response depend on whether it is independent and which particle ('na'am' or 'bala') is used, with 'bala' specifically reversing a negation.

Interpreting Words After a Question: The Four Categories and the Hanafi Rule
Dividing words after a question into four types and applying the rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories of words after a question and apply the Hanafi rule to determine whether a word is a response or a new beginning.

Core Idea
An extra word after a question turns the response into a new beginning (ibtidāʾ) — every word must have a purpose.

When someone speaks after a question or an incident, the legal interpretation depends on whether the speech is independent and whether extra words are added. The Hanafis classify these situations into four types, with a crucial rule: if an extra word beyond the minimum response is present, the speech is treated as a new oath or statement, not merely a response. This lesson explains the four categories and the Hanafi position, contrasting it with the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish independent vs non-independent speech
First, determine if the word would have meaning without the preceding context. If not (like 'na'am'), it is non-independent (ghayr mustaqill) and always a response.
Non-independent words are always responses.
2
Identify the three subcategories for independent speech
For independent words, they can be: (a) definitely a response (no possibility of new beginning), (b) apparently a response with possibility of new beginning, (c) apparently a new beginning with possibility of response.
These make up three of the four types.
3
Recognize the fourth category: independent with an extra word
When an extra word is added beyond what is necessary for a simple response (e.g., adding 'today' to 'if I eat lunch'), the word becomes a candidate for a new beginning.
This is the only type where disagreement arises.
4
Apply the Hanafi rule: extra word → new beginning (ibtidāʾ)
The Hanafi rule is to interpret the extra word as adding meaning, so the speech is taken as a new beginning (ibtidāʾ), not a response. This avoids rendering the extra word useless (laghw).
The principle: ḥaml al-ziyāda ʿalā al-ifāda.
5
Note the practical consequence and dispute with Shafi'is
If treated as a new beginning, the speaker's condition (e.g., 'If I eat lunch today, my slave is free') becomes binding. If treated as a response, it would not create a separate obligation. The Shafi'is treat it as a response, even with extra words.
The Hanafi position is upheld in the text.

[PAGE_272] ... [Previous discussion about neighbor and pre-emption omitted.] Issue: The word that occurs after a question or an incident, i.e., the word that occurs after a question or that occurs in an incident, and it also has a connection to that question/incident. So it can have four divisions. First: that it is not independent, i.e., that word is not an independent speech, and unless the question or incident is taken into account, it does not convey any meaning, like the word 'na'am' and 'bala'. The word 'na'am' affirms what precedes it, whether the preceding speech is positive or negative, and whether the preceding is a question or a statement. The word 'bala' occurs after negation, whether that negation is in the form of a question or a statement; it affirms the opposite, like 'Alaysa Allahu biqādir' (Is not Allah All-Powerful?) – its answer with [na'am] is wrong, and with 'bala' it is correct. So in the case of 'bala', the meaning will be: 'Yes, He is All-Powerful'. [Second:] Or it is independent and has [three probabilities]: [a] it comes as a definite response, i.e., it is definitely the response to the preceding, and there is no possibility of it being a new beginning. This is one of the three probabilities. [b] It is apparently a response, i.e., outwardly it is the response to the preceding, with the possibility of it being a beginning, i.e., there also exists the possibility of it being a new and independent speech and not a response to the preceding. This is the second of the three probabilities. [c] The opposite, this is the third of the three probabilities. So total four types. The summary of this probability is that apparently it is a beginning, i.e., outwardly it is a beginning and independent speech, and there is also room for the possibility of it being a response. Example: 'Alaysa lī ʿalayka kadha?' (Do I not have such and such a right upon you?) The addressee says 'bala' meaning 'yes'. Here answering with 'na'am' is not correct because its meaning would be 'no', and then the acknowledgment would not be established. Or a person says 'kāna ʿalayka kadha' (I had such and such a debt upon you), the addressee says 'na'am', then in this case acknowledgment is established. This is an example of the non-independent type. [PAGE_273] It is an example of the non-independent type, i.e., it is a response to its preceding; without it, it does not convey meaning. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) having forgetfulness in prayer and performing prostration of forgetfulness, and the incident of Ma'iz who committed fornication and was stoned. This is an example of the independent type that is definitely a response, i.e., 'sajada' and 'rajama' are independent speech and definitely a response to the preceding; there is no possibility of them being a new beginning. And like 'Ta'āl taghadda ma'ī' (Come eat lunch with me), the addressee says 'In taghaddaytu fakadhā' (If I eat lunch, then such and such), without any extra words. This is an example of the independent type that is apparently a response to the preceding. And like 'In taghaddaytu al-yawma' (If I eat lunch today) – this is an example of the independent type that is apparently a beginning, with the possibility of a response. In each context, the word 'naḥwa' (like) is mentioned to indicate that each example corresponds to one type. In the first three cases, these words are to be taken as a response. In the fourth case, i.e., where there is an extra word beyond what is necessary for the response, it is taken as a beginning, i.e., it is not a response but rather a new and independent speech. According to us, this is to make the extra word beneficial so that this extra word is not useless. So if someone says 'Today, if I eat any lunch' – whether he eats the same food he was invited to, or eats something else, or with the inviter or someone else or alone – in all these cases he will be guilty (of breaking his oath) and his slave will be freed. And if he says, 'I intended the response', then it can be accepted as truthful in conscience because he intended a meaning that his speech could possibly bear. But it will not be accepted judicially because it is contrary to the apparent [meaning] and because it entails a concession and personal benefit for him; in such cases, the court cannot verify his claim. And according to Imam al-Shafi'i, it is taken as a response, just like the first three cases, even where there is an extra word beyond what is necessary for the response.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage explains a key usul principle regarding how to interpret a word (or phrase) that occurs in response to a question or after an incident (such as a legal case). The author divides such words into four categories: (1) non-independent (ghayr mustaqill) – the word has no meaning without reference to the preceding, like 'na'am' and 'bala'; (2) independent and definitely a response – the word is clearly a response with no possibility of being a new statement, like the Prophet's prostration of forgetfulness or the stoning of Ma'iz; (3) independent and apparently a response but with possibility of being a new beginning; (4) independent and apparently a new beginning but with possibility of being a response. The first three types are taken as responses. The fourth type, where an extra word (e.g., 'al-yawma' – today) is added beyond what is necessary for a simple response, is taken as a new beginning (ibtida') according to the Hanafis. This avoids rendering the extra word useless. As a result, the person's vow (if he says 'If I eat lunch today, my slave is free') is given full effect, and he breaks his oath if he eats any lunch that day. The Hanafi position holds that if he claims he only intended to respond (to the invitation), this is accepted in conscience but not in court, because it contradicts the apparent meaning and benefits him. Imam al-Shafi'i, however, treats the fourth type as a response as well, analogous to the first three. The passage then provides examples from hadith and everyday speech to illustrate each category.

Key Terms
مستقل (mustaqill): Independent speech that conveys a complete meaning without reference to the preceding context.
غير مستقل (ghayr mustaqill): Non-independent speech that requires the preceding context to convey meaning, like 'na'am' or 'bala'.
جواب (jawāb): A response that directly answers or is connected to the preceding question or incident.
ابتداء (ibtidāʾ): A new beginning; an independent statement not connected as a response to the preceding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is taken as a response
B) It is taken as a new beginning
C) It is invalid
D) It depends on the speaker's intention

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: an extra word after a question transforms the response into a new beginning, giving every word its full legal effect.
