Conditional Sentences: Jawab or Ibtida'? The Hanafi Distinction
Usul rule on interpreting conditional sentences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for distinguishing between a responsive conditional (jawaab) and a new independent statement (musta'naf) by identifying the presence of extra words beyond the minimal response, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-position.

Core Idea
Extra words in a conditional indicate a new independent oath, not a mere response, because every added word must carry legal benefit.

In Hanafi usul, the interpretation of conditional sentences in oaths depends critically on whether the wording includes extra elements beyond the minimal reply. This passage from our text presents a clear typology: when a person responds to an invitation with a conditional without extra words, it is considered a jawaab; but when an extra term like 'today' is added, the conditional becomes a musta'naf — a new independent oath. The text then contrasts this with the Shafi'i view and grounds the Hanafi position in the general usul principle 'al-ibrat bi 'umum al-lafz la bi khusus al-sabab.' By understanding this distinction, you will be able to derive the correct legal consequence of breaking such a conditional and appreciate how the Hanafi school preserves the benefit of every word in legal speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories: jawaab and musta'naf
From the examples given, notice that a conditional without extra words (e.g., 'If I eat lunch') is classified as a response (jawaab) to the preceding invitation, whereas one with an extra word (e.g., 'today') is treated as a new independent statement (musta'naf).
Look for the presence of extra wording beyond what is strictly necessary to complete the response.
2
Locate the indicator: the extra word
The key difference between the first three cases and the fourth is the addition of 'اليوم' (today). This extra word is the specific indicator that changes the classification.
According to the Hanafi school, extra words must carry legal benefit; if the conditional were still a response, the extra word would be superfluous.
3
Derive the ruling for the fourth case
Because the conditional is now musta'naf, it binds the speaker to all instances of the condition. If the speaker says 'If I eat lunch today, my slave is free,' then regardless of what, when, or with whom he eats, the condition is fulfilled and the penalty (hanith) applies.
The court does not accept a claim of intending a response; it is accepted only in conscience (diyanah) if the speaker can plausibly mean the response.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i treats all conditionals as responses, rendering any extra words superfluous. This leads to a different practical outcome: the court would accept the speaker's claim of intending a response.
The disagreement stems from differing usul principles: Hanafi gives each word independent weight, while Shafi'i prioritizes the apparent linkage to the prior context.

معی آئیے میرے ساتھ یہ غدام کھائیے فقال ان تغديت فكذ ا مخاطب کہتا ہے کہ اگر میں نے خدا کھائی تو میرا غلام ازاد من غير زيادة اور ان الفاظ مذکورہ سے کوئی زیادہ لفظ نہیں بولتا ھذا نظیر المستقل یہ نظیر ہے اس مستقل کی جس کے متعلق ظاہر یہی ہے کہ یہ ماسبق کا جواب ہے ونحو ان تغديت اليوم الا جو قدر واجب فی الجواب تھا اس پر لفظ الیوم کی زیادتی کر دی بی نظیر ہے اس مستقل کی جس کے متعلق ظاہر یہ ہے کہ یہ ابتداء کلام ہے ساتھ ساتھ احتمال جواب بھی فی کل موضع الر یعنی پہر مقام کے اندر لفظ نحو ذکر کر کے اشارہ کیا یہ قسم واحد کی نظیر میں یعنی ہر ایک مثال ایک ایک قسم کی نظیر ہے ۔ فى الثلثة الدول پہلی تین صورتوں میں ان الفاظ کو محمول علی الجواب کیا جائیگا اور رابع میں یعنی جہاں قدر واجب فی الجواب سے لفظ زائد موجود ہے محمل على الابتداء یعنی یہ جواب نہیں بلکہ یہ کام از سر نو اور مستانف ہے ہمارے نزدیک حملاً للزيادة على الافادة تاکہ یہ زیادہ برید فائدہ کی مفید ہو اور یہ زیادہ علی الجواب لغو ہونے سے بچ جائے لہذا آج دن میں جو بھی غدار کھائی گا خواہ یہی غذا کھائے جس کی طرف اسے دعوت دی گئی یا اور کھاتے داعی کے ساتھ کھانے یا کسی اور کے ساتھ یا تنہا کھائے ان تمام صورتوں میں وہ حانث ہو جائیگا اور اس کا غلام ازاد ہو جائیگا ولو قال عنيت الجواب اگر اس زیادتی کی صورت میں وہ کہہ کہ میری مراد صرف جواب ما سبق تھا تو اسے دیانت صادق قرار دیا جاسکتا ہے کیونکہ اس نے ایسے معنی کا ارادہ کیا ہے جو اس کی کلام کہ کا محتمل ہوسکتا ہے لیکن اسے قضاء صادق قرار نہیں دیا جائیگا کیونکہ ایک تو خلاف ظاہر ہے دوسرا یہ کہ اس کے اندر اس پر تخفیف اور اس کا ذاتی فائدہ ہے ایسی صورتوں میں قضاء اس کی تصدیق نہیں ہو سکتی ۔ وعند الشافعي يحمل على الجواب امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے نزدیک سہیلی تین سورتوں کی طرح اس چوتھی صورت کو جس کے اندر زیادۃ علی قدر الجواب موجود ہے اس کو بھی محمول علی الجواب کیا جائیگا تو انھوں نے اس زیادہ کو بالکل لغو قرار دے دیا ۔ وهذا اما قيل ان العبرة الخز یہ وہی بات ہے جو کہی جاتی ہے کہ اعتبار عموم لفظ کا ہوتا ہے خصوص سبب کا اختیار نہیں کیا جاتا کیونکہ تمسک اور استدلال لفظ کے ساتھ ہوتا ہے اور وہ عام ہے اور خصوص سبب عموم لفظ کا منافی نہیں ہے۔ فان الصحابة ومن بعدهم صحابہ اور من بعدهم من التابعین رضی اللہ تعالیٰ اجمعین نے ان عمومات کے ساتھ تمسک اور استدلال کیا جو حوادث اور اسباب خاصہ میں واقع ہوئے اور ان اسباب و حوادث خاصہ پر مقصور نہیں رکھا جیسے آیت الظہار جو خولہ زوجہ اوس بن الصامت کے واقعہ میں نازل ہوئی اور آیت لعان بلال بن امیہ کے بارہ میں نازل ہوئی لیکن یہ احکام ان کے ساتھ مختص نہیں بلکہ امت عامہ کیلئے ہیں۔
An example of a non-independent statement is that it is a response to its predecessor; without it, it is not meaningful. And the prohibition of the Prophet, peace be upon him, in the mosque and the fornication which is known as stoning: meaning the Prophet committed forgetfulness in prayer so he performed prostration of forgetfulness, and the incident of Ma'iz who committed adultery so he was stoned. This is an example of an independent statement that is definitely a response, i.e., the mosque and the stoning are independent speech and definitely a response to what preceded, with no possibility of being a new utterance. And an example: 'Come, have lunch with me' – the addressee says: 'If I have lunch, then my slave is free' without any extra words. This is an example of an independent statement that is apparently a response to what preceded. And an example: 'If I have lunch today' – he adds the word 'today' beyond what is necessary for the response. This is an example of an independent statement that is apparently a new utterance, alongside the possibility of it being a response. In each place he used the word 'example' to indicate that each example represents a type. In the first three cases, these words are interpreted as a response. In the fourth case, where there is an extra word beyond what is necessary for the response, it is interpreted as a new utterance, i.e., it is not a response but an independent new statement. According to us, this is to carry the extra word to be beneficial so that this extra is beneficial and not superfluous to the response. Therefore, if he says 'If I eat lunch today', whether he eats the same food to which he was invited or eats something else, with the inviter or someone else or alone, in all these cases he will be considered guilty (hanith) and his slave will be free. If he says in this case of extra wording, 'I meant only the response to what preceded', then he can be considered truthful in terms of conscience (diyanatan) because he intended a meaning that his speech could possibly bear. But he will not be considered truthful in court (qada'an) because it is contrary to the apparent meaning and because there is leniency for him and personal benefit; in such cases, the court cannot confirm his claim. According to Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him), just like the first three cases, this fourth case which contains an extra word beyond what is necessary for the response is also interpreted as a response; thus he considers the extra word to be completely superfluous. And this is the principle that is stated: that consideration is given to the generality of the wording, not to the specificity of the cause, because adherence and argumentation are based on the wording, which is general, and the specificity of the cause does not contradict the generality of the wording. Indeed, the Companions and those after them from the Followers (may Allah be pleased with them all) adhered to and argued with these general statements that occurred in specific incidents and events, and did not restrict them to those specific causes and events, such as the verse of Zihar which was revealed in the incident of Khawlah, the wife of Aws ibn al-Samit, and the verse of Li'an which was revealed regarding Bilal ibn Umayyah; but these rulings are not specific to them, but are for the entire Muslim community.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on how to interpret conditional sentences (jawaab vs. musta'naf) in the context of oaths and responses, with a comparative discussion with the Shafi'i school. The text begins by giving examples: the Prophet's prostration of forgetfulness (sajda sahw) in the mosque and the stoning of Ma'iz are presented as clear independent statements that are responses to an implied cause. Then it introduces a scenario: a person says 'Come have lunch with me,' and the addressee responds, 'If I have lunch, then my slave is free' without extra words. This is taken as a response to the invitation. Another example adds the word 'today' beyond what is necessary, making it a new statement (ibtida') rather than a response. The ruling: in the first three types (implicit response, explicit conditional without extra words), the conditional is considered a response; in the fourth type (with extra wording), it is considered a new statement. The Hanafi reasoning is that the extra word should carry benefit, not be superfluous. Consequently, breaking the condition (e.g., eating lunch) triggers the penalty (hanith) regardless of the circumstances of eating, because the conditional is interpreted as a new independent oath. However, if the speaker claims he meant the conditional as a response (despite the extra word), his claim is accepted in conscience (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an), because it contradicts the apparent meaning and benefits him. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, interpreting all cases, including with extra words, as responses, rendering the extra word superfluous. The passage then transitions to a usul principle: 'al-ibrat bi 'umum al-lafz la bi khusus al-sabab' (consideration is given to the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the cause). This is supported by the practice of the Companions who applied general verses (like zihar and li'an) beyond the specific incidents of revelation. Thus, the lesson demonstrates how general usul principles govern the interpretation of specific conditional expressions.

Key Terms
جواب (jawaab): A conditional or other statement that is a response to a previous utterance or implied context.
ابتداء (ibtida'): A new independent statement unrelated to any previous utterance.
عبرت بعموم اللفظ لا بخصوص السبب (al-ibrat bi 'umum al-lafz la bi khusus al-sabab): The principle that legal rulings are based on the generality of the wording (of Quran and hadith), not on the specific occasion or cause of revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the conditional 'If I eat lunch today' become a new statement (musta'naf) rather than a response?
A) Because it is a response to an invitation.
B) Because the extra word 'today' indicates a broader condition beyond the immediate invitation.
C) Because the speaker intends it as a joke.
D) Because Imam al-Shafi'i says so.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, extra words in conditionals shift the classification from jawaab to musta'naf, ensuring every part of speech carries legal weight.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Carrying Mutlaq to Muqayyad: Conditions and Ikhtilaf
Explains conditions for carrying mutlaq to muqayyad with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three scenarios for carrying mutlaq to muqayyad in Hanafi usul and apply the correct rule to derive a fiqh ruling.

Core Idea
Mutlaq is only carried to muqayyad when ruling and incident are same AND texts enter on the ruling—not the cause.

In Hanafi usul, the relationship between mutlaq and muqayyad is governed by careful conditions. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position versus the Shafi'i approach, focusing on the key factors: unity of ruling, unity of incident, and whether the texts address the cause or the ruling itself.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish mutlaq and muqayyad
Identify two texts: one without a qualifying condition (mutlaq) and one with a qualifying condition (muqayyad).
Example: 'Free a slave' (mutlaq) vs. 'Free a believing slave' (muqayyad).
2
Check unity of ruling
If the ruling (hukm) differs, there is no need to carry; each text applies independently.
If ruling is the same, proceed to next step.
3
Check unity of incident
If the incident (hadithah) differs, Hanafis do not carry; each text stands alone. If incident is the same, proceed.
Example: kaffarah yamin vs. kaffarah qatl have different incidents → no carrying.
4
Determine entry on cause or ruling
If the texts enter on the cause (sabab), Hanafis do not carry because multiple causes can coexist. If they enter on the ruling itself, carry by consensus.
Example: sadaqat al-fitr texts enter on cause → no carrying; fasting three days texts enter on ruling → carry by consensus.
5
Consider negative rulings
If the ruling is negative (e.g., 'do not emancipate'), mutlaq is never carried to muqayyad by consensus, as it is possible to act on both by abstaining entirely.

فرہ کو آزاد نہ کرنا لم يحمل اتفاقا تو یہاں مطلق محمول علی المقید نہیں ہوگا یہ بالاتفاق ہے کیونکہ ان کے درمیان جمع ممکن ہے بان لا یعتق اصلا کہ کچھ بھی ازاد نہ کرے نہ کا فرد نہ بغیر کافرہ اسی طرح عمل
And negation of the necessary necessitates negation of the necessitated, and the necessary was transfer of ownership, so when he negated transfer of an infidel, this negation is necessary, thus emancipation of an infidel which is its necessitated is also negated. [Text?] He said: 'Do not emancipate on my behalf an infidel slave,' so this would necessitate restriction of the first ruling, i.e., 'Emancipate on my behalf a slave,' so this would necessitate the obligation of emancipating a believing slave. This is the difference regarding 'Do not emancipate on my behalf' — this is a ruling not mentioned in the speech, but 'Do not transfer ownership...' entails it because negation of the necessary entails negation of the necessitated. And if the ruling is one (i.e., the ruling in which absolute and restricted appear, if the ruling is single and unified), then it is seen: if the incident differs (e.g., expiation of oath and expiation of murder), for expiation of oath the manumission of a slave is stated absolutely, and the restriction 'believing' is not present; for expiation of murder, manumission of a believing slave is stated, which is restricted. According to us, the absolute is not carried to the restricted; rather, the absolute remains on its absoluteness and the restricted on its restriction. Therefore, for expiation of oath, any slave – whether believing or not – its manumission suffices; for expiation of murder, only manumission of a believing slave is valid. Al-Shafi'i carries (the absolute to the restricted) – in his view, the absolute is carried to the restricted in this case, whether analogy requires it or not. Some of them added: if analogy necessitates carrying, then carry; otherwise not, so they have two opinions. And if the incident is unified (i.e., the incident is also single and unified, like the ruling being one, as in sadaqat al-fitr where both absolute and restricted occur), then it is seen: if they enter on the cause (e.g., 'Pay from every free and slave' – in this text the restriction 'Muslim' is not present; another text: 'from every free and slave from the Muslims' – here the restriction 'from the Muslims' is mentioned. So both absolute and restricted enter on the cause because the cause of obligation for sadaqat al-fitr is a human head. Two texts have come: one indicating that the head is an absolute cause, and that is the hadith 'Pay for every free and child'; the other that the head is a Muslim head. So according to us, the absolute is not carried to the restricted; rather, each must be acted upon because there is no contradiction in causes. So a slave – whether Muslim or non-Muslim – sadaqat al-fitr must be paid for him, contrary to Imam Shafi'i who disagrees – in his view, the absolute is carried to the restricted, so only for a Muslim slave is sadaqat al-fitr obligatory upon the master. And if the absolute and restricted enter on the ruling in the case of unified incident (i.e., when the incident is single and unified, and the absolute and restricted enter on the ruling, not on the cause), then at that time the absolute is carried to the restricted by consensus between the two groups. For example, 'fast three days' as expiation – this text gives the ruling of fasting three days absolutely; and the reading of Ibn Mas'ud which is well-known has 'three consecutive days' – so it is restricted with the condition of consecutiveness. Here, by consensus, the absolute is carried to the restricted because it is impossible to combine between them (since the absolute permits non-consecutive fasting, the restricted prohibits it). This is when the ruling is affirmative. If the ruling is negative, then the absolute is not carried to the restricted by consensus. For example, 'Do not emancipate a slave' – this is absolute (i.e., do not emancipate any slave); and 'Do not emancipate an infidel slave' – this is restricted. Here, the absolute is not carried to the restricted by consensus because it is possible to combine between them by not emancipating at all (i.e., neither a slave nor an infidel slave). Similarly, act accordingly.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage discusses the usul rule of carrying the absolute (mutlaq) to the restricted (muqayyad) in Hanafi fiqh. The author explains the conditions under which this transfer occurs, particularly when the ruling and the incident are either the same or different, and whether the texts enter on the cause or on the ruling. The Hanafi position distinguishes between cases: if the ruling is the same but the incident differs (e.g., kaffarah yamin and kaffarah qatl), the absolute is not carried to the restricted; each text operates independently. If both ruling and incident are the same, but the texts enter on the cause, the absolute is also not carried because multiple causes can exist for a single ruling. However, if the texts enter on the ruling itself, then the absolute is carried to the restricted by consensus. In negative rulings, the absolute is never carried to the restricted. The passage contrasts the Hanafi view with that of Imam al-Shafi'i, who generally carries the absolute to the restricted in all cases unless ruled otherwise by some Shafi'is. The discussion is a typical Hanafi usul analysis focusing on textual interpretation and avoiding unnecessary restriction.

Key Terms
مطلق (mutlaq): A text that indicates a ruling without any qualifying condition.
مقید (muqayyad): A text that indicates a ruling with an additional qualifying condition.
سبب (sabab): The cause or occasion for a ruling; here, the head (for sadaqat al-fitr) or the acts (kaffarah).
حکم (hukm): The legal ruling itself (e.g., obligation to free a slave).
حادثہ (hadithah): The incident or event that triggers the ruling (e.g., oath, murder, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when both the ruling and the incident are the same, and the absolute and restricted texts enter on the ruling, what is the ruling?
A) The absolute is carried to the restricted by consensus.
B) The absolute remains on its absoluteness and the restricted on its restriction.
C) The absolute is carried only if qiyas necessitates it.
D) The absolute is not carried because the texts enter on the cause.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school carries mutlaq to muqayyad only when texts enter on the ruling with unified incident and ruling; otherwise, each text applies independently.

The Hanafi Position on Carrying Mutlaq upon Muqayyad
Hanafi rebuttal of Shafi'i rule on mutlaq/muqayyad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on carrying mutlaq upon muqayyad and apply the condition of ta'arud to determine when carrying is necessary.

Core Idea
A mutlaq is not carried upon a muqayyad unless genuine conflict (ta'arud) exists; otherwise, each text operates independently.

In Usul al-Fiqh, a classic dispute revolves around whether an absolute (mutlaq) text should be interpreted in light of a qualified (muqayyad) text on the same matter. The Shafi'i school argues that the muqayyad, being specific, takes priority. The Hanafi school, as presented here by the commentator, argues that this is only valid when there is actual conflict between the texts. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position and its proofs, highlighting the principle of applying both texts (i'mal al-dalilayn) and the avoidance of unnecessary hardship (haraj).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What exactly is the dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i regarding mutlaq and muqayyad? Clarify the point of ikhtilaf.
The issue is whether a mutlaq text must be interpreted as qualified by a muqayyad on the same subject.
2
State the Shafi'i position and its basis
Shafi'i claim: the qualified is 'speaking' (natiq) so it takes precedence over the silent (sakit) absolute. Also, qualification adds a description that extends by analogy.
Understand the opposing argument fully before evaluating the Hanafi response.
3
State the Hanafi response
Hanafi acceptance is conditional: only when there is genuine ta'arud (same incident, same ruling). Otherwise, each text is applied in its own context.
This is the key differentiating principle.
4
Examine the Hanafi proofs
Four main proofs: Quran (5:101), Ibn Abbas' statement, Companions' practice (Umar on ummahat al-nisa), and the principle of i'mal al-dalilayn.
Trace each proof in the passage.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
How does this usul difference affect actual fiqh rulings? The example of prohibiting marriage to the wife's mother (mutlaq) vs step-daughter (muqayyad).
Practical consequences matter for fatwa.

Will be done? So it is not freed at all. Because the absolute is silent from qualification and the qualified is speaking, so this speaking is more worthy than the silent. Hence the silent is carried upon the speaking. We say in response: This is a response in the form of 'affirming the positive'—yes, we accept that the qualified is more worthy, but this is only when the absolute and qualified conflict. And there is no conflict; conflict occurs only when the incident is one and the ruling is one, like in the case of three consecutive days. Apart from that, in other places there is no conflict. And because the qualification is an addition—this is the proof of the latter school who said that if analogy requires, then the absolute is carried upon the qualified. Summary of the proof: The qualification adds a description, and qualification by description runs in the course of a condition (shart). So when the ruling is negated, then absence of the description necessitates absence of the ruling, because specification by conditions from other than it necessitates negation of the ruling according to al-Shafi'i. And that is because negation of the ruling is the purport of the qualified text, so it is a legal ruling, and legal rulings are extended by analogy to similars. So in the text, negation of the ruling from other than the qualified is established by the text, and since the similars are in a single genus, they are analogous to each other, so this ruling is established in the analogical instance via analogy. And we have the saying of Allah: 'Do not ask about things...' [Surah al-Ma'idah 5:101]—this is a proof for us, because the verse indicates that the absolute continues upon its absoluteness and is not carried upon the qualified, because making an addition of qualification by oneself causes aggravation and hardship, and causes displeasure, as in the case of the cow of the Children of Israel: initially any cow would suffice, but they made it severe upon themselves, so Allah increased it upon them. And the mentioned verse prohibits this kind of aggravating and causing displeasure. Ibn Abbas said: 'Leave what Allah has left ambiguous, and follow what Allah has made clear.' This is the second proof: Ibn Abbas says: What Allah has left ambiguous, leave it as ambiguous; do not make any qualification or specification upon it from yourselves. And what Allah has explained and clarified, follow it. And since the absolute is ambiguous and the qualified is specified, we are not authorized to carry the absolute upon the qualified from ourselves. And the majority of the Companions did not qualify 'mothers of women'—this is the third proof: most Companions, among them Umar, left the prohibition of the mothers of the married women absolute, and did not accept the addition of the condition of consummation there, but kept the absolute upon its absoluteness and the qualified upon its qualification. The detail: Allah in the Quran mentioned 'your wives' mothers' among those whose marriage is forbidden, and left it absolute without mentioning the condition of consummation. Similarly, He made forbidden marriage with step-daughters: that is, the wife's daughter from a previous husband, whether he had intercourse with the mother or divorced her. Here, the condition of consummation is mentioned: it is forbidden to marry the step-daughter only if her mother has been consummated with after marriage. If a man married a woman but she died before consummation or was divorced, then he may marry her daughter from a previous husband—that step-daughter—because the Quran says: 'and your step-daughters under your guardianship, of those women you have entered into [i.e., consummated] with' (Surah al-Nisa 4:23). So here the condition of consummation is present. The majority of Companions did not accept to add this condition to the mothers of wives, but left the absolute as absolute and the qualified as qualified. Umar used to say: 'The mother of the woman is [forbidden] in the Book of Allah, so make her forbidden.' So it is established that the absolute is not carried upon the qualified. And because giving effect to both proofs—this is the fourth proof: summary is that it is obligatory to give effect to both proofs as much as possible. That is, it is obligatory to act upon both proofs and bring them into operation. And the way to do that is to act upon each in its own context. This can only happen by letting the absolute continue upon its absoluteness and the qualified upon its qualification, because if the absolute is carried upon the qualified, then that results in nullifying the absolute. Because the absolute allows both the qualified and the unqualified, whereas carrying upon the qualified nullifies the permissibility of the unqualified, even though that permissibility was the demand of the absolute, which becomes nullified. Therefore, what the Shafi'is say—that by acting upon the qualified, the absolute is also acted upon because the qualified contains the absolute—is not correct.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents a classic Hanafi vs Shafi'i dispute on whether an absolute (mutlaq) text should be interpreted as qualified (muqayyad) by a separate qualified text on the same subject. The Shafi'i position argues that the qualified text is 'speaking' (natiq) and therefore takes precedence over the absolute which is 'silent' (sakit) on the qualification. The Hanafi response begins by accepting this principle only when there is actual conflict (ta'arud) between the two texts, which they argue occurs only when the same incident and same ruling are under discussion—like the example of three consecutive days (referring to the fasting of kaffarah). In such a case, carrying the absolute upon the qualified is necessary. However, in other cases there is no conflict because each text applies to its own context. Then the author presents the Hanafi proofs: 1) Quranic verse (5:101) prohibiting unnecessary questioning, and the story of the cow of the Children of Israel, showing that adding restrictions causes hardship; 2) Statement of Ibn Abbas commanding to leave ambiguous texts ambiguous and follow only what is clearly explained; 3) Practice of the majority of Companions, especially Umar, who did not add the condition of consummation to the prohibition of mothers-in-law (ummahat al-nisa) while it was added for step-daughters (rabai'b); 4) The principle of applying both proofs simultaneously: acting upon both requires each to operate in its own field; carrying mutlaq upon muqayyad nullifies part of the mutlaq's effect. This is a mature usul dispute combining textual proof (naqli) and rational consistency (tarjih). The Hanafi position is that mutlaq and muqayyad are independent unless there is genuine ta'arud, which is rare.

Key Terms
مطلق (mutlaq): A general (unrestricted) term that includes all its individuals without specification.
مقید (muqayyad): A restricted term that adds a condition or quality limiting its application.
تعارض (ta'arud): Contradiction between two textual proofs when both demand opposite rulings for the same incident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when is it necessary to carry the mutlaq upon the muqayyad?
A) Whenever a qualified text exists on the same subject.
B) Only when there is actual conflict (ta'arud) between them, i.e., same incident and same ruling.
C) Only when the mutlaq is from the Quran and the muqayyad is from the Sunnah.
D) Never, because the mutlaq is always applied without restriction.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school teaches that a mutlaq text is not automatically restricted by a muqayyad; only genuine conflict (ta'arud) allows carrying the former upon the latter.

Why Taqyid al-Mutlaq by Qiyas Differs from Takhsis al-Amm
Refutation of the analogy between takhsis al-amm and taqyid al-mutlaq by qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between takhsis al-amm by qiyas and taqyid al-mutlaq by qiyas according to Hanafi usul, and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument.

Core Idea
Takhsis al-amm by qiyas requires prior qati' takhsis; taqyid al-mutlaq by qiyas does not, so the analogy fails.

In this lesson, we examine a key usul dispute: can restricting an absolute term (taqyid al-mutlaq) be performed by qiyas, analogously to specifying a general term (takhsis al-amm)? The opponent claims yes, citing agreed permissibility of takhsis al-amm by qiyas. The Hanafi school responds by showing that takhsis al-amm by qiyas itself is conditional upon prior qati' takhsis, so the analogy is invalid. Understanding this distinction clarifies the Hanafi position that qiyas cannot contradict a nass without prior definitive specification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two operations
First, define takhsis al-amm (specifying a general term to exclude some individuals) and taqyid al-mutlaq (restricting an absolute term by adding a condition). Both are interpretive methods that limit the scope of a text.
The opponent claims they are analogous.
2
State the opponent's claim
The opponent (Imam al-Razi) argues that since takhsis al-amm by qiyas is agreed upon, and the general's indication is stronger, then by analogy taqyid al-mutlaq should also be permissible by qiyas.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
3
Understand the opponent's proof structure
The opponent's qiyas: asl = takhsis al-amm by qiyas (permissible); far' = taqyid al-mutlaq by qiyas; illa = both involve limiting the scope of a text using a weaker proof (qiyas), and the stronger indication of the general makes specification more justified.
The opponent relies on the idea that what is permissible for the stronger should be permissible for the weaker.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi responder denies the permissibility of takhsis al-amm by qiyas in an absolute sense. He says it is only allowed after the general has been specified once by a qati' (definitive) proof. Only then can qiyas further specify. Here, in taqyid al-mutlaq, the restriction is being established initially by qiyas, without prior qati' text.
Thus, the illa is not present because the condition (prior qati' takhsis) is missing in the far'.
5
Recognize the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference applies in cases like kaffarat al-yamin: the absolute 'slave' is not restricted to 'believing slave' by qiyas in Hanafi school, whereas the Shafi'i school, allowing taqyid by qiyas, would require a believing slave.
This shows how a seemingly abstract usul dispute has concrete fiqh implications.

وليس حمل المطلق على المقيد لتخصيص العام كما زعموا ي جائز بالقياس . هذا جواب الخير و توقيت جواب ہے اس دلیل کا جو امام رازی نے محصول میں پیش فرمائی ہے اس امر یہ کہ حمل مطلق على المقید جائز ہے جب قیاس اس And the carrying of the absolute upon the restricted for the specification of the general, as they claim, is not permissible by qiyas. This is the answer to the proof that Imam al-Razi presented in al-Mahsul, that carrying the absolute upon the restricted is permissible when qiyas requires it, and that proof is that the indication of the general over individuals is superior to the indication of the absolute over individuals, because the indication of the general over individuals is primary, since its definition includes comprehensiveness of individuals, while the indication of the absolute over individuals is subsidiary, because the intent from the absolute is the essence itself or an unspecified part that can apply to many parts. So the indication of the absolute over individuals is alternative, not comprehensive. And the general is specified. And specification of the general by qiyas is a matter agreed upon between you and us, i.e., it is accepted by consensus that specification of the general by qiyas is possible, so it is necessary to restrict the absolute. Then it is proven that restricting the absolute is also correct by qiyas in your view. So he (the Hanafi responder) answered by denying the permissibility of specification, saying: Carrying the absolute upon the restricted is not like the specification of the general, because in our view, specification of the general by qiyas is not absolutely permissible. Rather, specification by qiyas is only permissible when the general has been specified once by a definitive (qati) proof, then secondarily it can be specified by a qiyas proof. And here, in the issue of carrying the absolute upon the restricted, you (the opponents) establish the restriction initially by qiyas, i.e., you add a restriction to the absolute from the start by qiyas. It is not that the absolute was first restricted by a text and then you restrict it a second time by qiyas. When it is not so, your qiyas becomes a nullifier of the absolute text, and that is impermissible. So the conclusion: According to us, specification of the general is not absolute in every case; rather it is specified only when it has been specified first by a definitive proof, then secondarily by qiyas. And in the issue of carrying the absolute upon the restricted, the absolute was not first restricted by a text, so that it could be restricted first by a definitive text and then secondarily by qiyas. Rather, your dispute with us is whether the absolute can be restricted initially by qiyas: you believe it is permissible, and we believe it is not. Therefore, this is not like the issue of specification of the general, so your proof is corrupt.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage addresses the opponent's claim that restricting a mutlaq by qiyas is analogous to specifying a general term (takhsis al-amm) by qiyas, which is agreed upon. The Hanafi responder denies the analogy. He explains that specification of the general by qiyas is only permissible in the Hanafi school after the general has already been specified by a definitive (qati') textual proof. Then, as a secondary step, qiyas can further specify it. However, in the case of restricting the mutlaq, the opponent is trying to establish the restriction initially by qiyas, without any prior qati' text that restricts it. That would nullify the absolute text's ruling, which is impermissible. Thus the analogy fails, and the opponent's proof is unsound. This highlights a key difference in usul: Hanafis allow takhsis al-amm by qiyas only after prior takhsis by qati', while Shafi'is may allow it directly. Similarly, taqyid al-mutlaq is not permitted by pure qiyas in this scenario.

Key Terms
اختصاص العام (takhsis al-amm): Specifying a general term to exclude some of its individuals.
حمل المطلق على المقيد (haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad): Carrying the absolute term to the restricted one, i.e., interpreting the absolute as restricted by analogy.
تقييد المطلق (taqyid al-mutlaq): Restricting an absolute term by adding a condition or qualification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, when is specification of a general term (takhsis al-amm) by qiyas permissible?
A) At all times, as long as the analogy is sound.
B) Only if the general term is from the Quran.
C) Only after the general has already been specified by a definitive text.
D) Never; qiyas cannot specify a general term.

Key Takeaway
Taqyid al-mutlaq by qiyas is impermissible in Hanafi usul because it would restrict the absolute text without prior qati' specification, unlike takhsis al-amm which requires such prior specification.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
Restriction of Absolute by Qiyas: Hanafi Position vs. Opponents
Defending Hanafi position on taqyid al-mutlaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prohibits initial taqyid al-mutlaq by qiyas and distinguish it from takhsis al-amm, applying the principle to concrete cases.

Core Idea
Initial restriction of an absolute by analogy is invalid; it requires a prior definitive text.

This passage tackles one of the more subtle disputes between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: whether analogy (qiyas) can initially restrict an absolute term (mutlaq). The Hanafi position is that it cannot—unlike specification of a general term (takhsis al-amm), which can be done by qiyas after a textual specification. The author defends this view, addresses counterexamples from expiations and zakat, and clarifies that apparent Hanafi restrictions are based on texts, not pure analogy. This lesson will walk you through the argument and its applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the opening sentences: the Hanafi position is that taqyid al-mutlaq by qiyas is impermissible initially (ab initio). The opponent (Shafi'i) allows it, analogizing to takhsis al-amm.
2
Trace the analogical argument
The opponent argues: just as a general term (amm) can be specified by qiyas after an initial textual specification, so an absolute term (mutlaq) should be restrictable by qiyas. Identify the shared illa: both involve adding a condition without a direct text.
The qiyas is from takhsis al-amm to taqyid al-mutlaq.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response distinguishes: in takhsis al-amm, the general has already been specified by a definitive text before the qiyasi specification. In taqyid al-mutlaq, no such prior textual restriction exists, so applying qiyas would effectively abrogate the absolute text. Therefore, the analogy fails.
The key is the presence of prior textual specification.
4
Evaluate the specific cases
The author addresses three apparent counterexamples: (1) expiation for killing requiring a believing slave, (2) soundness condition in oath expiation, (3) restriction of 'camels' to 'pasturing camels' in zakat, and (4) restriction of 'witness' to 'just witness' in contracts. For each, determine whether the restriction is by text or by qiyas.
The Hanafis argue that these are based on other texts or on the inherent meaning of the absolute (e.g., 'slave' excludes the deficient).
5
Synthesize the principle
Formulate the rule: An absolute term can only be restricted by a definitive text (nass qat'i) initially. After it has been restricted by text, a second restriction by qiyas is permissible. Specification of a general term follows a similar pattern but is more permissive because the general always undergoes initial textual specification.
This principle preserves the primacy of textual evidence in usul al-fiqh.

ہمارا مختلف فیہ مسئلہ ہے۔ یہ تقیدات نصوص واردہ کی بنا پر میں اس قسم کی تقیدات صحیح ہیں ۔ صل حكم المشترك التأمل فيه الخ مشترک جس کی تعریف پہلے گزر چکی ہے اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ التأمل فيه یعنی اس میں تامل اور غور و فکر کریں اور یہ تامل کبھی نفس صیغہ میں ہوگا اور کبھی دلائل اور امارات یعنی علامات میں ہوگا حتی يترجح بہ غور فکر اس لئے ہوگا تا کہ اس کے معانی متعددہ میں سے کوئی ایک معنی ترجیح حاصل کے جائے ولا يستعمل في اكثر الم یعنی مشترک جن معانی متعددہ کے لئے موضوع ہے تو بوقت اطلاق اور استعمال کے اس کی استعمال صرف ایک معنی میں ہوگی اور فی اطلاق واجدہ ایک معنی سے زیادہ میں اس کی استعمال نہیں کی جائے گی۔ لا حقیقۃ اس معنی سے زائد میں اس کی استعمال نہ حقیقتاً ہو سکتی ہے نہ مجازاً یعنی یوں کہ ایک معنی نئے زائد اور اکثر میں اس کی استعمال کی جائے اور اس استعمال کو حقیقت قرار دیا جائے بایں طور کہ اپنے معنی موضوع کہ میں مستعمل ہے ایسے نہیں
Specification of the general once with a definitive proof, then secondly with analogical proof is possible. And here the restriction is established initially by analogy, and in the issue of carrying the absolute upon the restricted, you [opponents] initially add a restriction to the absolute by analogy, whereas it was not restricted initially by a text; it is not that the absolute was already restricted by a text and then its restriction is done secondarily by analogy. When it is not so, then your analogy becomes invalid for the absolute text, and that is impermissible. So the conclusion: specification of the general according to us is not absolutely and in every circumstance; rather it is specified when its specification first occurs with a definitive proof, then secondarily with analogical proof. And the issue of carrying the absolute upon the restricted: the absolute was not restricted initially by a text; your and our disagreement is in the case where the restriction of the absolute is done initially by analogy: you hold its permissibility and we hold its impermissibility. Therefore this is not like the issue of specification of the general, so your proof is invalid. And there is a difference between expiations: when this general ruling has been mentioned that restriction of the absolute by analogy is impermissible, then he descended to this particular issue, i.e., the issue of expiations, and explained that in this issue there is another preventer from analogy besides the text on the thing being analogized. That is that killing is among the greatest sins, therefore severity is appropriate in it, so it is permissible to stipulate faith [in its expiation]. And for a sin lower than this greatest sin, the condition is not made. Because intensification of the expiation is according to the gravity of the offense. And the oath expiation is not like killing in sin, so analogizing a light offense to a severe one is not correct. Do not say: 'You restricted the slave with soundness...' This is a response to an objection raised against us Hanafis. The conclusion: When in the expiation of an oath the ruling was of an absolute slave, why did you restrict it with soundness? That it not be blind or crippled but sound of limbs. So you too have restricted the absolute; so we too, if we add the condition of faith, there is no harm.' He replied: 'But the absolute cannot include what is deficient in the meaning of being a slave and lacks the genus of benefit; that is not a slave.' By 'lacks the genus of benefit' is meant that the genus of benefit is completely absent: e.g., seeing — both eyes missing; e.g., grasping — both hands useless or amputated; legs useless — the benefit of walking is completely absent. It is deficient in being a slave; the absolute does not include it, because the absolute is directed to the complete. The complete in what the name is applied to. This is what is meant by the famous saying: when the absolute is used, the perfect individual is intended, like absolute water — it does not include rose water because it is not complete in being water. Therefore carrying the absolute upon the perfect individual is not a restriction; rather it is the realization of the referent of the absolute. Do not say: 'You restricted then the Prophet's statement...' This is an objection that you have also restricted the absolute in various places: one is the Prophet's statement 'In five camels, zakat' — the word 'camels' is absolute; and another hadith says 'In five pasturing camels, zakat'. Based on this second hadith, you restricted the first hadith with the condition of pasturing, even though the absolute and the restricted both enter upon the cause [of obligation] because the nisab is the cause of zakat. And when the absolute and restricted enter upon the cause, the madhhab according to you is that the absolute is not carried upon the restricted even if the incident is the same, as in sadaqat al-fitr. And you restricted the verse 'And call to witness when you transact' — this is the second objection. Its gist is that 'And call to witness when you transact' is an absolute witness, no condition of being just. And the other verse 'And call to witness two just men from among you' contains the condition of justice. You have restricted the absolute in 'And call to witness when you transact' with this condition, even though they are two different incidents. The absolute occurs in transaction (sale) and the restricted occurs in divorces, as in the Quran: 'Then when they have reached their term, retain them in kindness or separate from them in kindness, and call to witness two just men from among you.' He responded to both objections: that the restriction to pasturing is based on the Prophet's statement: 'There is no sadaqah on draught, pack, or fodder animals' i.e., animals used for work, like oxen for ploughing and beasts of burden, and fodder animals that are fed at home and not sent out to pasture — no zakat on them. And the condition of justice in witnesses is based on the statement: 'If a sinful person brings you news, investigate, lest you harm a people out of ignorance.' The gist of the answer is that these restrictions are not by analogy, which is our point of disagreement. These restrictions are based on incoming texts, thus such restrictions are correct. Then: The ruling of the common term: reflection upon it. The common term, whose definition has passed, its ruling is that one must reflect upon it. This reflection sometimes is on the word itself and sometimes on proofs and indications, so that through reflection one meaning among the multiple meanings may be given preponderance. And it is not used in more than one. That is, the common term is placed for multiple meanings; at the time of usage, it is used for only one meaning, and in a single usage it is not used for more than one meaning. Neither literally nor figuratively can it be used for more than this meaning, i.e., that it is used for a meaning beyond its own and for more, and that usage is considered literal — such that it is used in its subject meaning — this is not the case.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This is a passage from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work resolving a dispute about the permissibility of restricting an absolute term (taqyid al-mutlaq) by analogy (qiyas). The Hanafi position is that an absolute term cannot be initially restricted by analogy; it must first be restricted by a definitive text (nass qat'i). In contrast, specification of a general term (takhsis al-'amm) can be done by analogy after an initial specification by a definitive text. The author first distinguishes between the two cases: opponents (likely Shafi'is) try to analogize taqyid al-mutlaq to takhsis al-'amm. The Hanafis reject this because in taqyid al-mutlaq the restriction would be made initially by analogy, which is invalid. Then the author addresses the specific case of expiations (kafarat): why does the Hanafi school allow conditioning faith in the expiation for killing but not for oaths? The answer lies in a preventer from analogy: the text on the maqis (the thing being analogized) combined with the severity of killing, making it appropriate to add a condition. For oaths, the offense is lighter, so analogy to killing fails. The author then responds to objections that Hanafis themselves restrict absolutes in other contexts: (1) in zakat on camels, they restrict 'camels' to 'pasturing camels' based on another hadith, not by a pure analogy; (2) in witnessing transactions, they restrict 'witness' to 'just witness' based on another verse and hadith. These restrictions are based on other texts, not on independent analogy, so they do not contradict the Hanafi principle that initial taqyid by qiyas is impermissible. This passage displays classic Hanafi usul methodology: distinguishing between types of textual restrictions, using istihsan or qiyas khafi to explain exceptions, and defending the school against apparent inconsistencies.

Key Terms
مطلق (mutlaq): A term that indicates a genus without any condition of a quality (qayd).
مقید (muqayyad): A term that has a condition or qualification attached, opposite of mutlaq.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to a shared effective cause (illa).
تخصیص (takhsis): Specification: restricting the scope of a general term to exclude some of its individuals.
تقیید (taqyid): Restriction: limiting an absolute term by adding a condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the Hanafi position on the initial restriction of an absolute term (taqyid al-mutlaq) by analogy (qiyas)?
A) It is permissible in all cases because qiyas is a valid source.
B) It is impermissible because the absolute already implies a perfect individual and cannot be restricted.
C) It is impermissible because it would require a prior textual restriction, which is absent initially.
D) It is permissible only in cases of expiations (kafarat).

Key Takeaway
An absolute term (mutlaq) cannot be initially restricted by analogy (qiyas); it must first be restricted by a definitive text; only then may qiyas be used for further specification.

Ruling on the Common Term: Reflection and Single Meaning
Stating the two core rulings on the use and interpretation of the common term (mushtarak).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two rulings regarding the interpretation and use of a mushtarak term, and apply the requirement of ta'amul to determine the intended meaning.

Core Idea
A common term must be investigated and can only convey one meaning at a time.

You have learned that a mushtarak (common term) is a word placed for multiple distinct meanings. But how do we interpret such a word when it appears in a text? This passage provides two fundamental rulings: first, we must reflect (ta'amul) on the word and its context to find which meaning is intended; second, we cannot use the mushtarak for more than one meaning in a single instance, neither literally nor figuratively. Understanding these rulings is crucial for proper legal reasoning (istinbat) because many key terms in the Quran and Hadith are mushtarak.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dual ruling
The passage states two distinct rulings: (1) reflection (ta'amul) is obligatory when encountering a mushtarak; (2) the mushtarak must be restricted to a single meaning in any one usage. Note the wording: 'التأمل فيه' and 'لا يستعمل في أكثر'.
Memorize these two components.
2
Comprehend the process of ta'amul
Ta'amul can involve examining the word itself (sigha) or external evidence (qara'in). The goal is to give preponderance (tarjih) to one meaning over others. Identify what types of evidence are mentioned.
Tarjih is a core usul skill.
3
Understand the restriction on multiple meanings
The passage explicitly prohibits using a mushtarak for more than one meaning in a single utterance, both literally ('لا حقيقة') and figuratively ('لا مجازاً'). Consider why this is: a single lafz cannot be haqiqah in two different realities simultaneously.
Contrast with figurative usage that might allow multiple intentions? Not here.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take the word 'qar'' ('iddah verse). It is mushtarak between menstruation and purity. Using the process in this passage, explain what a Hanafi jurist must do and why they cannot mean both.
This solidifies the abstract rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صل حكم المشترك التأمل فيه الخ مشترک جس کی تعریف پہلے گزر چکی ہے اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ التأمل فيه یعنی اس میں تامل اور غور و فکر کریں اور یہ تامل کبھی نفس صیغہ میں ہوگا اور کبھی دلائل اور امارات یعنی علامات میں ہوگا حتی يترجح بہ غور فکر اس لئے ہوگا تا کہ اس کے معانی متعددہ میں سے کوئی ایک معنی ترجیح حاصل کے جائے ولا يستعمل في اكثر الم یعنی مشترک جن معانی متعددہ کے لئے موضوع ہے تو بوقت اطلاق اور استعمال کے اس کی استعمال صرف ایک معنی میں ہوگی اور فی اطلاق واجدہ ایک معنی سے زیادہ میں اس کی استعمال نہیں کی جائے گی۔ لا حقیقۃ اس معنی سے زائد میں اس کی استعمال نہ حقیقتاً ہو سکتی ہے نہ مجازاً یعنی یوں کہ ایک معنی نئے زائد اور اکثر میں اس کی استعمال کی جائے اور اس استعمال کو حقیقت قرار دیا جائے بایں طور کہ اپنے معنی موضوع کہ میں مستعمل ہے ایسے نہیں
Section: The ruling of the common term (mushtarak): reflection upon it. The common term, whose definition has passed, its ruling is that one must reflect upon it. This reflection sometimes is on the word itself and sometimes on proofs and indications, so that through reflection one meaning among the multiple meanings may be given preponderance. And it is not used in more than one. That is, the common term is placed for multiple meanings; at the time of usage, it is used for only one meaning, and in a single usage it is not used for more than one meaning. Neither literally nor figuratively can it be used for more than this meaning, i.e., that it is used for a meaning beyond its own and for more, and that usage is considered literal — such that it is used in its subject meaning — this is not the case.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This is a focused definition of the ruling on the common term (mushtarak), a word that has multiple distinct meanings. The author states two key rulings: (1) When encountering a mushtarak in a text, the interpreter must investigate (tahmil) and reflect, using the word's form (sigha) and external evidence (dalail wa amarat) to determine which meaning is intended (tarjih). (2) The mushtarak can only be used for one meaning at a time; it cannot be used simultaneously for more than one meaning, neither literally nor figuratively. This means if someone uses a common term intending to convey multiple meanings, that usage is not considered valid as a literal or figurative usage. This is a standard Hanafi usul position, emphasizing that a single utterance cannot denote multiple distinct meanings simultaneously unless specified otherwise. The passage ends by negating the possibility of using the term for more than one meaning and considering that as the original meaning.

Key Terms
مشترك (mushtarak): A word that is placed (wad') for multiple distinct meanings, such that it has a common factor (ishitrak) among them.
تأمل (ta'amul): Deliberate reflection and investigation to determine which meaning of the mushtarak is intended, using linguistic and contextual clues.
ترجیح (tarjih): Giving preponderance to one meaning over others based on evidence (qara'in).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on using a common term (mushtarak) to convey more than one of its meanings in a single utterance?
A) It is permissible as a figurative usage if context allows.
B) It is permissible only if the word is used in its literal sense for all meanings.
C) It is not permissible; it cannot be used for more than one meaning, neither literally nor figuratively.
D) It is permissible only in legal contexts, not in everyday speech.

Key Takeaway
A mushtarak term requires investigation (ta'amul) and can only be used for one meaning in a single context.

Ruling on Mushtarak (Homonym) in Usul al-Fiqh
Explaining the ruling on mushtarak via rational analysis of coinage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a mushtarak word cannot be used haqiqatan for more than one meaning simultaneously, applying the Hanafi understanding of wad'.

Core Idea
A word coined for multiple meanings cannot be used literally for more than one at once—each wad' confines the word to a specific meaning.

This passage from the commentary on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwiḥ delves into the hukm (ruling) of al-mushtarak, a word that has been coined for multiple distinct meanings. The text uses a rational analysis of possible modes of coinage (wad') to demonstrate that using such a word for more than one meaning in a literal sense (haqiqatan) is impossible. This lesson will guide you through the author's argument, clarifying the relationship between wad', haqiqah, and the necessity of contextual preponderance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling (hukm) of mushtarak
The text states that one must reflect (ta'ammul) on the wording and evidences to give preponderance to one meaning. The ruling is that the word is not used in more than one meaning simultaneously.
2
Analyze the three possible coinage modes
The language coiner (wadi') could have coined the word: (a) for each meaning with condition of isolation, (b) for the sum of meanings, or (c) for each meaning absolutely.
3
Test each mode against the permitted usage
The author argues that (b) is false because then the word would not be a true homonym, and (a) would prohibit using both meanings together. Only (c) or (a) alone allows haqiqi usage for one meaning.
4
Conclude the impossibility of multiple literal usage
Since each wad' specifies a word to a particular meaning, using it for more than one meaning simultaneously violates the wad', making it impossible haqiqatan.

اس کی وضع جیسے مجموع کے لئے ہے اس طرح اس کی وضع لكل واحد من المعنيين بھی ہے تو جوابا کہا جائے گا کہ اس صورت میں اس کی استعمال في المجموع یہ بھی استعمال في احد المعنیین ہے نہ کہ فی اکثر من معنی واحد وان وجد الاول والثالث الا اگر مالی اور تیسری صورت یعنی اس مشترک کی وضع ہر ایک معنی کے لئے بشرط الانفراد ہو یا علی الاطلاق ہو تو ثبت المدعی کہ اس وقت ہمارا دعویٰ ثابت ہو جائے گا کہ اس کی استعمال فی اکثر من معنی واحد علی سبیل الحقیقتہ نہیں ہو سکتی لان الوضع خصیص اللفظ بالمعنی کیونکہ وضع کے اندر تخصیص لفظ بالمعنی ہوئی ہے بایں طور کہ وہ لفظ اس معنی پر مقصور اور محصور ر ہے اس سے متجاوز ہو کر کیسی بغیر کی طرف نہ جائے اور بوقت استعمال کے غیر کا ارادہ نہ کیا جائے فکل وضع بوجب تو سیر وضع واجب قرار دیتی ہے کہ لفظ کے ساتھ صرف وہ معنی مراد لیا جائے جو اس وضع کے ساتھ موضوع رہے دیو جب ان يكون هذا المعنی اور وضع اس امر کوہ بھی واجب کرتی ہے کہ یہی معنی تمام مراد ہو فاعتبار كل من الوضعين
Section: The ruling on the homonym is to reflect upon it. The homonym, whose definition has passed earlier, its ruling is that one must reflect and ponder, and this reflection is sometimes in the wording itself and sometimes in the evidences and indicators, so that one meaning among its multiple meanings may be given preponderance. And it is not used in more than one[?] i.e., the homonym, which is posited for multiple meanings, at the time of absolute use and usage, its usage is only in one meaning, and not in more than one meaning. Not as a reality: in excess of this meaning, its usage cannot be either as reality or as metaphor. That is, if it is used in more than one meaning and that usage is considered real, in the sense that it is used in its original meaning, it cannot be. So a proof is given negating its real usage: because it was not posited for the collection. Therefore, using it in more than one meaning simultaneously is using it in the sum of meanings, which is not what it was posited for, so it cannot be real. Elaborating this, he said: Know that the coiner of language is not free from either having placed it for each of the multiple meanings without the other (i.e., when positing for one meaning, that meaning is considered with the condition of isolation, and conjunction with the other is not intended), or for each one with the other (i.e., its positing is for each meaning together with the other; in this case its positing is for the sum of the two meanings as a whole), or for each one absolutely (i.e., its positing is for each meaning without restriction, neither with condition of isolation nor condition of conjunction). If it is the first (i.e., each meaning was posited without the other, conditionally isolated), then it is not permissible that one meaning be intended together with the other (i.e., the other also intended at the same time). That would be against the positing. The second (positing for the sum) is not realized because the coiner did not posit it for the sum as a whole. Otherwise, its usage in one meaning without the other in a real sense would not be valid, because then the word would be used in other than what it was posited for. But this (usage in one meaning) is valid by agreement. So it is known that the homonym's positing is not for the sum of meanings. Also, on the assumption of occurrence (i.e., if hypothetically its positing were for the sum as a whole), then its usage in the sum would be usage in one meaning (because the sum is one meaning). In reality, in this case, the very notion of being homonymous would be confused, because then there would be no multiplicity of meanings, which is essential for a homonym. If it is said that its positing is for the sum as well as for each individual meaning, then the reply would be: in that case, using it in the sum is also using it in one of the meanings, not in more than one meaning. Even if the first and third possibilities exist? Except if for me? And the third situation (i.e., the homonym's positing is for each meaning either conditionally isolated or absolutely), then our claim is proven: that its usage in more than one meaning in a real sense is not possible, because positing is a specification of the word for a meaning, in the sense that the word is confined and restricted to that meaning, not exceeding to another, and at the time of usage, other than it is not intended. For every positing necessitates that the word be used only for the meaning for which it is posited. And this positing also necessitates that this meaning be entirely intended. So consideration of each of the two positing[?]
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling (hukm) for a mushtarak (homonym) - a word coined for multiple meanings. The ruling is that one must reflect (ta'ammul) on the context and evidences to determine which meaning is intended; the word cannot be used haqiqatan (literally/truly) for more than one meaning simultaneously. The author then provides a detailed analysis of possible ways a homonym could be coined: (1) each meaning individually with the condition of isolation, (2) each meaning together with the other (sum as a whole), or (3) each meaning absolutely (neither condition). He argues that the first scenario (isolation) would prohibit using the word for both meanings together; the second (sum) is factually false because then the word would not be a true homonym (it would be a single meaning) and usage in one meaning alone would be metaphorical; the only consistent scenario is the third (absolute) or the first if not combined. Ultimately, the conclusion is that a homonym cannot be used for more than one meaning at a time in a real (haqiqi) sense because each act of coining (wad') confines the word to a specific meaning, and using it for multiple meanings would violate that specification. The passage demonstrates rational argument based on the nature of language coinage.

Key Terms
وضع (wad'): The assignment of a word to a meaning by the language coiner.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal/real usage of a word in its original coined meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot a mushtarak word be used haqiqatan for more than one meaning at the same time?
A) Because it is against Arabic grammar.
B) Because the word was not coined for the sum of its meanings.
C) Because the word is always ambiguous.
D) Because the Prophet prohibited it.

Key Takeaway
A mushtarak word cannot be used haqiqatan for more than one meaning simultaneously because its coinage (wad') specifies it to each meaning individually, not to their collective sum.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
The Impossibility of Using a Common Word in Multiple Meanings Simultaneously
Refuting the possibility of using a mushtarak word in more than one meaning as haqiqah or majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three possible types of placement (wad') for a mushtarak word and explain why each precludes simultaneous usage in multiple meanings as haqiqah or majaz.

Core Idea
A word placed for multiple meanings cannot intend them all at once — the very nature of wad' forbids it.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, a common word (mushtarak) is defined as a word placed for more than one meaning. A key question arises: can such a word be used to mean all its meanings simultaneously? This passage presents the Hanafi position that it is impossible, whether as a reality (haqiqah) or as a metaphor (majaz). The reasoning is based on the nature of linguistic placement (wad') and the underlying purpose of commonality. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly interpreting ambiguous texts in the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios of placement
The author lists three possibilities: placement for each meaning with condition of singularity, placement for the totality, and placement for each meaning absolutely. Understand each scenario.
The second scenario (placement for totality) is rejected as non-existent for mushtarak.
2
Analyze why each scenario prevents simultaneous usage
For the first scenario, intending both meanings violates the condition of singularity. For the third, considering both placements leads to a contradiction (each meaning must be both isolated and together). The second scenario, if hypothetically true, would turn the word into a single meaning, destroying its commonality.
3
Understand the rational proof from the purpose of ishtirak
Whether the placer is Allah (for testing) or humans (for obfuscation), the purpose is only served when a single meaning is intended. If multiple meanings were intended simultaneously, the test or obfuscation would fail.
This is a teleological argument.
4
Apply to metaphorical usage
Using mushtarak in more than one meaning as majaz would require combining haqiqah (for each individual meaning) and majaz (for the totality) in one utterance, which is impossible because the same word cannot simultaneously be used in its placed meaning and in a non-placed meaning.
This is an additional argument against simultaneous metaphorical usage.

ساتھ موضوع رہے دیو جب ان يكون هذا المعنی اور وضع اس امر کوہ بھی واجب کرتی ہے کہ یہی معنی تمام مراد ہو فاعتبار كل من الوضعين تو بیک وقت ہر دونوں وضع کا اعتبار کرنا دوسری وضع کے اعتبار کے منافی ہوگا۔
