جائے تو اگر اطلاق واحد میں دونوں وضعین کا اعتبار کیا جائے تو ہر ایک معنی میں سحسب الارادہ انفراد عن الآخر بھی لازم اور اجتماع مع الاخر بھی تو یہ جمع بین القنافیین ہے بلکہ یہ لازم آئے گا کہ ہر ایک من المعنيين في حالة واحدة مراد بھی ہو اور غیر مراد بھی سود بها باطل بالضرورة ومن عرف سبب وقوع الاشتراك الجو لوگ وقوع اشتراک کے سبب کی معرفت رکھتے ہیں ان پر یہ مخفی نہیں ہے کہ اس کی استعمال معنیین میں نہیں ہو سکتی کیونکہ مشترک کی وضع لكل واحد من المعنیین ہوتی ہے اگر واضع اللہ تعالیٰ ہے تو مقصور ابتلا ہوتی ہے یعنی تعیین مراد کے لئے قرائن مخفیہ ہوتے ہیں۔ ان قرائن کی بنیاد پر عیاد سے کون جد و جہد کر کے تعیین مراد تک پہونچنے کی کوشش کر کے مستحق اجر ہوتا ہے تو یہ ابتلا اس وقت ہو سکتا ہے جب کی معانی مختلفہ میں سے صرف ایک معنی مراد ہو۔ اگر بیک وقت جمیع معانی مراد ہوں تو پھر ابتلا نہیں ہو سکتا۔ اگر واضع غیر اللہ تعالیٰ ہے یعنی عباد پھر یا تو اشتراک علی قصد الابہام ہوتا ہے یعنی ایک چیز کو مبہم کر دینا کہ سامع کو پوری مراد سمجھے میں نہ آوے تو یہ بھی اس وقت ہو سکتا ہے جبکہ ایک معنی مراد ہو ۔ اگر دونوں مراد ہوں تو پھر قصد ابہام پوری نہیں ہوتی۔ جیسے حضرت ابو بکر نے ہجرت کے دوران ایک سائل مشرک کو رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے متعلق کہا تھا جیل مہدینی السبیل تو اس سے سائل نے یہی سمجھا کہ ابوبکر راستہ سفر نہیں جانتے اور یہ شخص انھیں راستہ دکھا رہا ہے اور حضرت ابو بکر کی مراد کچھ اور تھی تو یہ ابہام اور امہام اس وقت ہو سکتا ہے جب اس کی استعمال ایک معنی میں ہو اگر دونوں معنی میں اس کی استعمال بیک وقت صحیح ہو تو پھر یہ الہام نہیں ہو سکتا۔ فقوله لانه لم يوضع للمجموع الج یہ ادھر اشارہ ہے کہ مشترک کی استعمال معین میں اس وقت صحیح ہو سکتی ہے جب کہ اس کی وضع مجموع کے لئے ہو۔ حالانکہ وضع للمجموع مشترک میں منتفی ہے۔ باقی جو دو صورتیں تھیں ان کے لحاظ سے اس کی استعمال دو معنیین میں بیک اطلاق نہیں ہو سکتی کما ذکرنا ولا مجازاً یعنی مشترک کی استعمال ایک معنی سے اکثر معانی میں بطریق مجاز بھی نہیں ہو سکتی۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں جمع بین الحقیقة والمجاز لازم آئے گی جو کہ باطل ہے ۔ اور وجہ لزوم یہ ہے کہ اگر ارادہ مجموع کا ہو تو یہ غیر موضوعا لہ ہے جو کہ مجازہ ہے۔ اور ہر ایک معنی مراد ہے تو یہ موضوع لہ ہے۔ تو اس وقت معنی حقیقی اور معنی مجازی کا ارادہ من لفظ واحد فی اطلاق واحد لازم آئے گا اور یہ جمع
If it were possible, then they presented a proof negating its real (haqiqi) usage: 'because it was not placed (wad') for the totality (al-majmu')' meaning its placement is not for the totality of meanings, so using it in more than one meaning simultaneously is using it in a totality of meanings, which is not what it was placed for, therefore it cannot be real. Elaborating on this, it was said: 'Know that the one who places (al-wadi') is not free from either having placed it for each of the multiple meanings without the other, i.e., when placing it for one meaning, that meaning is considered with the condition of singularity (bishart al-infrad), and intending together with the other is not intended; or for each one of them together with the other, i.e., in that case its placement is for the totality of the two meanings as a totality; or for each one of them absolutely (ala al-itlaq), i.e., its placement is for each meaning absolutely, neither considering the condition of singularity nor the condition of togetherness. If it is the first, i.e., if the first scenario is that its placement for each was without the other with the condition of singularity, then in this case it cannot be permissible that one meaning of the common word is intended together with the other, i.e., the other is also intended simultaneously. This is contrary to the placement. The second scenario, i.e., placement for the totality, is not actual and not established, because the placer did not place it for the totality as a totality. And otherwise it would not be correct, for then its usage in one meaning without the other as a reality would not be correct, because in that case the usage of the word would be in other than what it was placed for, but this is correct by consensus. So it is known that the placement of the common word is not for the totality of the two meanings. Likewise, assuming it occurs, i.e., by hypothesis, if its placement were for the totality as a totality, then its usage in the totality would be usage in one of the two meanings, because as a totality it is one meaning. In reality, in this case, there would be confusion within being common, because in this case, placed for one meaning as a totality, there would not be multiplicity of meanings, which is necessary in a common word. If it is said that its placement, just as it is for the totality, is also for each one of the two meanings, then in response it will be said that in this case its usage in the totality is also usage in one of the meanings, not in more than one meaning, even if the first and third exist. Unless the third scenario, i.e., the placement of this common word is for each meaning with the condition of singularity or absolutely, then our claim is established that its usage in more than one meaning as a reality cannot happen, because placement particularizes the word to the meaning. In the sense that in placement there is particularization of the word to the meaning, such that that word is restricted and confined to that meaning, not exceeding to another, and at the time of usage, other than it is not intended. So every placement necessitates that the placement makes it obligatory that only that meaning be intended with the word which remains placed with this placement. And when this meaning is [intended], the placement also makes this matter obligatory that this meaning is entirely intended. So considering each of the two placements simultaneously would be contrary to considering the other placement. The first scenario is obvious: when the word's placement was for each meaning with the condition of singularity, then in that usage, intending the other meaning as well is contrary to it. If the second scenario, i.e., the word's placement is for each meaning absolutely, i.e., neither with the condition of singularity nor with the condition of togetherness, then in this case also more than one meaning cannot be intended as a reality, because considering each of the two placements is contrary to considering the other placement. Because considering the placement of one meaning necessitates that only this meaning be considered specifically, and considering the placement of the other meaning also necessitates that only the other meaning be considered specifically. So if in a single usage both placements are considered, then for each meaning, according to intention, both isolation from the other and togetherness with the other would be necessary. This is combining two contradictories. Rather, it would necessitate that each of the two meanings in one state is both intended and not intended. This is inherently false. And whoever knows the reason for the occurrence of commonality (al-ishtirak) it is not hidden from them that its usage in two meanings cannot happen, because the placement of the common word is for each one of the meanings. If the placer is Allah Almighty, then it is for the purpose of trial (ibtila'), i.e., for determining the intended meaning, there are hidden contextual indicators (qara'in). Based on these indicators, the servant, by striving and exerting to reach the determination of the intended meaning, becomes deserving of reward. So this trial can happen only when from among the different meanings, only one meaning is intended. If all meanings are intended simultaneously, then trial cannot happen. If the placer is other than Allah Almighty, i.e., servants, then either the commonality is with the intention of obfuscation (ibham), i.e., to make something ambiguous so that the listener does not fully understand the intended meaning. This also can happen only when one meaning is intended. If both are intended, then the purpose of obfuscation is not fulfilled. For example, during the Hijrah, Abu Bakr said to a polytheist questioner regarding the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), 'The one who guides me on the path,' so the questioner understood that Abu Bakr does not know the path and this person is showing him the path, whereas Abu Bakr intended something else. So this obfuscation and ensnarement can happen when the word is used in one meaning. If its usage in both meanings simultaneously were correct, then this ensnarement could not happen. So his statement 'because it was not placed for the totality' is an indication that the usage of the common word in two meanings could be correct only when its placement is for the totality. However, placement for the totality is negated in the common word. As for the two remaining scenarios, according to them, its usage in two meanings in one usage cannot happen, as we mentioned, nor metaphorically (majazan), i.e., the usage of the common word in more than one meaning metaphorically is also not possible, because in that case combining the real and the metaphorical would be necessary, which is invalid. And the reason for the necessity is that if the intention is the totality, then it is other than what it was placed for, which is metaphorical. And if each meaning is intended, then it is what it was placed for. So at that time, intending the real meaning and the metaphorical meaning from one word in one usage would be necessary, and this is combining [the two].
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a rule explaining why a common word (mushtarak) cannot be used to mean more than one of its meanings simultaneously, whether as a reality (haqiqah) or as a metaphor (majaz). The author begins by stating the proof: the word was not placed (wad') for the totality of its meanings. If it were placed for the totality, using it in one meaning without the other would be metaphorical, but usage in one meaning is unanimously accepted as real (haqiqah), so the placement cannot be for the totality. The author then analyzes three possible types of placement: (1) placement for each meaning separately with the condition of singularity (bishart al-infrad), (2) placement for both meanings together as a totality (lisum al-jami'), and (3) placement for each meaning absolutely without condition (ala al-itlaq). The second (placement for the totality) is actually non-existent for mushtarak. For (1), using the word to intend both meanings simultaneously would violate the placement condition. For (3), considering both placements simultaneously leads to a contradiction: each meaning would have to be both isolated and together with the other, an impossibility. Additionally, the author provides a rationale based on the purpose of commonality: if the placer is Allah, it is for testing (ibtila') — requiring effort to determine which single meaning is intended. If both were intended, no test would exist. If the placer is a human, commonality is for obfuscation (ibham), which also requires a single intended meaning. Thus, simultaneous usage fails. Finally, the passage concludes that metaphorical usage in two meanings is also invalid because it would require combining haqiqah (for each individual meaning) and majaz (for the totality) in one utterance, which is impossible. The commentary follows the original order and uses Hanafi usul reasoning.

Key Terms
مشترک (mushtarak): A word that has been placed (wad') for more than one meaning.
وضع (wad'): The act of assigning a word to a meaning; the establishment of a linguistic convention.
حقیقت (haqiqah): Usage of a word in its originally assigned meaning (what it was placed for).
مجاز (majaz): Usage of a word in a meaning other than its original assigned meaning, based on a relation.
ابتلاء (ibtila'): Divine testing; the purpose behind making a word common (mushtarak) when the placer is Allah, to test humans in determining the intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul argument in this passage, why can a common word (mushtarak) not be used in more than one meaning simultaneously as a reality (haqiqah)?
A) Because the word was placed for the totality of meanings, so using it for one meaning would be incorrect.
B) Because the word was placed for each meaning individually, and simultaneously intending both violates the condition of the placement.
C) Because using it in more than one meaning is impossible due to a lack of contextual indicators.
D) Because common words only exist in metaphor, not reality.

Key Takeaway
A common word cannot be used to mean multiple things simultaneously because placement (wad') particularizes each meaning individually, making simultaneous intention logically impossible.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
6 min
Impermissibility of Using a Homonym for Multiple Meanings
Refutation of permissibility of using mushtarak for multiple meanings simultaneously

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a mushtarak cannot indicate multiple meanings simultaneously and evaluate the counter-evidence from Quran 33:56.

Core Idea
A mushtarak cannot indicate multiple meanings at once; each meaning's separate wad' prohibits simultaneous intention.

This lesson tackles a foundational usul dispute: can a single homonymous word (mushtarak) signify two different meanings at once? While some scholars cite Quran 33:56 as proof, the Hanafi school firmly rejects this, grounding its position in the nature of linguistic placement (wad') and the contextual analysis of the Quranic verse. By working through the logical arguments and the counter-evidence, you will sharpen your ability to critically evaluate evidence (istidlal) in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Read the passage to identify what exactly is being contested: whether a mushtarak can be used to intend multiple meanings simultaneously. Note the Hanafi position and the opposing view.
The dispute is purely about simultaneous intention, not sequential use.
2
Analyse the Hanafi logical proof
Examine the argument that two separate placements (wad') for different meanings cannot both be intended in a single utterance because each placement demands exclusive consideration of its meaning.
The proof also covers the impossibility of combining reality and metaphor.
3
Examine the opposing evidence
Read the opponent's citation of Quran 33:56, where the word 'yusalluna' (they send blessings) is attributed to both Allah and the angels, apparently with different meanings (mercy vs. forgiveness).
This is the strongest proof for the opposing view.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Study the Hanafi rebuttal: the verse's context (siyaq al-kalam) requires a single meaning of 'sending blessings' for the believers to emulate; the difference arises from the subject (Allah vs. angels), not from lexical homonymy.
Also note the rejection of a weak Hanafi objection based on pronoun multiplicity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical consequence: how does this dispute affect the interpretation of Quranic verses? Hanafis will only accept one meaning per occurrence, while opponents may derive multiple obligations from a single word.
This principle guides many Hanafi rulings on ambiguous Quranic terms.

اور مقتدی دونوں ایک ہی جیسا فعل کریں تو تب اقتدا ہوتی ہے گر فعل الگ الگ قسم کا ہو تو پھر اقتدا صحیح نہیں ہے۔ فلا بد من اتحاد معنى الصلوۃ تو ضروری ہے کہ یہاں صلوۃ کا معنی ایک ہی ہونا چاہتے لیکن اختلاف موضون کی بنا پر مختلف ہوگا ۔ جیسے باقی صفات میں ہوا کرتا ہے۔ لا بحسب الوضع اور یہ اختلاف باعتبار وضع کے نہیں ہے اسکی مزید تفصیل کرتے ہوئے کہا اعلم ان المجوزین یعنی وہ لوگ جو مشترک کے متعدد معانی کے درمیان جمع کو جائز قرار دیتے ہیں انھوں کی اللہ تعالی کے قول ان الله وملائکتہ یصلون علی النبی الآیتہ سے استدلال کیا ہے کہ صلوۃ اللہ کی جانب سے رحمت ہے اور ملائکہ سے استغفار تو ایک ہی لفظ سے ایک ہی اطلاق میں متعدد معانی مراد لئے جا رہے ہیں تو یہ جمع بین معانی المشترک ہے ۔ وقد اور دوا على هذه الآية من قبلنا الم یعنی ہمارے علما نے ہماری طرف سے اس آیتہ پر ایک اشکال فاسد وارد کیا ہے اور اسی پر بنیاد جواب رکھی ہے کہ یہ آئینہ متنارع فیہ مسئلہ میں سے نہیں ہے یعنی ایک ہی لفظ سے جمع بین المعانی کرنے سے نہیں ہے کیونکہ فعل تعدد ضمائر کی بنا پر متعدد ہو جاتا ہے۔ گویا کہ لفظ یصلی مکرر ہے۔ اللہ تعالیٰ کی طرف ایک تصیلی اور ملائکہ کی طرف دوسرا۔ اور انہوں نے جواب دیا کہ یہ تعد حسب المعنی ہے لا بحسب اللفظ لفظاً لعدم الاحتياج الى هذا اى الى تعدد اللفظ
وهذا الاشكال من قبلنا فاصد ہماری طرف سے یہ اشکال جو وارد کیا گیا ہے فاسد ہے کیونکہ لا یجوزنی مثل هذا یعنی بصورت تعدد ضمائر کے بھی ایک لفظ مشترک سے ہمارے نزدیک متعدد معانی مراد لینا جائز نہیں ہے۔ لہذا یہ آیتہ متنازع فیہ سے ہے والجواب الصحيح لنا ہماری طرف سے اس اشکال کاصحیح جواب یہ ہے کہ اس آیتہ میں استعمال مشترک ایک معنی سے زائد میں نہیں بلکہ صرف ایک ہی معنی مراد ہے لان سیاق الایة کیونکہ سیاق آیتہ مؤمنین پر واجب کرتا ہے کہ صلو علی النبی علیہ السلام کے بارہ میں اللہ تعالیٰ اور ملائکہ کی اقتدا کرنی لازم ہے اور یا قتدا
Then taking both formations into account at once would contradict considering the other formation. The first case is clear: when a word is placed for each meaning with condition of singularity, then including the other meaning in that application contradicts that. If it is the second case i.e. the word is placed for each meaning absolutely i.e. neither with condition of singularity nor with condition of conjunction, even then more than one meaning cannot be intended as reality because considering each of the two formations contradicts considering the other formation, because considering the formation for the first meaning requires that only this meaning particularly be considered, and considering the formation for the other meaning requires that only that meaning particularly be considered. So if in a single application both formations are considered, then each meaning would necessarily be both isolated from the other and combined with the other; this is contradictory. Rather it would follow that each of the two meanings in one state is both intended and not intended, which is obviously false. And whoever knows the reason for the occurrence of homonymy, it is not hidden from them that it cannot be used for two meanings because a homonym is placed for each meaning individually. If the setter is Allah Most High, then the purpose is trial, i.e. there are hidden clues for determining the intended meaning. Based on these clues, the servant strives to reach the determination of the intended meaning and becomes deserving of reward. This trial can only occur when only one meaning is intended from the various meanings. If all meanings are intended at once, then there can be no trial. If the setter is other than Allah Most High i.e. people, then either the homonymy is for the purpose of obscuring, i.e. making something ambiguous so that the listener does not fully grasp the intended meaning. This too can only occur when one meaning is intended. If both are intended, then the purpose of obscuring is not fulfilled. For example, Abu Bakr during the migration said to a polytheist questioner regarding the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'The man guides me on the path.' The questioner understood that Abu Bakr did not know the road and this person was showing him the way, while Abu Bakr's intention was something else. This obscuring can only occur when the word is used for one meaning; if its use for both meanings simultaneously were correct, then this obscuring would not be possible. So his saying 'because it is not placed for the composite' points out that the use of a homonym for more than one meaning can only be correct if it were placed for the composite, but placement for the composite is negated in a homonym. As for the two remaining cases, its use for two meanings in one application is not possible, as we mentioned, nor metaphorically i.e. the use of a homonym for more than one meaning metaphorically is also not possible, because in that case combining reality and metaphor would be necessary, which is invalid. The reason for the necessity is that if the composite is intended, that is not what it is placed for, so it is metaphorical; and if each individual meaning is intended, that is what it is placed for. Then intending a real meaning and a metaphorical meaning from a single word in one application would be necessary, and this combination of reality and metaphor is invalid. This combination is also explained thus: the use of a homonym for two meanings metaphorically could only be if there is a connection between those meanings, because connection between the real and metaphorical meanings is a condition. Now intending one meaning would be in the capacity that it is what it is placed for, and intending the other would be based on a connection by virtue of which it is a suitable meaning for what it is placed for. Thus combining reality and metaphor would be necessary. If it is said: 'They pray upon the Prophet ...' verse. Those who consider combining the meanings of a homonym valid say that in this verse the word 'salat' which appears as 'yusalluna' attributed to Allah and the angels, its meaning is mercy and forgiveness. Both these meanings are intended simultaneously from the word 'yusalluna', so this is combining the meanings of a homonym. We say this is the answer, summary: there is no homonymy, because the context of the speech is for requiring following, i.e. the believers are obligated to follow Allah and His angels, and following occurs only when the action is one and the follower and the one followed perform the same action; if the actions are different, then following is not correct. So there must be unity of the meaning of 'salat'; it is necessary that the meaning of 'salat' here be one, but it becomes different due to the difference of what it is attributed to, just as occurs in other attributes, not due to the word's placement. Elaborating on this, he said: Know that those who permit (i.e. those who consider it permissible to combine multiple meanings of a homonym) have used the saying of Allah Most High 'Indeed Allah and His angels send blessings upon the Prophet' (Quran 33:56) as evidence that 'salat' from Allah is mercy and from angels is seeking forgiveness. So from a single word in a single application multiple meanings are intended, and this is combining the meanings of a homonym. Our scholars from our side have raised a false objection to this verse and based their answer on it: that this verse is not from the disputed issue, i.e. it does not involve combining meanings from a single word because the action becomes multiple due to multiple pronouns. So the word 'yusalli' is repeated: one from Allah and another from the angels. And they replied that this multiplicity is according to meaning, not according to wording, because there is no need for this i.e. for multiplicity of wording. And this objection from our side is false, because such a thing is not permissible: i.e. even in the case of multiple pronouns, using a single homonymous word to intend multiple meanings is not permissible according to us. Hence this verse is from the disputed issue. And the correct answer from our side is that in this verse there is no use of a homonym for more than one meaning; rather only one meaning is intended, because the context of the verse requires that the believers must follow Allah and His angels regarding sending blessings upon the Prophet (peace be upon him).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is an usul dispute on the permissibility of using a mushtarak (homonym) to indicate multiple meanings simultaneously. The Hanafi position is that it is absolutely prohibited. The author first presents the argument: if a word is placed for each meaning with a condition (either exclusively or absolutely), then intending both meanings at once is contradictory because each placement requires exclusive consideration of that meaning. Additionally, if the word were placed for the composite meaning, multiple intentions might be possible, but a mushtarak is not placed for the composite. The author also refutes metaphorical use: using a mushtarak for two meanings metaphorically would entail combining reality and metaphor in one word, which is invalid. The opponent cites the verse 'Allah and His angels send blessings upon the Prophet' (33:56) as an example where the word 'salat' is used for mercy (from Allah) and forgiveness (from angels) simultaneously. The Hanafi response is that there is no true homonymy here; the context of the verse requires unity of action for the believers to follow, so the meaning must be one, but it differs due to the attributed subject, not because the word has multiple lexical meanings. The author also critiques a weak Hanafi objection that the multiplicity of pronouns makes the word repeated; this is false because even with multiple pronouns, a single lexical word cannot indicate multiple meanings. The correct answer is that the verse's context forces a single meaning of 'salat' (i.e., sending blessings) but its specific manifestation differs by subject.

Key Terms
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word that has multiple meanings due to separate placements (wad') for each meaning, such as 'salat' meaning mercy or forgiveness or prayer.
وضع (wad'): The linguistic placement of a word to signify a meaning; here, each meaning of a mushtarak has its own separate wad'.
مجاز (majaz): Use of a word in a meaning other than its original placement due to a connection (alāqah); the author argues that using a mushtarak metaphorically for multiple meanings leads to combining reality and metaphor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul presented, why is it not permissible to use a mushtarak (homonym) for multiple meanings simultaneously?
A) Because the word would then be considered ambiguous and cause doubt.
B) Because each meaning has a separate placement, and considering both at once contradicts the exclusivity of each placement, and it also would require combining reality and metaphor if used metaphorically.
C) Because the Qur'an prohibits using such words.
D) Because the Companions never used words that way.

Key Takeaway
A mushtarak is restricted to one meaning per use; simultaneous multiple intentions contradict the nature of wad' and are impermissible in Hanafi usul.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Manqūl vs Majāz: The Role of the Original Meaning
Explaining the different roles of the original meaning in manqūl vs. majāz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between manqūl and majāz by explaining why the original meaning (maʿnā awwal) is considered only for preference in manqūl but for correctness in majāz, and apply this distinction to the word 'dābbah'.

Core Idea
In manqūl, original meaning = preference; in majāz, original meaning = correctness.

This passage introduces a critical distinction in Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh between a transferred word (manqūl) and a metaphor (majāz). Using the example of 'dābbah' (beast) which originally meant 'anything that crawls' but later became restricted to 'horse' by custom, the author shows how the original meaning functions differently in each category. Understanding this is essential for correctly interpreting legal texts that contain manqūl or majāz expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage contrasts manqūl (by custom/Sharīʿah) and majāz (metaphor). Note the examples: dābbah for manqūl, asad for majāz.
2
Examine the role of the original meaning in manqūl
In manqūl, the original meaning (e.g., 'crawling') is not needed for correctness of applying the word to its new meaning (horse). It only explains why that word was chosen over others (tarjīḥ/preference).
The text says: 'نظره ان اعتبار المعنى الاول فيه ليس بصحة اطلاقه الى المنقول عليه'
3
Examine the role of the original meaning in majāz
In majāz, the original meaning is required for correctness. The word asad (lion) is applied by metaphor to a brave person because courage is a necessary concomitant of lion; the connection (ʿalāqah) makes the metaphor valid.
The text says: 'ان المجاز معنى اول كر بر اور اس كے ساتھ علاقہ صحت اطلاق كی خاطر ہوتا ہے'
4
Contrast and conclude
The critical difference: in manqūl, omission of original meaning does not invalidate the application; in majāz, it does. This shapes how legal terms are interpreted in fiqh.
Memorize the summary: 'خلاصہ یہ کہ منقول جیسے دابتہ عرفی کے اندر معنی اول کا اعتبار ... نه اس لئے کہ فرس پر اس کا اطلاق درست ہو جائے ۔ بل لترجيح هذا الاسم على غيره'

ہوتا ہے۔ فیطلق الاسد على كل من يوجد فيه الشجاعة مجازا الخ چونکہ مجاز میں معنی اول کا اعتبار اس لئے ہے کہ لفظ کا اطلاق ہر اس پر صحیح ہو جس کے اندر معنی اول کا لازم پایا جائے ۔ مثلاً اسد کا لازم ہے شجاعت جہاں شجاعت پائی جائیگی ان تمام پر لفظ اسد کا اطلاق مجازاً صحیح ہوگا ۔ بخلاف منقول کے کہ اس کے اندر معنی اول کا اعتبار صحتہ اطلاق کے لئے نہیں ۔ لہذا منقول عرفی لفظ دابتہ کا اطلاق ہر اس پر صحیح نہیں ہوگا جس کے اندر رسیب پایا جائے۔
The word dābbah (beast) is applied to the horse (faras) as a literal usage (ḥaqīqah), but if it is specified (by custom) — because according to common custom its predominant use is only for the horse, and other referents are precluded — now if dābbah is applied to the horse by way of specification (takhṣīṣ), with consideration of the original meaning (maʿnā) i.e., the meaning of 'what crawls on the earth' (mā yadibb ʿalā al-arḍ), then its application to the horse, linguistically, would be metaphorical (majāz) if the intended meaning is other than the original (wadʿ) — i.e., if the specific horse is meant from among things that crawl. Because the specific meaning, linguistically, is not what it was coined for. Or from the perspective of custom (ʿurf): when common custom specified the word dābbah for the horse, it is as if this word dābbah was initially coined for the horse; because when they specified it for the horse, in that application the original meaning is not considered. Rather, its name is such that in common custom the word dābbah itself has become the name of the horse, coined for it. Therefore, according to common custom, its application is literal (ḥaqīqah). It appears that in the word dābbah, consideration of the original meaning (i.e., mā yadibb) is not for the correctness of its application to what it has been transferred to (manqūl ʿalayh). That is, the application of manqūl to [all] individuals in which the original meaning is found — the original meaning is not considered for it. Like in literal usage (ḥaqīqah), the original meaning is considered so that the word can be applied to every individual that contains that original meaning. In manqūl it is not so. Otherwise it would follow that manqūl is applied correctly only to the individual in which the original meaning (dabīb) is found. However, for example, the word dābbah, which has become manqūl by custom, its application to everything that crawls on earth is not correct. Also, for the correctness of its application to the second meaning (i.e., the transferred meaning), the original meaning is not considered. For example, the application of dābbah by custom is only to the horse; for its correctness also, the original meaning is not considered. In contrast, in majāz (metaphor), the original meaning (the literal meaning) is considered so that the word's application to the necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the original meaning — which is the second, metaphorical meaning — becomes correct. Just as in majāz, a connection (ʿalāqah) of suitability (munāsabah) with the literal meaning is considered. In manqūl it is not like that. The summary: in manqūl like 'urfī dābbah, consideration of the original meaning (mā yadibb ʿalā al-arḍ) is neither so that its application to everything that crawls (kull mā yadibb) becomes correct, nor so that its application to the horse becomes correct. Rather, it is to give this name preference (tarjīḥ) over other names. For example, common custom has chosen the word dābbah for the horse and not chosen the word jidār (wall) or al-ḥajar (stone). The reason for this preference is that the original meaning of dābbah (i.e., moving on earth) exists in the horse, and the original meaning of jidār and ḥajar does not exist in it. For this preference, the original meaning is considered, not for the correctness of the application. In its specification (takhṣīṣ) to the second meaning (i.e., the transferred meaning). In this, the transferred word (manqūl) is given preference over other words; for presenting the reason for this preference, the original meaning is considered. Learn from this that when a word is coined (wadʿ) for a meaning, sometimes suitability (munāsabah) is not considered in that coining, like al-jidār and al-ḥajar; and sometimes suitability is considered during coining, like al-qārūrah which is called glass or bottle because the contained thing settles (qarār) in it, or like al-khamr because it overwhelms (mukhāmara) the intellect. And consideration of the original meaning in the secondary coining (wadʿ thānī) — for example, the word khamr coined for wine, and the consideration of the original meaning (overwhelming the intellect) in it — this is to express suitability (munāsabah) and preference (awlawiyyah), i.e., it is more suitable and more worthy among other words, not for the correctness of the application (ṣiḥḥat al-iṭlāq). Otherwise it would follow that a jar (dann) would also be called qārūrah because contained things settle in it. Therefore, because the original meaning is not considered for the correctness of the application, it is said: qiyās cannot be applied in language (lā yajrī al-qiyās fī al-lughah). If it were for correctness, then wherever the original meaning is found, the application would extend by analogy through intellectual reasoning. But it is not like that. Thus, it is not said that other intoxicating beverages (sāʾir al-ashriba) are khamr — i.e., besides grape wine ('aṣīr 'inab), other drinks that have the property of overwhelming the intellect are not called khamr according to linguistic coining, even if they contain the meaning of mukhāmara. Because the consideration of mukhāmara was not so that any drink containing mukhāmara would be correctly called khamr; otherwise qiyās in language would occur. Rather, it is for suitability and preference: the coiner considered it only for suitability and preference, so that the coiner coins a word that is suitable and balanced for that meaning. So preserve this discussion; it is noble and marvelous. Memorize it thoroughly. The feet of those who allowed qiyās in language have slipped due to negligence of this point. They understood that the consideration of the original meaning is for correctness of application. Then wherever they found this meaning, they declared that the linguistic application of khamr is correct — whether grape wine or date wine (nabīdh) and dried dates (tamar) wine, although linguistically these are not khamr. The consideration of mukhāmara was only to the extent of coining a balanced word, not for correctness of application. That was the discussion regarding manqūl. However, in majāz, consideration of the original meaning and its connection (ʿalāqah) is for the sake of correctness of application. Thus, the word asad (lion) is applied metaphorically to everyone in whom courage (shajāʿah) exists, because in majāz the original meaning is considered so that the word's application to everyone in whom the necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the original meaning is found becomes correct. For example, the necessary concomitant of asad is courage; wherever courage is found, the word asad can be applied metaphorically. Unlike manqūl, where consideration of the original meaning is not for correctness of application. Therefore, the manqūl ʿurfī word dābbah cannot be correctly applied to everything in which crawling (dabīb) is found.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh discussion distinguishing between manqūl (transferred usage) and majāz (metaphor) regarding the role of the original meaning. The author uses the example of the word 'dābbah' (beast) which in common custom ('urf) has been transferred from its literal meaning 'anything that crawls on earth' to specifically mean 'horse'. The key point is that in manqūl, the original meaning is NOT considered for the correctness of applying the word to its new specific referent (the horse) nor for extending it back to all crawling things. Instead, the original meaning is only considered to explain why this particular word was chosen over other possible words (like jidār or ḥajar) — that is, for preference (tarjīḥ) and suitability (munāsabah). This contrasts with majāz, where the original meaning is necessary for the correctness of the metaphorical application: for example, calling a brave man 'asad' (lion) is correct because the original meaning (lion) has a necessary concomitant (courage) that is present in the brave person. The passage then discusses the concept of qiyas in language: those who allow analogical extension in language erroneously think that the original meaning is considered for correctness, leading them to wrongly apply 'khamr' to all intoxicating drinks. The author emphasizes that this is a crucial distinction to understand.

Key Terms
منقول (manqūl): A word transferred from its original literal meaning (haqīqah) to a specific new meaning by custom (ʿurf) or by Sharīʿah, where the original meaning is not considered for the correctness of the application to the new meaning.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage where a word is applied to something other than its original meaning due to a connection (ʿalāqah) of suitability, and the original meaning is necessary for the correctness of the application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi usul discussion, what is the purpose of considering the original meaning (maʿnā awwal) in a manqūl word like 'dābbah'?
A) To ensure the word can be applied to every individual that contains that original meaning.
B) To justify the correctness of applying the word to the specific transferred meaning (the horse).
C) To show why this particular word was preferred over other possible words for that meaning.
D) To allow metaphorical extension to all things that share the original meaning.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the original meaning in manqūl serves only as a basis for preference, not a condition for correct application—unlike majāz where it is essential.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Qiyās fī al-Lughah and the Khamr Example
Explaining why qiyas is invalid in language using the khamr example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why qiyas is not valid in language (lā yajrī al-qiyās fī al-lughah) according to Hanafi usul, by applying the distinction that the original meaning is considered only for preference not for correctness, using the example of khamr.

Core Idea
Qiyas in language fails because the original meaning only justifies word choice, not analogical extension.

This passage extends the earlier distinction between manqūl and majāz to a methodological rule: qiyas (analogy) cannot be applied in language (al-lughah). Using the example of 'khamr' (wine), the author shows that the original meaning of 'overwhelming the intellect' was considered only to coin a suitable word, not to allow calling all intoxicants 'khamr'. This principle safeguards the precision of legal language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle under discussion
The passage introduces and defends the rule 'lā yajrī al-qiyās fī al-lughah' — analogy is not permissible in language. The khamr example is used to illustrate the reasoning.
2
Analyze the khamr example
The word 'khamr' is coined for grape wine because it overwhelms the intellect (mukhāmara). However, this original meaning is only considered to explain why this word was chosen (for suitability and preference), not to enable calling all intoxicants 'khamr' by analogy.
The text says: 'واعتبار المعنى الاول ... لا نصيحة الاطلاق'
3
Understand the consequence if qiyas were allowed
If the original meaning were considered for correctness, then any intoxicating drink (date wine etc.) would be called 'khamr' by analogy. But the correct linguistic usage restricts 'khamr' to grape wine, because the original meaning only informed the coinage, not the scope.
4
Connect to the earlier distinction
This follows from the manqūl discussion: in manqūl (and also in coined terms), the original meaning serves only for preference, never for extending the application by analogy. Those who allow qiyas in language have misunderstood this.
The text warns: 'لم تزل اقدام من سوغ الم ... قد انزلقت'

شجاعت جہاں شجاعت پائی جائیگی ان تمام پر لفظ اسد کا اطلاق مجازاً صحیح ہوگا ۔ بخلاف منقول کے کہ اس کے اندر معنی اول کا اعتبار صحتہ اطلاق کے لئے نہیں ۔ لہذا منقول عرفی لفظ دابتہ کا اطلاق ہر اس پر صحیح نہیں ہوگا جس کے اندر رسیب پایا جائے۔
The word dābbah (beast) is applied to the horse (faras) as a literal usage (ḥaqīqah), but if it is specified (by custom) — because according to common custom its predominant use is only for the horse, and other referents are precluded — now if dābbah is applied to the horse by way of specification (takhṣīṣ), with consideration of the original meaning (maʿnā) i.e., the meaning of 'what crawls on the earth' (mā yadibb ʿalā al-arḍ), then its application to the horse, linguistically, would be metaphorical (majāz) if the intended meaning is other than the original (wadʿ) — i.e., if the specific horse is meant from among things that crawl. Because the specific meaning, linguistically, is not what it was coined for. Or from the perspective of custom (ʿurf): when common custom specified the word dābbah for the horse, it is as if this word dābbah was initially coined for the horse; because when they specified it for the horse, in that application the original meaning is not considered. Rather, its name is such that in common custom the word dābbah itself has become the name of the horse, coined for it. Therefore, according to common custom, its application is literal (ḥaqīqah). It appears that in the word dābbah, consideration of the original meaning (i.e., mā yadibb) is not for the correctness of its application to what it has been transferred to (manqūl ʿalayh). That is, the application of manqūl to [all] individuals in which the original meaning is found — the original meaning is not considered for it. Like in literal usage (ḥaqīqah), the original meaning is considered so that the word can be applied to every individual that contains that original meaning. In manqūl it is not so. Otherwise it would follow that manqūl is applied correctly only to the individual in which the original meaning (dabīb) is found. However, for example, the word dābbah, which has become manqūl by custom, its application to everything that crawls on earth is not correct. Also, for the correctness of its application to the second meaning (i.e., the transferred meaning), the original meaning is not considered. For example, the application of dābbah by custom is only to the horse; for its correctness also, the original meaning is not considered. In contrast, in majāz (metaphor), the original meaning (the literal meaning) is considered so that the word's application to the necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the original meaning — which is the second, metaphorical meaning — becomes correct. Just as in majāz, a connection (ʿalāqah) of suitability (munāsabah) with the literal meaning is considered. In manqūl it is not like that. The summary: in manqūl like 'urfī dābbah, consideration of the original meaning (mā yadibb ʿalā al-arḍ) is neither so that its application to everything that crawls (kull mā yadibb) becomes correct, nor so that its application to the horse becomes correct. Rather, it is to give this name preference (tarjīḥ) over other names. For example, common custom has chosen the word dābbah for the horse and not chosen the word jidār (wall) or al-ḥajar (stone). The reason for this preference is that the original meaning of dābbah (i.e., moving on earth) exists in the horse, and the original meaning of jidār and ḥajar does not exist in it. For this preference, the original meaning is considered, not for the correctness of the application. In its specification (takhṣīṣ) to the second meaning (i.e., the transferred meaning). In this, the transferred word (manqūl) is given preference over other words; for presenting the reason for this preference, the original meaning is considered. Learn from this that when a word is coined (wadʿ) for a meaning, sometimes suitability (munāsabah) is not considered in that coining, like al-jidār and al-ḥajar; and sometimes suitability is considered during coining, like al-qārūrah which is called glass or bottle because the contained thing settles (qarār) in it, or like al-khamr because it overwhelms (mukhāmara) the intellect. And consideration of the original meaning in the secondary coining (wadʿ thānī) — for example, the word khamr coined for wine, and the consideration of the original meaning (overwhelming the intellect) in it — this is to express suitability (munāsabah) and preference (awlawiyyah), i.e., it is more suitable and more worthy among other words, not for the correctness of the application (ṣiḥḥat al-iṭlāq). Otherwise it would follow that a jar (dann) would also be called qārūrah because contained things settle in it. Therefore, because the original meaning is not considered for the correctness of the application, it is said: qiyās cannot be applied in language (lā yajrī al-qiyās fī al-lughah). If it were for correctness, then wherever the original meaning is found, the application would extend by analogy through intellectual reasoning. But it is not like that. Thus, it is not said that other intoxicating beverages (sāʾir al-ashriba) are khamr — i.e., besides grape wine ('aṣīr 'inab), other drinks that have the property of overwhelming the intellect are not called khamr according to linguistic coining, even if they contain the meaning of mukhāmara. Because the consideration of mukhāmara was not so that any drink containing mukhāmara would be correctly called khamr; otherwise qiyās in language would occur. Rather, it is for suitability and preference: the coiner considered it only for suitability and preference, so that the coiner coins a word that is suitable and balanced for that meaning. So preserve this discussion; it is noble and marvelous. Memorize it thoroughly. The feet of those who allowed qiyās in language have slipped due to negligence of this point. They understood that the consideration of the original meaning is for correctness of application. Then wherever they found this meaning, they declared that the linguistic application of khamr is correct — whether grape wine or date wine (nabīdh) and dried dates (tamar) wine, although linguistically these are not khamr. The consideration of mukhāmara was only to the extent of coining a balanced word, not for correctness of application. That was the discussion regarding manqūl. However, in majāz, consideration of the original meaning and its connection (ʿalāqah) is for the sake of correctness of application. Thus, the word asad (lion) is applied metaphorically to everyone in whom courage (shajāʿah) exists, because in majāz the original meaning is considered so that the word's application to everyone in whom the necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the original meaning is found becomes correct. For example, the necessary concomitant of asad is courage; wherever courage is found, the word asad can be applied metaphorically. Unlike manqūl, where consideration of the original meaning is not for correctness of application. Therefore, the manqūl ʿurfī word dābbah cannot be correctly applied to everything in which crawling (dabīb) is found.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh discussion distinguishing between manqūl (transferred usage) and majāz (metaphor) regarding the role of the original meaning. The author uses the example of the word 'dābbah' (beast) which in common custom ('urf) has been transferred from its literal meaning 'anything that crawls on earth' to specifically mean 'horse'. The key point is that in manqūl, the original meaning is NOT considered for the correctness of applying the word to its new specific referent (the horse) nor for extending it back to all crawling things. Instead, the original meaning is only considered to explain why this particular word was chosen over other possible words (like jidār or ḥajar) — that is, for preference (tarjīḥ) and suitability (munāsabah). This contrasts with majāz, where the original meaning is necessary for the correctness of the metaphorical application: for example, calling a brave man 'asad' (lion) is correct because the original meaning (lion) has a necessary concomitant (courage) that is present in the brave person. The passage then discusses the concept of qiyas in language: those who allow analogical extension in language erroneously think that the original meaning is considered for correctness, leading them to wrongly apply 'khamr' to all intoxicating drinks. The author emphasizes that this is a crucial distinction to understand.

Key Terms
قیاس فی اللغة (qiyās fī al-lughah): Analogically extending a word to all instances sharing the original meaning based on reasoning; the author argues it is invalid because the original meaning is only for preference in coining, not for correctness of application.
خمر (khamr): Linguistically, grape wine, coined due to its property of overwhelming the intellect (mukhāmara). The property is only for preference, not for analogical extension to other intoxicants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason the Hanafi school rejects qiyas in language (al-lughah)?
A) Because language is arbitrary and has no system of analogy.
B) Because the original meaning of a word is considered only for preference (tarjīḥ), not for correctness of application.
C) Because words cannot be extended beyond their literal meanings under any circumstance.
D) Because qiyas in language leads to confusion and should be avoided.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul holds that qiyas is not valid in language because the original meaning of a word serves only as a basis for preference in coinage, not as a condition for analogical extension.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
6 min
The Transformation of Haqiqa and Majaz Through Usage
Explaining the transformation of haqiqa and majaz through usage frequency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a word’s literal meaning may become metaphorical and vice versa due to predominant usage, using Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
A haqiqa abandoned becomes majaz; a majaz prevailing becomes haqiqa — usage reshapes linguistic meaning.

Words shift over time. In Hanafi usul, this is not mere historical curiosity — it determines legal interpretation. The classic example is salat: originally meaning supplication, but shar'i usage transformed it into the formal prayer. This lesson unpacks the mechanism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about haqiqa and majaz
The passage states that when haqiqa usage becomes rare (qalil), it becomes majaz, and when majaz usage becomes abundant (kathir), it becomes haqiqa.
2
Analyze the example of salat
Apply the claim to salat: originally haqiqa in du'a (supplication) and majaz in the formal prayer (specific pillars); after predominant shar'i usage, roles reversed.
The example demonstrates how frequency alone can invert the linguistic status.
3
Understand the criterion
The criterion is ghalabah (predominance) in usage, not intrinsic meaning. A word's status depends on how it is commonly used in the relevant discourse.
This applies especially to shar'i terms where the Lawgiver's usage may differ from pre-Islamic usage.
4
Note the legal implication
For usul, this transformation affects how we interpret Quran and hadith words; a word may have shifted in meaning between the time of revelation and later usage, so careful analysis is required.
For example, the word 'khamr' originally meant 'cover', but shar'i usage settled on 'intoxicant'.

فالحقيقة التي لم تهجروه حقیقتہ جو محجور نہیں ہوئی بلکہ مستعمل ہے تو وہ صریح ہے کیونکہ یہ ظاہر المراد ہے. والتی حجرت اور وہ حقیقتہ جو مھجور الاستعمال ہے وغلب معناها المجانز ہی اور اس کا مجازی معنی غالب الاستعمال ہے تو وہ حقیقتہ کنایہ کہلائے گی اور مجاز غالب الاستعمال صریح قرار پائے گا۔ اور مجاز غیر غالب کتابیہ قرار پائے گا ۔ واعلم ان الصريح والكناية یعنی جو صریح اور کنائیہ حقیقتہ کی دو قسمیں ہیں ۔ یہ دونوں حقیقی معنی کے لحاظ سے ہیں. اور وہ صریح و کنایہ جو مجانہ کے دو قسم ہیں۔ یوونتوں باعتبار معنی مجازی کے صریح و کنایہ ہیں تو حقیقتہ بھی دو قسم ہوئی ۔ صریح وکنایہ اور مجاز بھی دو قسم ہوا ۔ ایک صریح اور دوسرا کنا یہ ۔ تقسیم اور تعریف کنایتہ عند علماء الاصول ہے ۔ وعند علماء البیان اور علمام بیان و معانی کے نزدیک کنا یہ وہ لفظ ہے جس کے معنی موضوع لہ سے معنی ثانی مراد ہو۔ جو معنی اول کا ملزوم بخلاف المجانی بخلاف مجاز کے کہ وہ بخیر ما وضع لہ میں مستعمل ہوتا ہے ۔ اور یہ ارادہ موضوع لہ کے منافی ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی مجاز میں موضوع لہ کا ارادہ نہیں ہو سکتا ۔ پھر یہ حقیقتہ و مجاز یا تو مفرد میں ہوں گے جن کی تعریفات گزر چکی ہیں ۔ دامانی الجملہ اور یا حقیقتہ و مجاز جملہ میں ہوں گے ۔
But it will be only on [horse?]. And similarly, the shar'i word salat, its application shar'an to every du'a is not correct, but shar'an its application is only to specific pillars. And it is also established that when the usage of haqiqa becomes little, it becomes majaz. And when the usage of majaz becomes abundant, it becomes haqiqa. For example, the word salat was originally haqiqa in du'a and majaz in specific pillars; now in shar'i terminology its usage in specific pillars has become abundant so that its meaning of du'a is forgotten. Therefore, in specific pillars it is haqiqa and in du'a it is majaz. Then each of haqiqa and majaz, if in itself the intended meaning is not hidden but clear, then it is sarih. And if its intended meaning is hidden, then it is kinaya. So the haqiqa that is not abandoned but used, that is sarih because its meaning is apparent. And that haqiqa which is abandoned in usage and its majazi meaning is predominant, that haqiqa is called kinaya. And majaz with predominant usage becomes sarih, and majaz that is not predominant becomes kinaya. Know that sarih and kinaya are two types of haqiqa; these two are with respect to the haqiqi meaning. And sarih and kinaya that are two types of majaz; they are sarih and kinaya with respect to the majazi meaning. Thus haqiqa also became two types: sarih and kinaya, and majaz also became two types: one sarih and the other kinaya. The division and definition of kinaya according to usul scholars is such. And according to scholars of bayan, kinaya is a word from which a secondary meaning is intended, which is the necessary concomitant (lazim) of the original meaning. That is, applying the necessary and intending the necessary concomitant is called kinaya. And this kinaya is not contrary to intending the original meaning. Rather, it is used in its original meaning, but the intended [secondary] meaning is the necessary concomitant. For example, 'tawil al-nijad' is used in its original meaning, but the purpose is 'tall in stature' because tallness is the necessary concomitant of long peg. Thus it is applying the necessary and intending the necessary concomitant, unlike majaz which is used in something other than what it is placed for and intending the original meaning is contrary to it. That is, in majaz, the original meaning cannot be intended. Then this haqiqa and majaz are either in individual words (mufrad) whose definitions have passed, or in sentences (jumal).
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage defines and elaborates on the concepts of sarih and kinaya within haqiqa and majaz. It begins by illustrating how a shift in frequency of usage transforms haqiqa into majaz and vice versa, using the example of the word salat: originally haqiqa for supplication and majaz for the formal prayer, but due to predominant shar'i usage, the roles reversed. Then it explains that both haqiqa and majaz can be either sarih (clear and apparent) or kinaya (hidden and requiring inference). The key criterion is whether the intended meaning is evident or concealed. A haqiqa that is still in common use is sarih; a haqiqa that is abandoned and whose majazi meaning dominates is considered kinaya. Conversely, a majaz that is commonly used is sarih, and a rare majaz is kinaya. The passage then provides the usul scholars' definition of kinaya: a word used in its original meaning but intending a secondary meaning that is a necessary concomitant of the original, like 'tawil al-nijad' to mean a tall person. This differs from majaz, where the word is used for something other than its original meaning and the original meaning cannot be intended. Finally, it notes that haqiqa and majaz can occur in single words or complete sentences. The discussion is foundational for understanding how language functions in legal interpretation.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqa): A word used in its original, literal meaning as per its original coinage.
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a meaning other than its original due to a relation (علاقة) and a context that prevents the literal meaning.
غلب الاستعمال (ghalabah al-isti'mal): Predominance in usage; the key factor that drives the transformation between haqiqa and majaz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when a haqiqah word's usage becomes very rare (qalil) and its majazi meaning becomes predominant?
A) It remains haqiqah forever.
B) It becomes majaz, and the majazi meaning becomes haqiqah.
C) It becomes kinayah.
D) It becomes sarih.

Key Takeaway
Usage predominance (ghalabah al-isti'mal) can invert the status of haqiqa and majaz, a key consideration in Hanafi textual interpretation.
